If one cultivates in a mixed manner at the upper-supreme level, one receives the rebirth-fruit of the Akaniṣṭha Pure Abode heaven. If one has well cultivated the inconceivable immeasurable bodhisattva samādhi that induces the fourth dhyāna of the tenth bodhisattva stage, one receives the rebirth-fruit of the Great Maheśvara heaven that transcends the Pure Abodes.

**Pristine** refers to the borderline first dhyāna, in dependence upon which all supreme merits and swift supernatural powers are induced. As with the pristine characteristic of the first dhyāna, so too for the remaining dhyānas and formless attainments. The distinction in the case of the formless attainments is that they give rise to liberation and other merits of those stages. The distinctions of the defiled, pure, established, and pristine characteristics of all dhyānas and formless attainments should be understood in this way.

---

Thus have "Supports" been explained. Now "Factors of Awakening" shall be explained. The verse states:

> Factors of awakening are of many kinds;
> Foremost are the thirty-seven.

**The treatise states:** The categories of factors conducive to awakening (bodhipakṣya-dharma) are of many kinds. The foremost and supreme are thirty-seven, namely the four foundations of mindfulness and so forth, as extensively explained in the sūtras.

**The four foundations of mindfulness** (smṛty-upasthāna): First, the foundation of mindfulness of the body: taking the body as one's object, or taking the sūtra teachings superior to the body as one's object, or taking the instructions concerning it as one's object, through wisdom born of hearing, reflection, and cultivation, whether as mere mental image or as accomplished fact, one well establishes mindfulness in the domain of the body, in order to attain liberation from bondage regarding the body. As with the foundation of mindfulness of the body, so too for the foundations of mindfulness of feeling, mind, and dharmas, which should be understood likewise. The distinction among them is that each operates in its own domain as appropriate, up to attaining liberation from bondage regarding dharmas. Furthermore, in all cases one should speak of mind and mental factors associated with mindfulness. When one thus arouses the mind of observation, the objects have four kinds of phenomena: (1) phenomena grasped by mind, (2) phenomena experienced by mind, (3) phenomena discerned by mind, (4) phenomena of mind's defilement and purification.

**The four right exertions** (samyak-prahāṇa), as extensively explained in the sūtras: First, regarding evil, unwholesome dharmas that have already arisen, in order to sever them, one generates desire, makes effort, arouses right exertion, encourages the mind, and sustains the mind. *Already arisen* means subsumed under coarse manifest afflictions. *Evil, unwholesome dharmas* means desire-realm afflictions and secondary afflictions that can produce misconduct. The meaning of "evil" and "unwholesome" has been explained previously. *In order to sever* means cultivating their antidotes to weaken them. *Generating desire* means arousing the wish to realize their severance. *Making effort* means not tolerating evil and turning toward severance. *Arousing right exertion* means cultivating the antidotes in many kinds of firm practice. These three phrases indicate the lower-level antidotes of the wisdom of hearing and reflection in the indeterminate stage. *Encouraging the mind* means: when the mind is sinking and defiled by afflictions during the manifestation of the cultivated antidote, one encourages and uplifts the mind. *Sustaining the mind* means: when the mind is floating and defiled by afflictions during the manifestation of this antidote, one sustains and lowers the mind.

Second, regarding evil, unwholesome dharmas that have not yet arisen, in order to prevent their arising, as explained in detail. *Not yet arisen* means subsumed under intensified latent tendencies that are the causes able to produce coarse manifest afflictions. *In order to prevent their arising* means causing coarse manifest afflictions not to become active. *Generating desire* means arousing the wish to realize non-manifestation. *Making effort* means not forgetting to maintain abiding for the sake of non-manifestation, well maintaining mindfulness. Arousing right exertion, encouraging and sustaining the mind are all as previously explained.

Third, regarding wholesome dharmas that have not yet arisen, in order to cause them to arise, as explained in detail. *Not yet arisen* means not yet obtained. *Wholesome dharmas* means the three kinds of wisdom born of hearing, reflection, and cultivation; they are called "wholesome" because they are free from fault. *In order to cause them to arise* means to cause their attainment. *Generating desire* means arousing the wish for attainment. *Making effort* means seeking the correct methods for receiving and upholding them. *Arousing right exertion* means long-term, earnest, and firmly cultivating practice. These three phrases indicate the wholesome dharmas subsumed under the wisdom of hearing and reflection in the indeterminate stage as antidotes to evil, unwholesome dharmas. *Encouraging and sustaining the mind* means for the sake of attaining the wisdom of cultivation. The rest is as previously explained.

Fourth, regarding wholesome dharmas that have already arisen, causing them to abide, not to be forgotten, to be completed, to be doubled, to increase, and to become vast, as explained in detail. *Already arisen* means already obtained. *Causing them to abide* means the wisdom of hearing. *Not to be forgotten* means the wisdom of reflection. *To be completed* means the wisdom of cultivation. These three phrases indicate the mere guarding of what has already been obtained. *To be doubled, to increase, and to become vast* means, in their respective order, not being satisfied with merely those. *Generating desire* means arousing the wish for attainment. The rest is as previously explained.

**The four bases of spiritual power** (ṛddhi-pāda), as extensively explained in the sūtras: First, the concentration obtained through the predominance of desire. Suppose a practitioner has cultivated superior wholesome roots in previous lives. Toward the great teacher or toward a wise fellow practitioner, they generate faith and desire, and listen to the true Dharma. Having listened to the true Dharma in accordance with their faith and desire, they progressively realize the mind's abiding in a single object. Through this desire, concentration is accomplished.

Regarding evil, unwholesome dharmas that have already arisen and those not yet arisen, in order to sever them and prevent them from arising, one generates desire and so forth up to sustaining the mind. Regarding their antidote wholesome dharmas that have not yet arisen, in order to cause them to arise; regarding those already arisen, in order to cause them to abide, not to be forgotten, to be completed, to be doubled, to increase, and to become vast, one generates desire and so forth up to sustaining the mind.

Such a practitioner further cultivates eight kinds of severance-activities through desire: effort, faith, ease, right mindfulness, introspection, volition, and equanimity. "Through this desire, concentration is accomplished" means one attains mastery therein.

*Evil, unwholesome dharmas that have already arisen and not yet arisen* means those subsumed under lower-level manifest afflictions and those subsumed under weakened but not yet damaged or destroyed latent tendencies. *In order to sever them and prevent them from arising* means for the sake of being free from already-arisen mild manifest afflictions, and for the sake of damaging weakened latent tendencies. Generating desire and so forth up to sustaining the mind are as extensively explained above. Regarding their antidote wholesome dharmas not yet arisen, causing them to arise; regarding those already arisen, causing them to abide, not to be forgotten, to be completed, to be doubled, to increase, and to become vast, generating desire and so forth up to sustaining the mind, as should be known from the previous extensive explanation.

*Such a practitioner* means one who has practiced in this way for a long time. *Further cultivating desire* means desiring to realize non-manifestation and damage. *Effort* means: with desire as the cause, arousing right exertion in śamatha and vipaśyanā. *Faith* means: as the cause of generating desire, having definitive faith in that damage and attainment. *Ease* means: as a result of effort, removing bodily and mental heaviness, making body and mind fit. *Right mindfulness* means: within guarding against the secondary afflictions of sinking and floating, causing the mind not to forget. *Introspection* means: when at times mindfulness is lost and secondary afflictions manifest, having discriminating awareness. *Volition* means creating mental activity within calming and uplifting. *Equanimity* means: in non-defiled abiding, mental equipoise, mental uprightness, and mental non-agitation.

These eight kinds of severance-activities should be known in all bases of spiritual power. The distinction among them is: second, the concentration obtained through the predominance of effort: suppose a practitioner relies on instructional and advisory teachings, and whether in solitude, in a forest, or in a quiet room, practices with long-term courage, thoroughness, ardor, and right exertion, and realizes the mind's abiding in a single object. Through right exertion, concentration is accomplished. The rest is as previously explained.

Third, the concentration obtained through the predominance of mind: suppose a practitioner has previously cultivated śamatha practice. Through this cause, by reflecting on internal dharmas, one swiftly realizes the mind's abiding in a single object. Through cultivating mind, concentration is accomplished. The rest is as previously explained.

Fourth, the concentration obtained through the predominance of investigation: suppose a practitioner is learned and retentive, with their learning well accumulated. Dwelling alone in solitude, regarding those dharmas, they discern with wisdom, supremely discern, thoroughly observe and examine. Through this, one realizes the mind's abiding in a single object. Through investigation, concentration is accomplished. The rest is as previously explained.

**The five faculties** (indriya), as extensively explained in the sūtras: (1) The faith faculty: through the mundane path, the mind becomes purified, bright, unsullied, free from secondary afflictions, attaining immovable abiding. After this, seeking direct realization of the truths, one cultivates the preparatory practices. In order to permanently sever the latent tendencies, and in order to attain their antidotes, one arouses predominant faith. (2) The exertion faculty: depending on the faith faculty, one progresses with valor, operating together with faith. (3) The mindfulness faculty: depending on the exertion faculty, one has clear non-forgetting, operating together with it. (4) The concentration faculty: depending on the mindfulness faculty, the mind abides in a single object, operating together with it. (5) The wisdom faculty: depending on the concentration faculty, one discerns and selects among dharmas, operating together with it.

**The five powers** (bala), as extensively explained in the sūtras: They are identical to the faith faculty and so forth. Through being well cultivated and extensively cultivated, one is no longer confused by the opposing dharmas of faithlessness and so forth, and can furthermore counteract the confusing dharmas. Because they are unconquerable, they are called "powers."

**The seven factors of awakening** (sapta-bodhyaṅga), as extensively explained in the sūtras: (1) The mindfulness factor of awakening: through the mundane path, having obtained well-prepared powers and with the path of seeing imminent, through having previously cultivated the mundane mindfulness factor of awakening, one induces the supramundane, effortless, non-discriminating clarity regarding the truths and non-forgetting of the truths. (2) The dharma-investigation factor of awakening: through the previously induced effortless non-discrimination, depending on mindfulness and operating together with mindfulness, one has understanding of and awakening to the truths. All the remaining factors of awakening likewise, through the previously induced effortless non-discrimination, depend on the preceding factor and operate together with it. This should be understood throughout. The distinctions among them are: (3) the exertion factor of awakening, meaning mental courage regarding the truths; (4) the joy factor of awakening, meaning mental delight regarding the truths; (5) the ease factor of awakening, meaning bodily and mental fitness within the true truths; (6) the concentration factor of awakening, meaning the mind abiding in a single object within the true truths; (7) the equanimity factor of awakening, meaning mental equipoise, mental uprightness, and mental non-agitation within the true truths.

As also stated in the sūtras, "Therein one further well cultivates the support of calm," and so forth in detail. *Support of calm* means the support of calmness in the desire realm. *Support of freedom from desire* means the support of freedom from desire in the form and formless realms. *Support of cessation* means the support of the complete, remainderless permanent severance of the aggregates, bases, and elements already obtained. *Tending toward relinquishment* means the non-continuation of the aggregates, bases, and elements in the future.

**The eight noble path factors** (āryāṣṭāṅga-mārga), as extensively explained in the sūtras: (1) **Right view**: the purity of view when one attains the factors of awakening in the path of seeing, together with the purity of view when one establishes the subsequently-attained factors of awakening in the path of cultivation; combining these two is called right view. (2) **Right thought**: depending on right view and operating together with it: thought of renunciation, thought of non-ill-will, thought of non-harmfulness. In the path of cultivation, continuously attending to and reflecting on the truths, in correspondence with uncontaminated attention, causing the mind to enter and deeply enter, seeking and deeply seeking, presently seeking, comprehending, reckoning, observing, reflecting, and having the nature of reflection. (3) **Right speech**: in the path of cultivation, depending on right thought, through continuous uncontaminated attention and reflection on the truths, one obtains four kinds of verbal karma subsumed under precepts beloved of the noble ones: not delighting in departure from and delighting in departure from, eliminating various forms of separation, purified, guarded, not acting in departure from action, not engaging, not violating, not transgressing, as a bridge, as a raft, distant from and not transgressing, and not transgressing in various ways. (4) **Right action**: in the path of cultivation, through continuous uncontaminated attention and reflection on the truths, one obtains three kinds of bodily karma subsumed under precepts beloved of the noble ones: not delighting in departure from and delighting in departure from, eliminating various forms of separation, as explained in detail above. (5) **Right livelihood**: in the path of cultivation, through reflection on the truths and so forth, one distances oneself from the evil dharmas of wrong livelihood and what it produces, subsumed under precepts beloved of the noble ones, as explained in detail above. (6) **Right effort**: in the path of cultivation, through reflection on the truths and so forth, within the cultivation of the foundations of mindfulness, right exertions, bases of spiritual power, faculties, and powers: desire, right exertion, effort, valor, fitness, unconquerability, the mind's right arousal, and the nature of continuous diligence. (7) **Right mindfulness**: in the path of cultivation, through reflection on the truths and so forth, whether depending on the path of śamatha, the path of vipaśyanā, or the combined path, within the cultivation of the dharma-investigation, exertion, joy, ease, concentration, and equanimity factors of awakening: mindfulness, right mindfulness, recollection, all forms of mindfulness, non-forgetfulness, the mind's clarity, and the nature of not losing, supremely not losing, and supremely not losing dharmas. (8) **Right concentration**: in the path of cultivation, through reflection on the truths and so forth, further depending on the three paths and with right mindfulness collected within one's cultivation: abiding, peacefully abiding, closely abiding, evenly abiding, not being confused, not being scattered, rightly collected, the mind of śamatha abiding in a single object. These path factors should be understood as arising sequentially, each later one depending on and corresponding to the preceding ones.

---

Furthermore, the verse states:

> Knowledge and gates of liberation,
> Modes of practice, and calming and observing.

**The treatise states: Knowledge** refers to ten kinds of knowledge, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **Dharma-knowledge** (dharma-jñāna): uncontaminated knowledge regarding the objects and meanings of what is known, which can be commonly understood and directly perceived. (2) **Consequential knowledge** (anvaya-jñāna): uncontaminated knowledge regarding the objects and meanings of what is known, which cannot be commonly understood and is not directly perceived. (3) **Knowledge of others' minds** (para-citta-jñāna): the knowledge born of cultivation, as a fruit of cultivation, that can know the minds and mental factors of others; and the sublime knowledge of the tathāgatas that knows all sentient beings' inclinations, dispositions, and latent tendencies for the purpose of instruction and guidance. (4) **Conventional knowledge** (saṃvṛti-jñāna): mundane wisdom. Depending on this, the Tathāgata teaches the sublime Dharma to sentient beings in accord with their inclinations and latent tendencies. (5) **Knowledge of suffering** (duḥkha-jñāna): within conditioned factors with outflows, reflecting on impermanence, suffering, emptiness, and non-self; whether knowledge or vision, clarity, awakening, and the nature of the wisdom of discerning observation. (6) **Knowledge of arising** (samudaya-jñāna): within the causes of conditioned factors with outflows, reflecting on cause, arising, production, and condition; whether knowledge or vision; the rest is as before. (7) **Knowledge of cessation** (nirodha-jñāna): within the cessation of conditioned factors with outflows, reflecting on cessation, calm, sublimity, and escape; whether knowledge or vision; the rest is as before. (8) **Knowledge of the path** (mārga-jñāna): within the uncontaminated path that can sever conditioned factors with outflows, reflecting on path, suchness, practice, and deliverance; whether knowledge or vision; the rest is as before. (9) **Knowledge of exhaustion** (kṣaya-jñāna): suffering has been known, arising has been severed, cessation has been realized, the path has been cultivated; whether taking exhaustion as its object or for the purpose of exhaustion; whether knowledge or vision; the rest is as before. (10) **Knowledge of non-arising** (anutpāda-jñāna): suffering has been known and need not be known again, arising has been severed and need not be severed again, cessation has been realized and need not be realized again, the path has been cultivated and need not be cultivated again; whether taking non-arising as its object or for the purpose of non-arising; whether knowledge or vision; the rest is as before.

**Gates of liberation** (vimokṣa-mukha) refers to three gates of liberation: (1) the gate of liberation of emptiness, (2) the gate of liberation of signlessness, (3) the gate of liberation of wishlessness.

**Emptiness** is of two kinds: (1) what is known, (2) knowledge. What is known means: within dharmas that are falsely imagined regarding sentient beings, and within dharmas that are falsely imagined regarding dharmas, the non-existence of both these imagined natures, as well as the existence of non-self in what remains. Within dharmas, the imagined nature is non-existent, which is the nature of the existence of non-self. Within dharmas, the nature of the existence of non-self is the non-existence of the imagined nature. Precisely within this, the nature of the non-duality of existence and non-existence is the non-discriminating object-domain. Knowledge means knowing that object-domain in accord with reality.

**Signlessness** is also of two kinds: (1) what is known, (2) knowledge. What is known means: precisely the empty object-domain of what is known, because in this object-domain all characteristics are not operative. Knowledge is as previously explained.

**Wishlessness** is also of two kinds: (1) what is known, (2) knowledge. What is known means: the characteristics and appearances of conditioned factors that arise through inversion due to the absence of knowledge. Knowledge means knowing that object-domain with aversion and understanding.

The emptiness practice means: within conditioned factors, a self cannot be found; and within characteristics, conventionally discriminated dharmas cannot be found. The signlessness practice means: precisely within conditioned factors, the non-self nature of sentient beings can be found; and within characteristics, the non-self nature of conventionally discriminated dharmas can be found; and within cessation, the practices of cessation, calm, sublimity, and escape. The wishlessness practice means: impermanence, suffering, impurity, like a disease, like a tumor, like an arrow, and the practices of cause, arising, production, and condition.

Taking the wisdom-cognized empty path and practicing path, suchness, practice, and deliverance: this is also an emptiness practice. Taking the wisdom-cognized signless path and practicing path, suchness, practice, and deliverance: this is also a signlessness practice. Taking the wisdom-cognized wishless path and practicing path, suchness, practice, and deliverance: this is also a wishlessness practice.

If without distinction they are collectively called emptiness, signlessness, and wishlessness, this comprehends the wisdom born of hearing, reflection, and cultivation, both mundane and supramundane, as should be known. If they are called the samādhis of emptiness, signlessness, and wishlessness, this is exclusively the wisdom born of cultivation, comprehending both mundane and supramundane, as should be known. If they are called the gates of liberation of emptiness, signlessness, and wishlessness, this is exclusively supramundane, as should be known.

**Modes of practice** (pratipatti) refers to four kinds, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **Painful practice with slow penetration**: for those of dull faculties who have not attained the happiness of abiding in the present life, the path and practice for the exhaustion of all outflows. (2) **Painful practice with swift penetration**: for those of sharp faculties; the rest is as before. (3) **Pleasant practice with slow penetration**: for those of dull faculties who have attained the happiness of abiding in the present life, the path and practice for the exhaustion of all outflows. (4) **Pleasant practice with swift penetration**: for those of sharp faculties; the rest is as before.

**Traces** (pratipada) refers to four dharma-traces, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **The trace of non-attachment**: because it can maintain the morality aggregate, it is called a dharma-meaning trace. For those who have not yet received it, it causes them to receive it; for those who have already received it, it causes them to guard, increase, and expand it. As with non-attachment, (2) the trace of non-hatred is likewise. (3) **The trace of right mindfulness**: because it can maintain the concentration aggregate, it is called a dharma-meaning trace. For what has not yet arisen, it causes it to arise; for what has already arisen, it causes it to increase and expand. (4) **The trace of right concentration**: because it can maintain the wisdom aggregate, the liberation aggregate, and the knowledge-and-vision-of-liberation aggregate, it is called a dharma-meaning trace. For what has not yet arisen or been realized, it causes it to arise and be realized; for what has already arisen and been realized, it causes it to increase and expand.

**Calming** (śamatha) means: when practicing correctly within the dharmas heard and reflected upon, through attention to the object-domain of the mental image of concentration, and through attaining peaceful concentration, one settles the mind internally.

**Observing** (vipaśyanā) means: when practicing correctly within the dharmas heard and reflected upon, through attention to the object-domain of the mental image of concentration, and through attaining peaceful concentration, one discerns and selects among dharmas.

---

Furthermore, the verse states:

> Dwelling places and reliances,
> Generation of the aspiration and compassion,
> Practices, penetration,
> Stages, and the pāramitās.

**The treatise states: Dwelling places** refers to four dwelling places, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **The dwelling place of wisdom**: the wisdom of the world that correctly observes skillful means, for the purpose of establishing the supramundane knowledge that realizes the truths. (2) **The dwelling place of truth**: the supramundane wisdom already obtained through realization of the truths, for the purpose of establishing the severance of inversions based on real phenomena. (3) **The dwelling place of relinquishment**: the severance of inversions based on real phenomena, for the purpose of establishing the complete cessation of all remaining afflictions. (4) **The dwelling place of peace**: the peace of all remaining afflictions, for the purpose of establishing the non-arising of all suffering.

**Reliances** refers to four kinds of reliance, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **Rely on the Dharma, not on persons**: if a dharma has been spoken by the Tathāgata or spoken by disciples, one should learn and follow the twelve divisions of the teaching; one should not follow and learn the conduct practiced by persons. (2) **Rely on meaning, not on words**: if a dharma is not composed of ornamental words and elegant phrases by rhetoricians, but only manifests, independently fulfills, and is pure, bright, and complete in the holy life, one should respectfully understand such dharmas; one should not rely on the elegant words and phrases of rhetoricians that can manifest an inverted holy life or fail to manifest the holy life. (3) **Rely on sūtras of definitive meaning, not on sūtras of provisional meaning**: regarding the teachings of the Tathāgata that resemble the profound, that correspond to emptiness, and that accord with dependent arising, one should not mistakenly grasp the superficial literal meaning, nor should one abide in the view-grasping of one's own internal convictions. One should diligently investigate and seek out sūtras of definitive meaning. (4) **Rely on wisdom, not on consciousness**: one should not merely be satisfied with having heard and generate knowledge without further cultivating practice in accord with the Dharma. Rather, for the sake of exhausting all outflows, one should diligently seek the wisdom of inner realization that directly knows the true truths.

**Generation of the aspiration** refers to the bodhisattvas' generation of the aspiration for awakening (bodhicitta-utpāda). When bodhisattvas who abide in the dharma-nature of bodhisattvas, wishing to benefit all sentient beings in the world-systems of the ten directions, through the force of strong causal conditions corresponding to that practice, make a great vow regarding anuttarā samyaksaṃbodhi and receive the method of generating the aspiration, declaring: "I shall certainly realize anuttarā samyaksaṃbodhi, in order to liberate all sentient beings of the ten directions from all afflictions and from all sufferings and difficulties."

This received generation of the aspiration is of two kinds: (1) conventional generation of the aspiration, (2) generation of the aspiration through realization of the dharma-nature.

Conventional generation of the aspiration means: suppose there is a person who, standing reverently before a wise one with an elevated intention, makes a vow saying: "Venerable, may you bear witness!" or "Noble one, may you bear witness!" or "Upādhyāya! I, named such-and-such, from this day forth generate the aspiration for anuttarā samyaksaṃbodhi, for the sake of benefiting all sentient beings. From now on, all my practices of giving, morality, patience, diligence, meditation, and wisdom are all for the sake of realizing anuttarā samyaksaṃbodhi. I now go forth in harmony with the bodhisattva mahāsattvas. May you bear witness that I am a bodhisattva." A second and third time likewise.

Generation of the aspiration through realization of the dharma-nature means: suppose there is a person who has passed beyond the first incalculable kalpa (asaṃkhyeya), has realized the first bodhisattva stage of Extreme Joy (pramuditā-bhūmi), has entered the bodhisattva's definitive stage of non-arising, has come to know in accord with reality the supreme awakening and the means to awakening, has realized that they themselves are approaching and closely approaching the great fruit of awakening, and through understanding the complete equality of self and others has attained the intention of the Great Self. Having reached the bodhisattva path of not abiding in either saṃsāra or nirvāṇa, they have attained the vast intention. Through all this, regarding the great awakening, their vow is irreversible. This is called generation of the aspiration through realization of the dharma-nature.

**Compassion** refers to: having thus generated the aspiration, the bodhisattva, regarding all sentient beings in the world-systems of the ten directions, whether beings suffering three kinds of regression, or beings with the determinate sufferings of the five destinies, or beings with four kinds of extreme suffering, or beings with six kinds of severe suffering upon suffering, or beings with three kinds of characteristic suffering upon suffering, performs the practice of liberating them from suffering, with the nature of non-harmfulness.

**Practices** refers to ten kinds of dharma-practice, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) Regarding the dharmas of the bodhisattva piṭaka, whether much or little, with respect and reverence, the dharma-practice of copying. (2) With offering materials, whether inferior or superior, the dharma-practice of making offerings. (3) Having copied them oneself, out of compassion, the dharma-practice of giving them to others. (4) When another person generates the intention and with respect and reverence, using a wonderful voice to proclaim and read aloud, the dharma-practice of listening attentively out of veneration. (5) Generating pure faith and understanding with a respectful and serious mind, the dharma-practice of reading and perusing. (6) In order to cultivate practice in accord with the Dharma, having received it from a teacher, the dharma-practice of reciting. (7) Having recited it, in order to firmly maintain it, the dharma-practice of rehearsing with a broad and wonderful voice. (8) Out of compassion for others, transmitting it to them and, as appropriate in detail or in brief, the dharma-practice of explaining and expounding. (9) Dwelling alone in solitude, thoroughly and carefully investigating and in accord with principle observing and examining, the dharma-practice of reflection. (10) As one has reflected, cultivating śamatha and vipaśyanā, in order to enter into and in order to cause all the sought meanings to be accomplished, the dharma-practice of practice.

---

## Fascicle 3

### Compendium of Topics, Part Three

**Penetrations** (prativedha) refers to seven kinds of penetration, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **Penetration of syllables** (vyañjana): because one is without conceptual discrimination regarding the thirty-two syllables, one realizes in accord with reality the meaning of the characteristics that are the object of practice, having previously well cultivated the practice of the superior dharma within the mind. (2) **Penetration of syllable-characteristics**: because one discriminates the characteristics of syllables such as the form of a lion, one realizes in accord with reality the meaning of the characteristics that are the object of practice. The rest is as stated above. (3) **Penetration of the grasper** (grāhaka): one realizes in accord with reality that the mental dharmas corresponding to objects apprehended have only the characteristic of mere cognition. The rest is as stated above. (4) **Penetration of the grasped** (grāhya): one realizes in accord with reality that the objects of all consciousnesses are merely the reflected images of consciousness-only. The rest is as stated above. (5) **Penetration of bondage** (bandhana): one realizes in accord with reality the bondage of characteristics and the bondage of heaviness. The rest is as stated above. (6) **Penetration of liberation** (mokṣa): one realizes in accord with reality the liberation from the bondage of characteristics and the liberation from the bondage of heaviness. The rest is as stated above. (7) **Penetration of dharma-nature** (dharmatā): regarding bondage and liberation, one realizes in accord with reality the dharma-nature of suchness, which is the dependent arising of all formations since beginningless time and the cessation thereof, having previously well cultivated the practice of the superior dharma within the mind.

**Grounds** (bhūmi) refers to the ten bodhisattva grounds, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **The Ground of Extreme Joy** (pramuditā-bhūmi): bodhisattvas dwelling on this ground, having previously well cultivated the practice of the superior dharma within the mind, surpass the direct realization of all śrāvakas and pratyekabuddhas and attain the direct realization of bodhisattvas. Because they correctly realize the supreme direct realization, the great bodhisattvas on this ground abide in supreme joy. Therefore this ground is called Extreme Joy. (2) **The Ground of Freedom from Defilement** (vimalā-bhūmi): bodhisattvas dwelling on this ground, having previously well cultivated the practices of the first ground, surpass all śrāvaka and pratyekabuddha grounds and realize the extremely pure and subtle aggregate of morality (śīla-skandha), counteracting all subtle violations of the precepts. Therefore this ground is called Freedom from Defilement. (3) **The Ground of Radiance** (prabhākarī-bhūmi): bodhisattvas dwelling on this ground, having previously well cultivated the second ground, surpass all śrāvaka and pratyekabuddha grounds and realize the extremely pure aggregate of concentration (samādhi-skandha), which serves as the support for the great light of wisdom. Therefore this ground is called Radiance. (4) **The Ground of Blazing Wisdom** (arciṣmatī-bhūmi): bodhisattvas dwelling on this ground, having previously well cultivated the third ground, surpass all śrāvaka and pratyekabuddha grounds and realize the extremely pure, subtle aggregate of wisdom (prajñā-skandha) that takes as its object the factors of awakening and is capable of grasping the domain of dharmas, able to burn all afflictions in manifest presence. Therefore this ground is called Blazing Wisdom. (5) **The Ground Extremely Difficult to Conquer** (sudurjayā-bhūmi): bodhisattvas dwelling on this ground, having previously well cultivated the fourth ground, surpass all śrāvaka and pratyekabuddha grounds and realize the extremely pure, subtle aggregate of wisdom that takes as its object the truths and all knowable dharmas, accomplishing the extremely difficult holy path of not abiding in either cyclic existence or cessation. Therefore this ground is called Extremely Difficult to Conquer. (6) **The Ground of Manifest Presence** (abhimukhī-bhūmi): bodhisattvas dwelling on this ground, having previously well cultivated the fifth ground, surpass all śrāvaka and pratyekabuddha grounds and realize the extremely pure, subtle aggregate of wisdom that takes as its object the dharma-domain of the cessation of the activities produced by both cognition and non-cognition. For the most part, characterized wisdom spontaneously continues in manifest presence. Therefore this ground is called Manifest Presence. (7) **The Ground of Far-Going** (dūraṅgamā-bhūmi): bodhisattvas dwelling on this ground, having previously well cultivated the sixth ground, surpass all śrāvaka and pratyekabuddha grounds and realize the extremely pure and subtle aggregates of liberation and of liberation-knowledge-and-vision (vimukti-jñāna-darśana-skandha). Because they have gone far into the liberation from all manifest characteristics of activity. Therefore this ground is called Far-Going. (8) **The Ground of Immovability** (acalā-bhūmi): bodhisattvas dwelling on this ground, having previously well cultivated the seventh ground, surpass all śrāvaka and pratyekabuddha grounds and realize the extremely pure and subtle aggregates of liberation and of liberation-knowledge-and-vision. Because they are liberated from all obstacles to mastery over characteristics, they attain the support of the effortless, spontaneously continuing path. Therefore this ground is called Immovability. (9) **The Ground of Excellent Wisdom** (sādhumatī-bhūmi): bodhisattvas dwelling on this ground, having previously well cultivated the eighth ground, surpass all śrāvaka and pratyekabuddha grounds and realize the extremely pure and subtle aggregates of liberation and of liberation-knowledge-and-vision. Being liberated from all obstacles to unimpeded eloquence, they serve as the support of faultless, vast wisdom. Therefore this ground is called Excellent Wisdom. (10) **The Ground of the Dharma Cloud** (dharmameghā-bhūmi): bodhisattvas dwelling on this ground, having previously well cultivated the ninth ground, surpass all śrāvaka and pratyekabuddha grounds and realize the extremely pure and subtle aggregates of liberation and of liberation-knowledge-and-vision. Being liberated from obstacles to the generation of great supernatural powers and wisdom, they serve as the support of the complete fulfillment of the cloud-like dharma-body. Therefore this ground is called Dharma Cloud.

**Pāramitās** refers to the ten pāramitās, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **The pāramitā of giving** (dāna-pāramitā): based on the mind of awakening (bodhicitta), with compassion as the guide, well cultivated through the ten kinds of dharma-practice, with the seven kinds of penetration as one's firm foundation, whether through material goods, or through the correct Dharma, or through fearlessness, possessing the nature of five kinds of meritorious qualities of the Great Self: first, giving without attachment; second, giving without regard; third, giving without error; fourth, giving without conceptual discrimination; fifth, giving with dedication. Through this practice, bodhisattvas gather sentient beings by means of the necessities of life, and through repeated practice and abundant cultivation, they bring the provisions to fulfillment and shall accomplish the fruit of anuttarā samyaksaṃbodhi. As with the pāramitā of giving, so too with morality through wisdom: one should understand accordingly. The differences among them are as follows.

The second, the **pāramitā of morality** (śīla-pāramitā): whether through the cessation and avoidance of the unwholesome, or through the gathering of wholesome dharmas, or through benefiting sentient beings, it has the nature of bodily, verbal, and mental actions included within the restraining precepts. Through this practice, bodhisattvas gather sentient beings through non-anger and non-harassment.

The third, the **pāramitā of patience** (kṣānti-pāramitā): whether through enduring the harm done by others with the nature of non-anger, or through bearing sufferings with the nature of non-disturbance, or through the nature of correct wisdom that investigates dharmas. Through this practice, bodhisattvas gather sentient beings by enduring all harmful and injurious acts.

The fourth, the **pāramitā of diligence** (vīrya-pāramitā): whether through donning the armor of the mind, or through skillful means and effort, or through benefiting sentient beings, it has the nature of the mind's courageous vigor in a continuously ripened stream, for the purpose of producing all wholesome roots. Through this practice, bodhisattvas, even though they have not yet subjugated afflictions, are able to single-mindedly cultivate wholesome practices throughout.

The fifth, the **pāramitā of meditation** (dhyāna-pāramitā): whether through counteracting afflictions, or through generating meritorious qualities, or through benefiting sentient beings, it has the nature of the mind abiding in a single object. Through this practice, bodhisattvas subjugate afflictions and cause them to abide in the state of non-manifestation.

The sixth, the **pāramitā of wisdom** (prajñā-pāramitā): whether through counteracting afflictions, or through generating meritorious qualities, or through benefiting sentient beings, it has the nature of discriminating dharmas. Through this practice, bodhisattvas permanently sever the seeds of all afflictive obstructions and cognitive obstructions.

The seventh, the **pāramitā of skillful means** (upāya-kauśalya-pāramitā): through these skillful means, bodhisattvas, whether through compliance, or through opposition, or through differing dispositions, or through performing favors and returning favors, or through coercion, or through purity, gather sentient beings with three kinds of benefit: causing them to accept, causing them to be disciplined, causing them to abide peacefully, and causing them to be established in various wholesome states.

The eighth, the **pāramitā of vows** (praṇidhāna-pāramitā): among bodhisattvas, one who possesses any one of these as their nature, because laziness and afflictions are numerous, thereupon makes correct vows and cultivates wholesome practices: "May I in future lives obtain the nature of courageous, correct diligence, with afflictions attenuated." Through this cause, in subsequent lives, whatever was aspired for comes to fruition, and one obtains powerful strength in cultivating wholesome dharmas.

The ninth, the **pāramitā of power** (bala-pāramitā): because bodhisattvas have obtained powerful strength in the wholesome dharmas they cultivate, relying on this power, they swiftly generate the pāramitā of meditation.

The tenth, the **pāramitā of knowledge** (jñāna-pāramitā): it is the worldly wisdom induced by the knowledge of dharma-principles included within the pāramitā of meditation in the bodhisattva piṭaka. Relying on this wisdom, bodhisattvas are able to swiftly generate the pāramitā of wisdom included within the supramundane, non-discriminating path that does not abide in either cyclic existence or cessation.

These ten pāramitās are all cultivated together on every ground. As for what is predominant, the pāramitā of giving is solely on the first ground, and in due order through to the pāramitā of knowledge on the tenth ground: one should understand accordingly.

Furthermore, the verse states:

> Bodhisattva practices and means of attraction,
> Together with their dhāraṇī,
> Samādhi and other gates,
> And the immeasurable mental attentions.

**The treatise states:** **Bodhisattva practices** refers to four kinds of bodhisattva practice, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **The practice of reaching the other shore** (pāramitā-caryā): the ten pāramitās are collectively called the practice of reaching the other shore, because they all have the meaning of great-vehicle liberation. (2) **The practice of the factors of complete awakening** (bodhipakṣya-caryā): the thirty-seven factors of awakening are collectively called the practice of the factors of complete awakening, because they have the meaning of realizing in accord with reality all that is to be known. (3) **The practice of supernatural powers** (abhijñā-caryā): the six supernatural powers are collectively called the practice of supernatural powers, because they all serve to attract beings to be converted, causing them to generate reverence and enter the sacred teaching. (4) **The practice of maturing sentient beings**: the four means of attraction are collectively called the practice of maturing sentient beings. For beings who have already entered the sacred teaching, this has the meaning of gathering them through two kinds of giving, material and Dharma. Herein, material gathering means causing maturation through the skillful means of assisting and receiving. Dharma gathering means causing maturation through the skillful means of correct reception, with both direct teaching and subsidiary teaching.

These four means of attraction are explained as means of attraction based on five kinds of gathering practice. The five gathering practices are: (1) gathering by causing attachment to oneself, (2) gathering by causing acceptance of the wholesome, (3) gathering by causing the arising of diligent effort toward the wholesome, (4) gathering by causing the wholesome to mature, (5) gathering by causing wholesome liberation. **Means of attraction** refers to the four means of attraction, as extensively explained in the sūtras. If one establishes them, one should understand as in the establishment of the practice of maturing sentient beings.

**Dhāraṇī gates**: the immeasurable dhāraṇī gates of bodhisattvas, as extensively explained in the sūtras. If one wishes to explain the characteristics of dhāraṇī briefly: bodhisattvas who have accomplished mastery over the classes of syllables, who have penetrated names, phrases, and syllable-bodies with sovereign freedom, obtain the power of this kind of retentive memory (dhāraṇī). Through the power of memory, from any single syllable they are able to display, discriminate, and expound the meanings of all kinds of defilement and purity. Therefore they are called dhāraṇī gates.

**Samādhi gates**: the immeasurable samādhi gates of bodhisattvas, as extensively explained in the sūtras. If one wishes to explain them briefly, there are furthermore eight kinds, namely: from the first dhyāna through to the station of neither-perception-nor-non-perception. The bodhisattva mahāsattvas, relying on each and every one of these samādhi gates, produce immeasurable samādhis whose names are not reached even by śrāvakas and pratyekabuddhas. All these samādhis are able to establish in the world-systems of the ten directions all the activities accomplished by samādhi. Therefore they are called samādhi gates.

**Immeasurable mental attentions** refers to five immeasurable mental attentions, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **Immeasurable mental attention toward sentient beings**: bodhisattvas, with the subtle mental attention well cultivated through the practice of the superior dharma, contemplate the immeasurable sentient world comprised in the immeasurable world-systems of the ten directions, with its inexpressible variety of karmic retribution: those with one foot, two feet, four feet, or many feet; those with form or without form; those with perception, without perception, or with neither perception nor non-perception; those in the desire realm, the form realm, or the formless realm; those in the hells, the animal realm, the realm of hungry ghosts, the human realm, or the heavenly realm; those born from eggs, from moisture, from wombs, or through transformation. Having contemplated thus, they know in accord with reality: "Such beings transmigrate thus, such beings return thus, such are defiled thus, such are purified thus, such engage in wrong conduct thus, such engage in right conduct thus; because of such and such differentiation in conduct, there arise such and such kinds of karmic ripening."

(2) **Immeasurable mental attention toward world-systems**: bodhisattvas contemplate in the same way the physical world-system aspect of the immeasurable world-systems of the ten directions. Having contemplated thus, they know in accord with reality: "This world-system is defiled; this world-system is pure." They know in accord with reality that all are like magical illusions, being merely the reflected images of false conceptual discrimination, empty and unreal. Following their characteristics, they flow onward through formation and destruction, with various forms and shapes established in differentiation, whether superior or inferior, coarse or subtle, distant or near, or analyzed down to the ultimate atoms; regarding expansion and contraction, regarding manifestation and transformation, regarding alteration, regarding distance and proximity, regarding concealment and revelation, and other such matters, they attain mastery and know in accord with reality.

(3) **Immeasurable mental attention toward the dharma-realm**: bodhisattvas contemplate in the same way the specific and shared characteristics of all dharmas in the immeasurable world-systems of the ten directions. Having contemplated thus, they know in accord with reality: "This dharma is form; this dharma is not form. As with form and non-form, so too: visible and invisible, resistant and non-resistant, with outflows and without outflows, conditioned and unconditioned, wholesome, unwholesome, and indeterminate, with faults and without faults, bound to the desire realm, bound to the form realm, and bound to the formless realm, of learning, beyond learning, and neither of learning nor beyond learning, severed by the path of seeing, severed by the path of cultivation, and not to be severed, dharmas of transmigration and dharmas of return, dharmas of defilement and dharmas of purity, dharmas of cyclic existence and dharmas of cessation, dharmas of ordinary beings and dharmas of noble sages, dharmas of śrāvakas, dharmas of pratyekabuddhas, and dharmas of tathāgatas." They know all such dharmas in accord with reality.

(4) **Immeasurable mental attention toward those to be disciplined**: bodhisattvas contemplate in the same way the various dispositions and various natures of beings to be converted in the immeasurable world-systems of the ten directions, whether of the śrāvaka lineage, the pratyekabuddha lineage, or the tathāgata lineage, and other such lineages of those to be disciplined. Having contemplated thus, they know in accord with reality regarding those to be disciplined: "This one has dull faculties; this one has intermediate faculties; this one has keen faculties. This one has inferior conviction; this one has vast conviction. This one is of the conduct of desire with a desire-oriented intention; this one is of the conduct of aversion with an aversion-oriented intention; this one is of the conduct of delusion with a delusion-oriented intention; this one is of equal-part conduct with an equal-part intention. This one has an ascending intention; this one has a non-ascending intention. This one has an intention of thin dust, virtuous and wholesome. This one has intensified latent tendencies; this one has attenuated latent tendencies; this one has extremely subtle latent tendencies. This one has weakened latent tendencies; this one has un-weakened latent tendencies. This one has complete latent tendencies; this one has incomplete latent tendencies. This one understands through extensive explanation; this one understands from hearing a summary. This one is to be disciplined through rejection; this one is to be disciplined through acceptance. This one is to be disciplined gently; this one is to be disciplined firmly; this one is to be disciplined through both firmness and gentleness. This one can only be disciplined through being set aside." They know all such matters in accord with reality.

(5) **Immeasurable mental attention toward means of discipline**: bodhisattvas contemplate in the same way the means of disciplining beings to be converted in the immeasurable world-systems of the ten directions. Having contemplated thus, they know in accord with reality: "This one can be disciplined only through teaching secret dharmas; this one can be disciplined only through teaching explicit dharmas. This one through the skillful means of acceptance; this one through the skillful means of subjugation. This one through the skillful means of direct teaching; this one through the skillful means of subsidiary teaching. This one should be treated with compliance; this one should be treated with opposition. This one through a differing disposition. This one should have favors done and returned; this one should be shown authority and power. This one through purity. This one through the display of extraordinary supernatural transformations; this one through the display of extraordinary prophecies; this one through the display of extraordinary admonitions; this one through the display of various forms of authority; this one through various kinds of skillful guidance and instruction. This one through a firm manner; this one through a gentle manner; this one through both firm and gentle manner. This one through being set aside. This one can be disciplined through a brief statement of the essential dharma; this one can be disciplined only through an extensive exposition of the essential dharma." They know all such skillful means in accord with reality.

Furthermore, the verse states:

> Suchness, mental attention, and characteristics,
> Faith in the inconceivable,
> The vast intention,
> And one should know the specific enumerations.

**The treatise states:** **Characteristics of suchness mental attention** refers to mental attention directed toward seven kinds of all-pervading suchness, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **Mental attention to the suchness of cyclic existence** (pravṛtti-tathatā): bodhisattvas who have already seen the truths, when contemplating defiled and pure dharmas with the mental attention well cultivated through the practice of the superior dharma, contemplate the real nature of the beginningless cyclic flow of all formations. Having contemplated thus, they are free from the view of no cause and from the view of unequal causation. (2) **Mental attention to the suchness of the real characteristic** (lakṣaṇa-tathatā): as stated above, when contemplating the causes of defiled and pure dharmas, they contemplate the selflessness of persons and the selflessness of dharmas. Having contemplated thus, all views of a self, together with all mental attentions involving the conceptual discrimination of characteristics, no longer manifest. (3) **Mental attention to the suchness of consciousness-only** (vijñaptimātra-tathatā): as stated above, when contemplating the basis of defiled and pure dharmas, they contemplate the nature of all dharmas as consciousness-only. Having contemplated thus, they know in accord with reality: "Because the mind alone is defiled, sentient beings are defiled; because the mind alone is purified, sentient beings are purified." (4) **Mental attention to the suchness of establishment** (sthāpanā-tathatā): as stated above, when contemplating the substance of defiled dharmas, they contemplate the truth of suffering. Having contemplated thus, wishing to make it known, they teach sentient beings. (5) **Mental attention to the suchness of wrong conduct** (mithyā-pratipatti-tathatā): as stated above, when contemplating the cause of defiled dharmas, they contemplate the truth of the origin of suffering. Having contemplated thus, wishing to have it abandoned, they teach sentient beings. (6) **Mental attention to the suchness of purity** (viśuddhi-tathatā): as stated above, when contemplating the substance of pure dharmas, they contemplate the truth of cessation. Having contemplated thus, wishing to have it realized, they teach sentient beings. (7) **Mental attention to the suchness of right conduct** (samyak-pratipatti-tathatā): as stated above, when contemplating the path of purity, they contemplate the truth of the path. Having contemplated thus, wishing to have it cultivated, they teach sentient beings.

**Faith in the inconceivable** (acintya-adhimukti): bodhisattvas have attained faith regarding what is difficult to conceive, as extensively explained in the sūtras. If one wishes to explain the characteristics of this faith briefly: regarding the meaning of the most extremely profound objects of knowledge, the domains of practice of bodhisattvas who have entered the grounds and of tathāgatas, and the most extremely vast majestic power of buddhas and bodhisattvas, one generates sincere and straightforward faith. This is an inconceivable domain, not a domain of speculative assessment. Having fully understood it, one generates faith.

**The vast intention** (āśaya): it refers to the intention of the Great Self and the vast, universal intention. The **intention of the Great Self** means that bodhisattvas, having attained the understanding of equality between self and others, possess the pure faith and desire for all sentient beings to attain liberation. The **vast, universal intention** means that bodhisattvas, having attained the understanding of non-discrimination and equality regarding cyclic existence and cessation, possess the pure faith and desire to benefit sentient beings while not abiding in either.

**One should know the specific enumerations**: one should know the specific enumerations of the various factors of awakening. Having thus explained the factors of awakening, we shall now explain persons (pudgala). The verse states:

> Followers of faith and the other seven,
> Again eight kinds should be known,
> Together with the seven-returns-at-most and others,
> And six kinds beginning with the regressing type.

**The treatise states:** Persons (pudgala) should be understood as being of many kinds. First, I shall explain the seven kinds of noble ones, namely followers of faith and the other seven, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **Follower of faith** (śraddhānusārin): suppose there is one who by nature has dull faculties, whose continuum is ripened, who from former times has always practiced through the conduct of faith. Through this causal condition, they now practice toward the contemplation of the truths through the conduct of following faith. (2) **Follower of dharma** (dharmānusārin): suppose there is one who by nature has keen faculties, whose continuum is ripened, who from former times has always practiced through the conduct of investigating dharmas. Through this causal condition, they now practice toward the contemplation of the truths through the conduct of following the investigation of dharmas. (3) **One liberated through faith** (śraddhādhimukta): this is the same as the follower of faith who has already seen the noble truths. (4) **One who has attained through views** (dṛṣṭiprāpta): this is the same as the follower of dharma who has already seen the noble truths. (5) **Body-witness** (kāyasākṣin): one who has attained the eight liberations through bodily witness and dwells in their complete fulfillment, but has not yet attained the exhaustion of all outflows without remainder. (6) **One liberated through wisdom** (prajñāvimukta): one who has already attained the exhaustion of all outflows without remainder, but has not yet attained the eight liberations through bodily witness and their complete fulfillment. (7) **One liberated both ways** (ubhayatobhāgavimukta): one who has already attained the exhaustion of all outflows without remainder, and has also attained the eight liberations through bodily witness and their complete fulfillment.

**Eight kinds** refers to the fruit of stream-entry, its approach, and so forth, making eight, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **The approach to stream-entry** (srota-āpatti-mārga): suppose there is one whose continuum is ripened, who has surpassed all the grounds of external ordinary beings and entered the state of correctness and definite separation from birth. If they have not yet realized the initial fruit of stream-entry, they shall certainly not die prematurely. (2) **The fruit of stream-entry** (srota-āpatti-phala): if classified by what predominates, it is the permanent severance of the three fetters; if classified comprehensively, it is the permanent severance of all afflictions severed by the path of seeing. Because this noble one has already seen the truths, this is the first attainment of the fruit of going against the stream. (3) **The approach to once-returning** (sakṛdāgāmi-mārga): suppose there is one who, through the worldly path, has already eliminated desire for the desire realm twofold and then enters the state of correctness and definite separation from birth; or one who, from the fruit of stream-entry, cultivates the counteracting practice for the purpose of severing the upper and middle grades of afflictions of the desire realm. (4) **The fruit of once-returning** (sakṛdāgāmi-phala): either one who has already eliminated desire twofold enters the state of correctness and definite separation from birth and afterward realizes it, or a stream-enterer who progressively severs the upper and middle grades of afflictions of the desire realm attains it. Based on this severance, one is said to have attenuated desire, aversion, and delusion. How is this known? When, through deliberate mental attention, one observes objects and the mind gives rise to equanimity, with no mind tending toward habitual patterns, no mind proceeding toward habitual patterns, no mind attached to habitual patterns, one should know that this person's three poisons are attenuated. (5) **The approach to non-returning** (anāgāmi-mārga): suppose there is one who, through the worldly path, has previously eliminated all desire for the desire realm and then enters the state of correctness and definite separation from birth; or one who, from the fruit of once-returning, cultivates the counteracting practice for the purpose of severing the remaining afflictions of the desire realm. (6) **The fruit of non-returning** (anāgāmi-phala): either one who has previously eliminated desire enters the state of correctness and definite separation from birth and afterward realizes it, or a once-returner who completely severs the remaining afflictions of the desire realm attains it. (7) **The approach to arhatship** (arhat-mārga): suppose there is a learner who has already seen the path and, for the purpose of severing the afflictions of the station of neither-perception-nor-non-perception, cultivates the counteracting practice. (8) **The fruit of arhatship** (arhat-phala): it is attained through the permanent severance of all afflictions of the station of neither-perception-nor-non-perception.

**Seven-returns-at-most and others** refers to eight kinds based on the differentiation of births, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **Seven-returns-at-most** (saptakṛtvas-paramaḥ): this is the fruit of stream-entry itself. Through well cultivating the noble path, whether in the heavens, among humans, or alternating between heavens and humans, after taking at most seven births one reaches the end of suffering. (2) **Family-to-family** (kolaṅkola): this is the fruit of stream-entry itself. Through well cultivating the noble path, whether born in the heavens or born among humans, going from family to family, one reaches the end of suffering. (3) **One-interval** (ekavīcika): this is the fruit of once-returning itself. Through well cultivating the noble path, whether born in the heavens, one attains cessation right in that place; or born among humans, one attains cessation right in this place. (4) **One who attains cessation in the intermediate state** (antarā-parinirvāyin): this is the fruit of non-returning itself. Having severed the fundamental birth-fetter but not yet severed the fetter tending toward birth, through the power of superior cultivation of the noble path, one attains cessation in the intermediate state (antarābhava); or there is one who, without proceeding toward a place of birth, attains cessation; or there is one who, proceeding toward a place of birth but not yet reaching one's destined birth, attains cessation. (5) **One who attains cessation upon birth** (upapadya-parinirvāyin): this is the fruit of non-returning itself. Through intermediate cultivation of the noble path, having not yet severed the two kinds of birth-fetter, upon being born in any one of the mind-born heavens, at the very first moment of birth one attains cessation. (6) **One who attains cessation without exertion** (anabhisaṃskāra-parinirvāyin): this is the same noble one who, after practicing a small amount of practice and with little diligence, attains cessation. The rest is as stated above. (7) **One who attains cessation with exertion** (sābhisaṃskāra-parinirvāyin): this is the fruit of non-returning itself. Through inferior cultivation of the noble path, having not yet severed the two fetters, upon being born in any one of the mind-born heavens, after practicing much practice and with much diligence, one attains cessation. (8) **One who goes upstream** (ūrdhvasrotas): this is the fruit of non-returning itself. Upon being born in any one of the mind-born heavens, being unable to attain the exhaustion of outflows in that place, one further progresses to a higher birth and in a subsequent existence attains cessation.

**Six kinds beginning with the regressing type** refers to six kinds of the fruit of the non-learner, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **The regressing type** (parihāṇa-dharman): one who possesses such dull faculties that whether one contemplates self-harm or does not, whether one is heedless or not, one can equally lose the pleasant abiding in the present dharma and worldly meritorious qualities, and one is unable to refine one's faculties or generate qualities of a superior grade. (2) **The deliberating type** (cetanā-dharman): one who possesses such dull faculties that if one contemplates self-harm, one is able not to regress, but when not contemplating harm, one can regress. This person thinks: "It is better that I be victorious over the māras than that the māras be victorious over me." Having thought thus, one contemplates self-harm. This person too is unable to refine one's faculties or generate qualities of a superior grade. (3) **The guarding type** (anuraksaṇā-dharman): one who possesses such dull faculties that, though not contemplating self-harm, through non-heedlessness one is able not to regress; but if the mind is heedless, one can regress. This person is unable to refine one's faculties or generate qualities of a superior grade. (4) **The immovably abiding type** (sthitākampya): one who possesses such dull faculties that, though not contemplating self-harm and though being heedless, one still does not regress. Yet one is unable to refine one's faculties or generate qualities of a superior grade. (5) **The type capable of progress** (prativedhanīya): one who possesses such dull faculties that one is capable of not regressing, and is able to refine one's faculties and generate qualities of a superior grade. (6) **The unshakeable type** (akopya-dharman): one who from the beginning by nature possesses keen faculties. This person, regarding all wholesome roots, is not shaken by the dharma of regressing from what has been attained, nor is one shaken by the need to generate superior meritorious qualities or refine one's faculties further. Therefore one is called the unshakeable type.

Furthermore, the verse states:

> Seven kinds beginning with dull faculties,
> Householder and renunciant,
> Three beginning with the śrāvaka vehicle,
> Saveable and unsaveable.

**Seven kinds beginning with dull faculties**: (1) **Dull faculties**: one who possesses the five faculties of faith and so forth, either dull by nature or not yet developed, and whose effort is slow when seeking progress to a superior stage. (2) **Keen faculties**: one should understand this as the opposite. (3) **Conduct of desire** (rāga-carita): because one has long habituated desire in previous lives and has not cultivated the antidote to desire, therefore in this life, even when encountering inferior desirable objects, one gives rise to fierce, continuous desire that is difficult to abandon, difficult to be satiated with, and one's effort in cultivating wholesome dharmas is slow. As with the conduct of desire, so too the fourth, **the conduct of aversion** (dveṣa-carita), and the fifth, **the conduct of delusion** (moha-carita). The difference is: even encountering a trivially offensive object, one gives rise to fierce, continuous aversion; even encountering a crude, shallow object of delusion, one gives rise to fierce, continuous delusion. (6) **Equal-part conduct** (samacarita): because one has not habituated the superior grades of desire, aversion, and delusion in previous lives, or if one has, one has also cultivated their antidotes, therefore in this life, when encountering the three kinds of desirable and other objects, one gives rise to the three afflictions of desire, aversion, and delusion corresponding to the grade of the object, neither difficult nor easy to abandon, neither difficult nor easy to be satiated with, and one's effort in cultivating wholesome dharmas is neither slow nor fast. (7) **Conduct of thin dust** (mandadhūli-carita): suppose there is one who in previous lives has not long and repeatedly habituated desire, aversion, and delusion, yet has cultivated their antidotes. Therefore in this life, even when encountering superior desirable and other objects, one is unable to give rise to fierce, continuous afflictions of desire, aversion, and delusion. Though they may arise at times in inferior or intermediate grades, they are easy to abandon and easy to be satiated with, and one's effort in cultivating wholesome dharmas is swift.

**Householder**: one who dwells in the household, a white-robed layperson, enjoying the five desires, engaged in worldly occupations to support one's livelihood.

**Renunciant**: one who maintains the deportment and appearance of one who has left the household, who has abandoned worldly objects, who upholds the precepts, and who supports oneself through properly seeking alms in accord with the Dharma.

**Three kinds beginning with the śrāvaka vehicle**: (1) **The śrāvaka vehicle**: one who abides in the dharma-nature of a śrāvaka and, for the sake of bringing about one's own realization of cessation, having made the correct vow, cultivates the practice of skillful means. (2) **The pratyekabuddha vehicle**: one who abides in the dharma-nature of a pratyekabuddha and, for the sake of bringing about one's own realization of cessation, without relying on a teacher's instruction, having made the correct vow, cultivates the practice of skillful means. (3) **The great vehicle** (mahāyāna): one who abides in the dharma-nature of the great vehicle and, for the sake of bringing about one's own and others' realization of cessation, without relying on a teacher's instruction, having made the correct vow, cultivates the practice of skillful means.

**Saveable**: those who possess the dharma-nature of cessation of one of the three vehicles.

**Unsaveable**: those who lack the dharma-nature of cessation of any of the three vehicles.

Furthermore, the verse states:

> Nine kinds beginning with those who have entered skillful means,
> Two based on the differentiation of birth,
> Again, through the differentiation of realms,
> One should know thirteen kinds.

**The treatise states:** **Nine kinds beginning with those who have entered skillful means**: (1) **One who has entered skillful means**: one who has attained firm, pure faith in the Dharma and Vinaya self-awakened and self-taught by the Tathāgata, who has received the precepts, who has heard the correct Dharma, who has developed generosity, and who possesses right view. (2) **One who has not entered skillful means**: one should understand this as the opposite. (3) **One with obstructions**: one who has three kinds of obstructions: the obstruction of afflictions, the obstruction of karma, and the obstruction of retribution. These obstruct the cultivation of wholesome dharmas. (4) **One without obstructions**: one should understand this as the opposite. (5) **One not yet matured**: one who has not yet attained the wholesome roots that provision the mind-stream and is unable in the present life to realize the vision of the truths through direct experience, and does not attain the cessation realized by the inferior, intermediate, or superior vehicles in the present life. (6) **One already matured**: one should understand this as the opposite. (7) **One completely fettered** (pṛthagjana): an ordinary being. (8) **One not completely fettered**: the six kinds of noble learners, from the fruit of stream-entry through to the sixth, the approach to arhatship. (9) **One without fetters**: the non-learner, the fruit of arhatship.

**Two based on the differentiation of birth**: (1) **The human realm**: those born in the human realm who have obtained human form. (2) **The non-human realm**: those born in the other realms, with differentiation among the classes of birth in the hells, the animal realm, the realm of hungry ghosts, the heavenly realm, the nāgas, the yakṣas, the asuras, the garuḍas, the kiṃnaras, the mahoragas, and so forth.

**Again, through the differentiation of realms, one should know thirteen kinds**: (1) **Ordinary beings of the desire realm**: those born in the desire realm who have not yet seen the truths. (2) **Learners of the desire realm**: the six kinds of learners born in the desire realm who have already seen the noble truths, from the fruit of stream-entry through to the sixth, the approach to arhatship. (3) **Non-learners of the desire realm**: those born in the desire realm who have attained the fruit of arhatship. (4) **Ordinary beings of the form realm**: those born in the form realm who have not yet seen the truths. (5) **Learners of the form realm**: the two kinds of learners born in the form realm who have already seen the noble truths: the fruit of non-returning and the approach to arhatship. (6) **Non-learners of the form realm**: those born in the form realm who have attained the fruit of arhatship. (7) **Ordinary beings of the formless realm**: those born in the formless realm who have not yet seen the truths. (8) **Learners of the formless realm**: the two kinds of learners born in the formless realm who have already seen the noble truths: the fruit of non-returning and the approach to arhatship. (9) **Non-learners of the formless realm**: those born in the formless realm who have attained the fruit of arhatship. (10) **Pratyekabuddhas of the desire realm**: those who abide in the dharma-nature of a pratyekabuddha and who, in previous lives, whether they had not yet seen the truths or had already seen them, are now born in the desire realm and, without relying on a teacher's instruction, through the power of previous causes, cultivate the dharmas of the factors of awakening and realize the permanent exhaustion of all fetters. These are of two kinds: first, those who wander alone like a rhinoceros (khaḍgaviṣāṇa-kalpa); second, those who dwell in assemblies of the victorious solitary type. (11) **Bodhisattvas of the desire realm**: those born in the desire realm who abide in the dharma-nature of a bodhisattva and, for the sake of bringing about one's own and others' realization of cessation, having made the correct vow, cultivate all the practices of skillful means toward unsurpassed awakening. (12) **Bodhisattvas of the form realm**: those born in the form realm who abide in the dharma-nature of a bodhisattva, who avoid the formless realm and cultivate the meditative absorptions, and, for the sake of bringing about one's own and others' realization of cessation, having made the correct vow, cultivate all the practices of skillful means toward unsurpassed awakening. (13) **The inconceivable buddha-tathāgatas**: those who, relying on the cultivation of the non-discriminating path that does not abide in either cyclic existence or cessation, realize the transformation of the basis (āśraya-parāvṛtti) that is shared by all buddhas, included within the dharma-body of liberation, the unsurpassed universal activity pervading the world-systems of the ten directions, accomplishing all beneficial activities for all sentient beings without end.

Having thus explained persons, we shall now explain fruits. The verse states:

> Five kinds of severance among fruits,
> Comprehensive knowledges and purities,
> Confirmed faiths, fruits, realms, and awakenings,
> Non-learner dharmas by their specific enumerations.

**The treatise states:** **Five kinds of severance among fruits**: among the fruits, there are five kinds of severance. (1) **Severance of active afflictions** (paryutthāna-prahāṇa): through four kinds of counteraction, one separates from the manifest activity of afflictions. The four kinds of counteraction are: counteraction of distraction, counteraction of disclosure, counteraction of weakening, and counteraction of suppression. Counteraction of distraction means cultivating the practice of the first eight excellent dharma-practices, or cultivating other wholesome dharmas of the meditative stations. Counteraction of disclosure means cultivating the practice of the ninth dharma-practice. Counteraction of weakening means that through the provisioning of the mind by prior wholesome roots, the afflictions become weak. Counteraction of suppression means that through the worldly path, one suppresses the seeds of afflictions according to one's power. (2) **Severance of latent tendencies** (anuśaya-prahāṇa): through the supramundane path, one permanently severs the seeds of afflictions according to one's power. (3) **Severance through the permanent exhaustion of desire**: because latent afflictions are permanently severed, desire-afflictions are severed. As with the severance through the permanent exhaustion of desire, so too the fourth, **severance through the permanent exhaustion of aversion**, and the fifth, **severance through the permanent exhaustion of delusion**: one should understand that through the power of the extremely pure, skillful penetrative vision, the afflictions regarding all objects are permanently severed. Therefore they are called permanent exhaustion.

**Comprehensive knowledges** (parijñā) refers to nine comprehensive knowledges, included among the fruits, explained in terms of the comprehensive knowledge of severance, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **The comprehensive knowledge of the severance of afflictions severed by seeing the truth of suffering and the truth of the origin that are bound to the desire realm**: because these two truths are included in the defiled. (2) **The comprehensive knowledge of the severance of afflictions severed by seeing the truth of suffering and the truth of the origin that are bound to the form and formless realms**: because these two realms are included in the meditative stations.
