(3) **The comprehensive knowledge of the severance of afflictions severed by seeing the truth of cessation that are bound to the desire realm**: because this is included in the undefiled and unconditioned. (4) **The comprehensive knowledge of the severance of afflictions severed by seeing the truth of cessation that are bound to the form and formless realms**: because this is included through the predominance of the meditative stations. (5) **The comprehensive knowledge of the severance of afflictions severed by seeing the truth of the path that are bound to the desire realm**: because this is included in the undefiled and conditioned. (6) **The comprehensive knowledge of the severance of afflictions severed by seeing the truth of the path that are bound to the form and formless realms**: because this is included through the predominance of the meditative stations. (7) **The comprehensive knowledge of the severance of the five lower fetters** (pañcāvarabhāgīya-saṃyojana): because one has surpassed the lower realm. (8) **The comprehensive knowledge of the exhaustion of desire for the form realm**: because one has surpassed the intermediate realm. (9) **The comprehensive knowledge of the exhaustion of desire for the formless realm**: because one has surpassed the subtle realm.

**Purities** (viśuddhi) refers to nine kinds of purity, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **Purity of morality** (śīla-viśuddhi): suppose there is one who dwells well in morality and guards well the precepts of the prātimokṣa, who is complete in proper deportment and proper range of activity, who sees great danger in the slightest faults, and who takes up and trains in the training rules. (2) **Purity of mind** (citta-viśuddhi): suppose there is one who, relying on the purity of morality, separates from desires and unwholesome dharmas and, as previously explained, dwells in the complete fulfillment of the first, second, third, and fourth dhyānas. (3) **Purity of view** (dṛṣṭi-viśuddhi): suppose there is one who, possessing the purity of mind, which is fresh and white, without stain, free from afflictions, having attained the immovable abiding, for the purpose of realizing the knowledge of the exhaustion of outflows, contemplates the truths, and knows in accord with reality: "This is the noble truth of suffering; this is the noble truth of the origin of suffering; this is the noble truth of the cessation of suffering; this is the noble truth of the path leading to the cessation of suffering." (4) **Purity of having crossed beyond doubt** (kaṅkṣā-vitaraṇa-viśuddhi): suppose there is one who, relying on the purity of view, is without confusion and without doubt regarding the Buddha, the Dharma, and the Saṅgha. (5) **Purity of the knowledge and vision of what is and is not the path** (mārgāmārga-jñāna-darśana-viśuddhi): suppose there is one who, relying on the purity of having crossed beyond doubt, attains excellent knowledge and vision that only the path taught by the Buddha and practiced by the Saṅgha is able to achieve liberation. What is this? It is the exhaustion of suffering and the realization of the end of suffering. As for the paths taught by the various non-Buddhist teachers, they are unable to exhaust suffering or realize the end of suffering. (6) **Purity of the knowledge and vision of practice** (pratipatti-jñāna-darśana-viśuddhi): suppose there is one who, relying on the purity of the knowledge and vision of what is and is not the path, attains excellent knowledge and vision that knows the path of liberation has inferior, intermediate, and superior grades: the inferior is included in the practice of painful progress with slow penetration; the intermediate is included in the practices of painful progress with swift penetration and pleasant progress with slow penetration; the superior is included in the practice of pleasant progress with swift penetration. (7) **Purity of the knowledge and vision of the severance of practice** (pratipatti-prahāṇa-jñāna-darśana-viśuddhi): suppose there is one who, relying on the purity of the knowledge and vision of practice, attains excellent knowledge and vision: "I should sever the inferior and intermediate practices, and for the sake of generating the superior holy practice." (8) **Purity of objectless cessation** (anālambana-nirodha-viśuddhi): suppose there is one who, relying on the purity of the knowledge and vision of the severance of practice, realizes the permanent exhaustion of all outflows without remainder. (9) **Purity of the buddha-land** (buddhakṣetra-viśuddhi): this is the unsurpassed power shared by all buddhas, the fruition that is able to manifest the inconceivable adornments of the buddha-land, the extremely pure buddha-thought and the extremely pure bodhisattva-thought and their retinue dharmas.

**Confirmed faiths** (aveccaprasāda) refers to four confirmed faiths, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **Confirmed faith in the Buddha** (buddha-aveccaprasāda): the well-established supramundane faith in the Tathāgata that belongs to one who has already seen the truths, together with the subsequently attained well-established worldly faith. As with confirmed faith in the Buddha, so too the second, **confirmed faith in the Dharma**, and the third, **confirmed faith in the Saṅgha**: one should understand accordingly. (4) **Confirmed faith in the morality beloved of the noble ones** (āryakāntaśīla-aveccaprasāda): the well-established supramundane faith in the definite, non-transgressive morality beloved of the noble ones that has been attained by one who has already seen the truths, together with the subsequently attained well-established worldly faith.

**Fruits** refers to the four fruits of the ascetic (śramaṇa-phala), as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **The fruit of the stream-entering ascetic** (srota-āpanna-śramaṇa-phala): if classified by what predominates, the three fetters are permanently severed, namely: the view of a self (satkāya-dṛṣṭi), grasping at rules and rites (śīlavrataparāmarśa), and doubt (vicikitsā). If classified comprehensively, all afflictions severed by the path of seeing are permanently severed. Through that severance, one attains the fruit of stream-entry, being one of the non-falling-back type, either seven-returns-at-most or family-to-family. (2) **The fruit of the once-returning ascetic** (sakṛdāgāmin-śramaṇa-phala): if classified by what predominates, the three fetters are permanently severed, with desire, aversion, and delusion attenuated. If classified comprehensively, all afflictions severed by the path of seeing are permanently severed, together with the permanent severance of the upper and middle grades of afflictions of the desire realm severed by the path of cultivation. Through that severance, one attains the fruit of once-returning, or the one-interval type. (3) **The fruit of the non-returning ascetic** (anāgāmin-śramaṇa-phala): if classified by what predominates, the five lower fetters are permanently severed, namely: the view of a self, grasping at rules and rites, doubt, sensual desire, and ill will. If classified comprehensively, all afflictions severed by the path of seeing are permanently severed, together with the permanent severance of all afflictions of the desire realm severed by the path of cultivation, or with the permanent severance of afflictions of the form realm, or with the permanent severance of a portion of afflictions of the formless realm. Through that severance, one attains the fruit of non-returning, being one who attains cessation in the intermediate state, or upon birth, or without exertion, or with exertion, or the upstream-going type. (4) **The fruit of the arhat ascetic** (arhat-śramaṇa-phala): if classified by what predominates, desire, aversion, and delusion are permanently severed without remainder. If classified comprehensively, all afflictions severed by both the path of seeing and the path of cultivation are permanently severed without remainder. Through that severance, one attains the arhat's permanent exhaustion of outflows, as extensively explained, including the arhat's six constant abiding dharmas.

**Realms** (dhātu) refers to three kinds of realm, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **The realm of severance** (prahāṇa-dhātu): the severance of formations severed by the path of seeing. (2) **The realm of dispassion** (virāga-dhātu): the dispassion from formations severed by the path of cultivation. (3) **The realm of cessation** (nirodha-dhātu): the cessation of formations included within the basis (āśraya).

**Awakenings** (bodhi) refers to three kinds of awakening, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **Śrāvaka awakening**: the cessation attained through the transformation of the basis in the śrāvaka vehicle, together with the path leading to cessation. (2) **Pratyekabuddha awakening**: the cessation attained through the transformation of the basis in the pratyekabuddha vehicle, together with the path leading to cessation. (3) **Unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening** (anuttarā samyaksaṃbodhi): the cessation attained through the transformation of the basis in the great vehicle, together with the path leading to cessation, and the path that accomplishes the benefit and happiness of all sentient beings.

**Non-learner dharmas** refers to the ten non-learner dharmas, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **Non-learner right view**: the arhat's undefiled mental attention, conjoined with the contemplation of suffering as suffering through to the contemplation of the path as the path, consisting of the discrimination, thorough discrimination, and so forth of dharmas, as extensively explained above. As with non-learner right view, so too through to the eighth, **non-learner right concentration**: one should understand as explained above. (9) **Non-learner right liberation**: being free from the heaviness of all afflictions, the quality of pliancy and fitness that belongs to the non-learner mind, free from the obstruction of afflictions. (10) **Non-learner right knowledge**: the arhat's knowledge of exhaustion (kṣaya-jñāna) and knowledge of non-arising (anutpāda-jñāna).

**By their specific enumerations**: the fruits explained above should each be understood according to their specific numerical differentiations.

Furthermore, the verse states:

> Severance is due to multiple causes,
> Establishing the basis of severance,
> Through mental attention, reliance, and cultivation,
> And the order of attaining severance.

**The treatise states:** **Severance is due to multiple causes**: the fruit of severance is the severance of afflictions due to multiple causes, namely through four kinds of causes: (1) because the basis (āśraya) is extinguished, (2) because the basis is transformed, (3) because one knows the object, (4) because one delights in the object. Furthermore, there are five causes for the severance of afflictions: (1) because one knows their substance, (2) because one knows their objects, (3) because one knows their faults, (4) because one avoids their conditions, (5) because one cultivates the mental attention to their counteracting dharmas. Furthermore, there are four causes for afflictions having been severed: (1) because the basis has been completely extinguished, (2) because the basis has been completely transformed, (3) because the counteraction has been completely cultivated, (4) because the mind has been completely liberated.

**Establishing the basis of severance**: afflictions are severed from their objects. Once afflictions regarding objects have been severed, there is no bondage; the associated dharmas are also consequently severed. Afflictions of the future and present can be severed: the permanent destruction of their heaviness is called the severance of afflictions.

**Through mental attention**: afflictions are severed through the mental attention that cultivates the comprehensive contemplation of the truths.

**Through reliance**: afflictions are severed by relying on the seven bases of meditative absorption, namely from the first dhyāna through to the seventh, the station of nothingness.

**Through cultivation**: afflictions are severed through the cultivation of the four foundations of mindfulness, the four right exertions, through to the cultivation of the eight noble path factors.

**And the order of attaining severance**: there are five orders in the severance of afflictions. (1) First, the afflictions severed by the path of seeing are severed. (2) Afterward, the afflictions severed by the path of cultivation are severed. (3) First, the manifest afflictions are gradually disciplined. (4) Then afterward, all afflictions are permanently severed. (5) Finally, one transcends all afflictions entirely.

Furthermore, the verse states:

> The differentiations of severance should be known,
> Together with the characteristics and benefits of severance;
> As has been explained above,
> One should further know the many kinds.

**The treatise states:** **The differentiations of severance should be known as many kinds**: the severance of afflictions has many kinds of differentiation. There is severance of active afflictions; there is severance of latent tendencies. There is severance through the worldly path; there is severance through the supramundane path. There is severance through the mental attention of the śrāvaka vehicle; there is severance through the mental attention of the pratyekabuddha vehicle; there is severance through the mental attention of the bodhisattva vehicle. There is temporary severance; there is permanent severance. Such and other differentiations in the cessation of afflictions should be understood.

**The characteristics and benefits of severance should further be known as many kinds**: the severance of afflictions has many kinds of characteristic benefits that should be understood, as extensively explained in the sūtras. These are: the dharma of not falling back, being definitely proceeding toward awakening, having arrived at the correct Dharma, being on the verge of the correct Dharma, having realized the correct Dharma, having reached the source of realization, having thoroughly reached the source of realization, and the accomplishment of noble knowledge and vision. One no longer conceives that suffering, happiness, and other such dharmas are self-made, other-made, both self- and other-made, or neither self- nor other-made. One no longer conceives that suffering and happiness arise without cause. One no longer intentionally takes the lives of animals and other beings. One no longer intentionally transgresses one's training rules. One is no longer able to commit the five acts of immediate retribution. One no longer seeks out or requests non-Buddhist teachers, nor regards them as true fields of merit. One no longer gazes upon and attends to the faces of the assemblies of other śramaṇas, brāhmaṇas, and non-Buddhist teachers. One no longer generates doubt or confusion regarding the dharmas of the three times. One no longer receives the retribution of an eighth existence. Having thus realized the fruit of arhatship, with outflows permanently exhausted, having done what was to be done, having accomplished what was to be accomplished, one attains the arhat's six constant abiding dharmas, as extensively explained in the sūtras: one accomplishes six kinds of continuous abiding dharmas. When the eye sees form, the mind is without sorrow or joy, abiding in equanimity with mindfulness and clear knowing. Likewise with the ear and sounds, the nose and odors, the tongue and tastes, the body and tangibles, and when the mental consciousness cognizes dharmas, the mind is without sorrow or joy, abiding in equanimity with mindfulness and clear knowing. Whatever practices one undertakes, whether for one's own benefit, for the benefit of others, for the benefit of the multitude, for the happiness of the multitude, out of compassion for the world, or for gods and humans to attain meaning, benefit, and happiness: such and other benefits of the permanent severance of afflictions, with their many kinds of characteristics, should be understood.

---

## Fascicle 4

### Compendium of Topics, Part Four

Having thus explained fruits, we shall now explain meritorious qualities. The verse states:

> Immeasurables, liberations,
> Bases of mastery and totality bases,
> Non-contention, wish-born knowledge,
> Unimpeded knowledges, and supernatural powers.

**The treatise states:** **Immeasurables** (apramāṇa) refers to the four immeasurables, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **Immeasurable loving-kindness** (maitrī): with a mind conjoined with loving-kindness, without enmity, without hatred, without harmfulness, vast, great, immeasurable, and supremely well cultivated, one pervades one direction, then in due order through to all ten directions, throughout all boundless world-systems, with the mind's resolve pervading completely, dwelling in its fulfillment. "Conjoined with a mind of loving-kindness" means: toward sentient beings who are in a state of neither suffering nor happiness, wishing to bestow happiness, the mind is conjoined with the corresponding intention (āśaya). "Without enmity" means the counteraction of this: because of the aversion that wishes to inflict suffering. "Without hatred" means the counteraction of this: because of the aversion that obstructs happiness. "Without harmfulness" means the counteraction of this: because of the aversion that wishes to impose what is unsuitable. "Vast" means: through the mental attention directed to the domain of what is seen. "Great" means: through the mental attention directed to the domain of what is heard. "Immeasurable" means: through the mental attention directed to the domain of what is cognized. "Supremely well cultivated" means: through the correspondence with repeated practice, being free from the hindrances. "Pervading one direction, then in due order through to all ten directions, throughout all boundless world-systems" means: pervading both the physical world-system and the sentient-being world-system as objects. "With the mind's resolve" means: taking as one's object the domain contemplated by the mind's resolve. "Pervading completely" means: taking as one's object the uninterrupted domain of sentient beings. "Dwelling in its fulfillment" means: as previously explained regarding the dhyānas.

(2) **Immeasurable compassion** (karuṇā): with a mind conjoined with compassion, and so forth as extensively explained. "Conjoined with a mind of compassion" means: toward sentient beings who are in a state of suffering, wishing to remove their suffering, the mind is conjoined with the corresponding intention. "Without enmity" means the counteraction of this: because of the harm that inflicts suffering. "Without hatred" means the counteraction of this: because of the harm that obstructs the removal of suffering. "Without harmfulness" means the counteraction of this: because of not delighting in imposing what is unsuitable. The rest is as stated above.

(3) **Immeasurable sympathetic joy** (muditā): with a mind conjoined with joy, and so forth as extensively explained. "Conjoined with a mind of joy" means: toward sentient beings who are in a state of happiness, rejoicing in their happiness, the mind is conjoined with the corresponding intention. "Without enmity" means the counteraction of this: because of not delighting in wishing to inflict suffering. "Without hatred" means the counteraction of this: because of not delighting in obstructing their happiness. "Without harmfulness" means the counteraction of this: because of not delighting in imposing what is unsuitable. The rest is as stated above.

(4) **Immeasurable equanimity** (upekṣā): with a mind conjoined with equanimity, and so forth as extensively explained. "Conjoined with a mind of equanimity" means: wishing that they not be defiled, the mind is conjoined with the corresponding intention. "Without enmity" means the counteraction of this: because of causing defilement through desire and aversion. "Without hatred" means the counteraction of this: because of obstructing the removal of defilement through desire and aversion. "Without harmfulness" means the counteraction of this: because of the inverted non-defilement of desire and aversion. The rest is as stated above.

What is the essential nature of these four immeasurables? Loving-kindness has the wholesome root of non-aversion as its substance. Compassion has the wholesome root of non-harmfulness as its substance. Sympathetic joy has the wholesome root of non-jealousy as its substance. Equanimity has the wholesome roots of non-desire and non-aversion as its substance. They are all dharmas of compassion for sentient beings. Among these four, loving-kindness is solely non-aversion. The next two immeasurables are a portion of non-aversion. Equanimity is a portion of non-desire and non-aversion. Furthermore, the concentration conjoined with them, together with all the associated mental factors and their retinue, are also the substance of the four immeasurables: one should understand this. Only when one has first well cultivated the mind through the practice of the superior dharma and then relies on the pure dhyānas can one attain the pure immeasurables: one should understand this.

**Liberations** (vimokṣa) refers to the eight liberations, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **The liberation of observing forms while possessing form**: "possessing form" means relying on the meditative concentration of the form realm through the mind's resolve and contemplation. "Forms" means those forms as extensively discriminated in the section on the bases of mastery. "Observing" means: for the purpose of attaining mastery over transformation regarding forms, through the mind's resolve and contemplation, one displays those characteristics. (2) **The liberation of observing external forms while internally without the perception of form**: "internally without the perception of form" means relying on the formless meditative concentration through the mind's resolve and contemplation. "External" means: through the mind's resolve and contemplation, excluding the sense-faculties such as the eye, one contemplates the remaining forms. "Observing forms" means as stated above. (3) **The liberation of the pure, through bodily witness, dwelling in its fulfillment**: "pure" means: through the mind's resolve and contemplation, one exclusively contemplates pure, subtle forms for the purpose of attaining the superior pleasant abiding. "Liberation" means: one is liberated from the obstructing function of the mind regarding both pure and impure forms. "Body" means: the mental body. "Witness" means: one bears witness through the wisdom of severance and attainment. "Dwelling in its fulfillment" means as stated above. The formless liberations are as previously discriminated. The difference herein is: for the purpose of realizing mastery over all kinds of bodily action, and for the purpose of being liberated from the obstacles thereto, and furthermore by removing light-and-form and through the mind's resolve contemplating boundless space, this is called the fourth, the **liberation of the station of boundless space**. For the purpose of generating the noble supernatural powers, non-contention, wish-born knowledge, unimpeded knowledges, and other such meritorious qualities, and for the purpose of realizing mastery over the mind that is able to assist in generating those meritorious qualities, and for the purpose of being liberated from the obstacles thereto, and furthermore through the mind's resolve contemplating boundless consciousness, this is called the fifth, the **liberation of the station of boundless consciousness**. After the practitioner has carried out this method of generating meritorious qualities and caused the fourth dhyāna to manifest in present awareness for the purpose of generating the meritorious qualities, then for the purpose of realizing mastery over the most supreme, undefiled abiding, and for the purpose of being liberated from the obstacles thereto, and furthermore through the mind's resolve contemplating nothingness, this is called the sixth, the **liberation of the station of nothingness**. For the purpose of realizing mastery over the most supreme abiding of the pinnacle of existence, and for the purpose of being liberated from the obstacles thereto, and furthermore through the mind's resolve contemplating neither-perception-nor-non-perception, this is called the seventh, the **liberation of the station of neither-perception-nor-non-perception**. For the purpose of realizing mastery over the most supreme, quiescent abiding, and for the purpose of being liberated from the obstacles thereto, and furthermore proceeding from the mind of the station of neither-perception-nor-non-perception to the cessation of the in-breath and out-breath and the cessation of grasping at objects, this is called the eighth, the **liberation of the cessation of perception and feeling**.

**Bases of mastery** (abhibhvāyatana) refers to the eight bases of mastery, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **Internally having the perception of form, observing external forms that are few; whether beautiful or ugly, whether inferior or superior; regarding those forms, one masters them, one knows and sees them: one attains such a perception. This is called the first base of mastery.** "Internally having the perception of form" means as explained in the section on liberations. "External" means: by excluding the sense-faculties such as the eye, one reveals the remaining forms. "Observing forms" means as stated above. "Few" means: through the mind's resolve and contemplation, forms included among material possessions. "Whether beautiful or ugly" means: through the mind's resolve and contemplation, forms included among pure and impure forms. "Whether inferior or superior" means: through the mind's resolve and contemplation, forms included among pure and impure sounds, odors, flavors, and tangibles. "Mastering those forms" means: through the mental attention of counteracting what is to be counteracted, one is not troubled by the obstructing function. "Knowing" means: through the path of śamatha. "Seeing" means: through the path of vipaśyanā. "Attaining such a perception" means: regarding what is truly mastered, one attains the perception of genuine mastery without conceit; regarding what is not mastered, one attains the perception of genuine non-mastery without conceit. (2) **Internally having the perception of form, observing external forms that are many, and so forth: this is called the second base of mastery.** The two kinds with internal formlessness, observing few and observing many, are likewise. "Internally having the perception of form, observing external forms" means as stated above. "Many" means: displaying forms throughout the entire sentient-being world-system and physical world-system through pervading contemplation. The rest is as stated above. (5) **Internally without the perception of form, observing external forms that are blue**: blue in hue, blue in visibility, blue in radiance, like an umāka flower, or like a blue-dyed cloth of Vārāṇasī. Likewise one should extensively explain yellow, red, and white. The differences herein are: yellow is like a karṇikāra flower, or like a yellow-dyed cloth of Vārāṇasī. Red is like a bandhujīvaka flower, or like a red-dyed cloth of Vārāṇasī. White is like the star Uśanas, or like a fresh white cloth of Vārāṇasī. "Blue" is the general term. "Blue in hue" means innate blue. "Blue in visibility" means blue formed through combination. "Blue in radiance" means the fresh, pure, radiant blue emanating from those two. As with blue, so too with yellow, red, and white: one should understand as extensively explained. The rest is as stated above. That two similes are given for each is to reveal two kinds of form: innate and combined. These eight bases of mastery serve as the support for the cultivation of the three kinds of liberations regarding form-objects. The latter four bases of mastery involve the mind's resolve and contemplation directed toward forms of the desire-realm heavens and forms of the form realm. Furthermore, one should understand that the bases of mastery involve the mental attention of counteracting the seeds that follow from lower grounds; they do not involve the mental attention of counteracting what is to be counteracted on one's own ground.

**Totality bases** (kṛtsnāyatana) refers to the ten totality bases, as extensively explained in the sūtras. The earth totality base: one comprehends above, below, and to the sides as non-dual and immeasurable. Likewise with water, fire, and wind totalities. Blue, yellow, red, white, space, and consciousness totalities are likewise comprehended above, below, and to the sides as non-dual and immeasurable. "Earth totality base" means: because the basis of color pervades completely, the color that depends on it also pervades completely, through its being developed. "One comprehends" means: the person who realizes this contemplation. "Above, below, and to the sides" means: pervading all directions and the four intermediate directions. "Non-dual" means: separate from the remaining elements and unmixed with other visible forms, pervading completely. "Immeasurable" means: without limits or boundaries, pervading completely. As with the earth totality base, so too with the remaining water, fire, wind, blue, yellow, red, and white totality bases, each as appropriate. "Space totality base" means: through the mental attention that counteracts all form-characteristics, contemplation pervades completely. The rest is as stated above. "Consciousness totality base" means: through the mental attention that contemplates boundless consciousness, contemplation pervades completely. The rest is as stated above.

Herein, through the three liberations one attains mastery over forms. Having attained that, only then can one truly be said to have mastery over forms, because it is fully accomplished. Above the station of consciousness, there is no pervading, because one has departed from the discrimination that depends on objects of immeasurable form and shape. One should understand that the bases of mastery and the totality bases are the paths that purify the liberations. Because the bases of mastery master their objects, and because the totality bases pervade their objects, they are able to purify the liberations: one should understand this.

**Non-contention** (araṇā): it has the nature of non-deluded knowledge and vision, included within the conduct that guards others from afflictions, together with the associated concentration and mental factors. This is because this conduct pervades most of what is practiced.

**Wish-born knowledge** (praṇidhi-jñāna): it has the nature of non-deluded knowledge and vision, included within the complete and exhaustive knowing in accord with reality of knowable dharmas throughout the three times and beyond time, together with the associated concentration and mental factors. This is because this conduct pervades most of what is practiced.

**Unimpeded knowledges** (pratisaṃvid) refers to the four unimpeded knowledges, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **Unimpeded knowledge of dharmas** (dharma-pratisaṃvid): it has the nature of non-deluded knowledge and vision included within the realization in accord with reality of the differentiated names of all kinds of all dharmas, together with the associated concentration and mental factors. (2) **Unimpeded knowledge of meaning** (artha-pratisaṃvid): it has the nature of non-deluded knowledge and vision included within the realization in accord with reality of the various characteristics of all kinds of all dharmas. The rest is as stated above. (3) **Unimpeded knowledge of etymology** (nirukti-pratisaṃvid): it has the nature of non-deluded knowledge and vision included within the realization in accord with reality of the etymological explanations of all kinds of all dharmas. The rest is as stated above. (4) **Unimpeded knowledge of eloquence** (pratibhāna-pratisaṃvid): it has the nature of non-deluded knowledge and vision included within the realization in accord with reality of the penetration of all kinds of all dharmas, together with the associated concentration and mental factors. This is because this conduct pervades most of what is practiced.

**Supernatural powers** (abhijñā) refers to the six supernatural powers, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **The supernatural power of psychic ability, bearing witness through knowledge and vision** (ṛddhi-abhijñā): it has the nature of non-deluded knowledge and vision for the purpose of manifesting mastery over all kinds of bodily action, together with the associated concentration and mental factors. This is because this conduct operates in many definite domains. (2) **The supernatural power of the divine ear, bearing witness through knowledge and vision** (divya-śrotra-abhijñā): it has the nature of non-deluded knowledge and vision for the purpose of hearing all kinds of verbal action at will. The rest is as stated above. (3) **The supernatural power of knowing the differentiation of others' minds, bearing witness through knowledge and vision** (paracittajñāna-abhijñā): it has the nature of non-deluded knowledge and vision for the purpose of entering the mental conduct of all kinds of other beings. The rest is as stated above. (4) **The supernatural power of recollecting past lives, bearing witness through knowledge and vision** (pūrvanivāsānusmṛti-abhijñā): it has the nature of non-deluded knowledge and vision for the purpose of entering all kinds of former destinies and courses of conduct. The rest is as stated above. (5) **The supernatural power of the knowledge of death and rebirth, bearing witness through knowledge and vision** (cyutyupapatti-jñāna-abhijñā): it has the nature of non-deluded knowledge and vision for the purpose of entering all kinds of sentient beings' destinies and courses of conduct. The rest is as stated above. (6) **The supernatural power of the exhaustion of outflows, bearing witness through knowledge and vision** (āsravakṣaya-abhijñā): it has the nature of non-deluded knowledge and vision for the purpose of entering liberation from all afflictions and from all suffering without remainder, together with the associated concentration and mental factors. This is because this conduct operates in many definite domains.

Furthermore, the verse states:

> Marks, minor marks, and purities,
> Together with the powers and fearlessnesses,
> Non-guardednesses and mindful presences,
> And the permanent severance of habitual tendencies.

**The treatise states:** **Marks** refers to the thirty-two marks of a great being (mahāpuruṣa-lakṣaṇa), as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **The mark of a great being with well-established feet**: because the Tathāgata's provisions for awakening are well and fully completed, and because he has fully upheld and received the practice of equality, he has brought about this mark. Through this mark, whoever briefly sees the Tathāgata immediately believes that he is a great being. "Foot-mark" means the foot-sign, the foot-appearance. As with the mark of well-established feet, so too with all the remaining marks of a great being: one should understand each as appropriate. That is, regarding the hands, one should explain the hand-marks, hand-signs, and hand-appearances. Likewise regarding the crown of the head and the remaining bodily features, one should explain each extensively according to its name.

**Minor marks** (anuvyañjana) refers to the eighty minor marks, as extensively explained in the sūtras. These minor marks, if classified comprehensively, are as explained in the Bodhisattva Grounds chapter. If classified according to the marks that are appropriate for sentient beings and following the predominant marks, they are as explained in the *Mahāprajñāpāramitā-sūtra*. If one wishes the extensive discrimination of the minor marks, one should understand them as explained in the section on the extensive discrimination of marks.

**Purities** refers to four all-aspect purities, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **Purity of the basis** (āśraya-viśuddhi): because the Tathāgata has realized all-aspect pure knowledge and all-aspect pure severance, he attains ultimate unsurpassed mastery regarding the taking up, abiding in, and relinquishing of the basis. (2) **Purity of the domain** (ālambana-viśuddhi): because the Tathāgata has realized all-aspect pure knowledge and all-aspect pure severance, he attains ultimate unsurpassed mastery regarding the domain of transformation of all things. (3) **Purity of mind** (citta-viśuddhi): because the Tathāgata has realized all-aspect pure knowledge and all-aspect pure severance, he attains ultimate unsurpassed mastery regarding the development of the mind in all kinds of worldly and supramundane wholesome roots. (4) **Purity of knowledge** (jñāna-viśuddhi): because the Tathāgata has realized all-aspect pure knowledge and all-aspect pure severance, he attains ultimate unsurpassed mastery regarding the unattached, unobstructed knowledge of all kinds of objects of knowledge.

**Powers** (bala) refers to the ten powers of the Tathāgata, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) **The power of knowledge of what is possible and impossible** (sthānāsthāna-jñāna-bala): it has the nature of unobstructed knowledge regarding all kinds of cause and effect, able to answer questions and make declarations in accord with reality, together with the associated concentration and mental factors. (2) **The power of knowledge of one's own karma** (svakarma-jñāna-bala): it has the nature of unobstructed knowledge regarding the karma arising in all kinds of particular places and continua, together with the resultant retribution. The rest is as stated above. (3) **The power of knowledge of dhyānas, liberations, samādhis, and samāpattis**: it has the nature of unobstructed knowledge regarding the skillful means of gathering all kinds of worldly pure meritorious qualities. The rest is as stated above. (4) **The power of knowledge of the superiority and inferiority of faculties** (indriyaparāpara-jñāna-bala): it has the nature of unobstructed knowledge regarding the differentiation of faculties among all kinds of sentient beings to be converted, which serve as the basis for supramundane meritorious qualities. The rest is as stated above. (5) **The power of knowledge of the various convictions** (nānādhimukti-jñāna-bala): it has the nature of unobstructed knowledge regarding the differentiation of intentions (āśaya) among all kinds of sentient beings to be converted. The rest is as stated above. (6) **The power of knowledge of the various dispositions** (nānādhātu-jñāna-bala): it has the nature of unobstructed knowledge regarding the differentiation of latent tendencies among all kinds of sentient beings to be converted. The rest is as stated above. (7) **The power of knowledge of the paths leading to all destinations** (sarvatragāminī-pratipat-jñāna-bala): it has the nature of unobstructed knowledge regarding the differentiation among all kinds of vehicles and means of liberation. The rest is as stated above. (8) **The power of knowledge of recollecting past lives** (pūrvanivāsānusmṛti-jñāna-bala): it has the nature of unobstructed knowledge regarding all kinds of differentiation among former-life destinies. The rest is as stated above. (9) **The power of knowledge of death and rebirth** (cyutyupapatti-jñāna-bala): it has the nature of unobstructed knowledge regarding all kinds of differentiation among future-life destinies. The rest is as stated above. (10) **The power of knowledge of the exhaustion of outflows** (āsravakṣaya-jñāna-bala): it has the nature of unobstructed knowledge regarding the differentiation among all kinds of skillful means of liberation from destinations and non-destinations, together with the associated concentration and mental factors. Furthermore, in all the powers, one should state that they are able to answer questions and make declarations in accord with reality.

**Fearlessnesses** (vaiśāradya) refers to the four fearlessnesses, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) The Buddha makes the truthful declaration: "I am the perfectly and fully awakened one." If anyone should challenge: "Regarding such-and-such a dharma, you are not perfectly awakened," the Buddha correctly sees that there is no basis for such a challenge. Therefore he is fearless. This is because the Tathāgata has realized the excellently pure all-aspect knowledge. (2) The Buddha makes the truthful declaration: "My outflows are exhausted." If anyone should challenge: "Such-and-such outflows are not yet exhausted," the Buddha correctly sees that there is no basis for such a challenge. Therefore he is fearless. This is because the Tathāgata has realized the excellently pure all-aspect severance. These two fearlessnesses are based on the virtue of self-benefit. (3) The Buddha makes the truthful declaration: "I have taught my disciples the dharmas that constitute obstructions: defilement necessarily constitutes an obstruction." If anyone should challenge: "Even if one is defiled and habituated to such dharmas, they cannot constitute an obstruction," the Buddha correctly sees that there is no basis for such a challenge. Therefore he is fearless. This is based on the Tathāgata's teaching to beings to be converted of all kinds of dharmas that are to be counteracted. (4) The Buddha makes the truthful declaration: "I have taught my disciples the path of liberation: cultivating concentration leads to liberation." If anyone should challenge: "Even if one cultivates this path, one cannot be liberated, one cannot correctly exhaust suffering or reach the end of suffering," the Buddha correctly sees that there is no basis for such a challenge. Therefore he is fearless. This is based on the Tathāgata's teaching to beings to be converted of all kinds of counteracting dharmas. These two fearlessnesses are based on the virtue of benefiting others.

**Non-guardednesses** (arakṣya) refers to the three non-guardednesses, as extensively explained in the sūtras. (1) The Tathāgata's manifest bodily actions are excellently pure; there are no impure manifest bodily actions that need to be concealed. Therefore he does not guard. This is because the Tathāgata's bodily actions are excellently pure at all times and in all aspects. When he teaches the Dharma to beings to be converted, he is able, through superior power, to subdue and gather the entire assembly. As with the non-guardedness of bodily action, so too with the second, the **non-guardedness of verbal action**, and the third, the **non-guardedness of mental action**: one should understand accordingly.

**Mindful presences** (smṛtyupasthāna) refers to the three mindful presences, as extensively explained in the sūtras. When gathering beings to be converted, regarding the differentiation of conduct among three kinds of assemblies, one abides in supreme equanimity: without love, without anger, without defiled mind.

**The permanent severance of habitual tendencies**: the Tathāgatas have departed from the beginningless, immeasurable, incalculable great kalpas of birth and death. Because they have realized the nature of not being carried along, they have realized the Tathāgata's excellently pure knowledge and severance.

Furthermore, the verse states:

> The dharma of non-forgetfulness,
> Together with the Tathāgata's great compassion,
> The exclusive dharmas of the Buddha,
> And the all-aspect sublime knowledge.

**The treatise states:** **The dharma of non-forgetfulness**: because one has realized the all-aspect capacity never to miss the right time for any action to be done for any sentient being to be converted, one has realized the Tathāgata's excellently pure knowledge and severance.

**The Tathāgata's great compassion**: the Tathāgata's compassion is called "great compassion" (mahākaruṇā) for four reasons. (1) Because it is accomplished through activity based on the all-aspect, excellently pure transformation of the basis. (2) Because it is obtained through long cultivation. (3) Because it is induced by excellently pure knowledge. (4) Because it takes as its object the extremely deep, firm, various, and solidly established suffering of all aspects.

**Exclusive dharmas of the Buddha** (āveṇika-buddha-dharma) refers to the eighteen exclusive dharmas of the Buddha, as extensively explained in the sūtras. Because they surpass all śrāvaka and pratyekabuddha grounds, their establishment should be understood. (1) **The Tathāgata has no erroneous actions**: because there are no undefiled, accidental mistakes or faults. (2) **He has no sudden, violent speech**: because there are no undefiled, loud laughter or violent sounds. (3) **He has no forgetfulness**: because there is no undefiled failure to recollect what was long ago done or long ago said. (4) **He has no unsettled mind**: because in all deportments of walking, standing, and so forth, the mental attention of concentration always follows and turns continuously. (5) **He has no perception of variety**: because regarding cyclic existence and cessation, he has realized the non-discriminating, non-differentiating knowledge. (6) **He has no relinquishment without prior investigation**: because he ultimately never abandons the work of sentient beings. (7) **His aspiration does not regress**: because he has attained the purity of the cognitive obstruction. As with the non-regression of aspiration, so too with the eighth, **non-regression of diligence**; the ninth, **non-regression of mindfulness**; the tenth, **non-regression of concentration**; the eleventh, **non-regression of wisdom**; and the twelfth, **non-regression of the knowledge and vision of liberation**: one should understand accordingly. (13) **Unattached, unobstructed knowledge regarding the past**: because at the moment of wishing and attending, he knows all aspects. As with regard to the past, so too with the fourteenth, **regarding the future**, and the fifteenth, **regarding the present**, his unattached, unobstructed knowledge: one should understand accordingly. (16) **All bodily actions of the Tathāgata are led by knowledge and proceed in accordance with knowledge**: because they are generated and received through knowledge, at all times employing skillful means. As with bodily action, so too with the seventeenth, **verbal action**, and the eighteenth, **mental action**: one should understand accordingly.

**All-aspect sublime knowledge** (sarvākāra-jñāna): because one has realized the Tathāgata's most extremely pure knowledge and severance. It has the nature of unobstructed knowledge regarding the numerical differentiations of all kinds of characteristics of both defiled and pure dharmas, together with the associated concentration and mental factors. Furthermore, the Tathāgata, abiding in the undefiled realm, for the purpose of accomplishing all activities for all sentient beings throughout the lands of the ten directions, manifests the Buddha's existence and birth, manifests bodily appearance and verbal expression, and with the mind having conduct and having proclamation, achieves complete and perfect awakening, turns the wondrous wheel of Dharma, and enters the great cessation, with unobstructed knowledge and the associated concentration and mental factors. This is also called the all-aspect sublime knowledge.

Furthermore, meritorious qualities of the Buddha such as the marks and minor marks, for the purpose of interpreting the meaning of the sūtras, have been briefly presented. If one wishes extensive discrimination, one should understand them as explained in the Bodhisattva Grounds chapter.

Having thus separately explained the nine topics, we shall now explain their general discrimination. The verse states:

> One should know that of the preceding nine topics,
> The first serves as the basis for two,
> The next two and the latter six
> Comprise defilement and purity.
> Because of the differentiation of the defiled basis,
> Because of the object of purity,
> Because the mind does not flow into distraction,
> Because of the skillful means of correct cultivation,
> Because of the differentiation of their stages,
> Because of the causes such as linguistic expression,
> Because of their fruits, and because of their qualities,
> The number and order are exactly thus.

**The treatise states:** Among the preceding nine topics, the first topic, all dharmas, serves as the basis for two things: (1) the basis for defilement, and (2) the basis for purity. The next two topics comprise all defilements: (1) through the topic of realms, (2) through the topic of defilements. The latter six topics comprise all purity: (1) through the topic of the truths, (2) through the topic of supports, (3) through the topic of the factors of awakening, (4) through the topic of sentient beings, (5) through the topic of fruits, (6) through the topic of meritorious qualities.

The two topics comprise defilement because they are the basis of defilement and the differentiation of defilement. The six topics comprise purity because: the object of purity; within the objects, the mind does not flow into distraction; through non-distraction, one correctly applies skillful means to the object; the stages of those who correctly apply skillful means are differentiated; because of causes such as linguistic expression; because of the fruits of correct skillful means; and because of the meritorious qualities of those fruits. Herein, "the differentiation of their stages" and "the causes such as linguistic expression" are both established within the topic of sentient beings: one should understand this. "Because of the causes such as linguistic expression" means: for the ease of linguistic expression, for conformity with the world, for the avoidance of fear, and for the purpose of causing faith and knowledge regarding the meritorious qualities and faults of self and others. For these reasons, the number of these nine topics is definite, and their order is definite: one should understand this.

In order to contemplate these nine topics, one should further explain them extensively. The verse states:

> If one wishes to correctly contemplate
> The immeasurable question-and-answer differentiations,
> Through the words of the buddhas,
> They are comprised by topics and concepts.

**The treatise states:** If one wishes to contemplate the immeasurable question-and-answer differentiations of the above nine topics, through two kinds of inclusion one should contemplate them: (1) through the inclusion of all buddha-word topics, and (2) through the inclusion of all buddha-word concepts. Herein, the inclusion of all buddha-word topics should be understood through three kinds of sūtra: (1) the sūtra of increase by tens (*Daśottara*), (2) the sūtra of extensive meaning (*Arthavistara*), and (3) the sūtra of the collection of different categories (*Saṃgītiparyāya*). The inclusion of all buddha-word concepts is through four kinds of summary verses (udāna-gāthā). What are the four? The verse states:

> Sentences, delusion, elaboration,
> Abiding, truth, purity, sublimity,
> Quiescence, nature, principle,
> Provisional designation, direct realization.
>
> Direction, stage, discrimination,
> Action, retention, increase, decrease,
> Darkness, language, what is realized, the supreme,
> Renunciation, transmigration, storage and protection.
>
> Investigation and manifestation,
> Sleep and connection,
> Mutual inclusion of characteristics and correspondence,
> Teaching, upholding, and sequence.
>
> Task, object, and yoga,
> Śamatha and vipaśyanā,
> The mental attentions and instructions,
> Qualities, awakening, and the sacred teaching.

**The treatise states:** **Sentences** (pada): namely, the six sense-bases, immeasurable domains, immeasurable directions, immeasurable time-periods. Furthermore, there are three realms: (1) the desire realm, (2) the form realm, (3) the formless realm. Furthermore, there are three world-systems: (1) a small thousand-fold world-system, (2) a middle thousand-fold world-system, (3) a great thousand-fold world-system. Furthermore, there are four groups: (1) the householder group, (2) the renunciant group, (3) the upāsaka group, (4) the non-human group. Furthermore, there are three feelings: painful feeling, pleasant feeling, and neither-painful-nor-pleasant feeling. Furthermore, there are three times: past, future, and present. Furthermore, there are Three Jewels: the jewel of the Buddha, the jewel of the Dharma, and the jewel of the Saṅgha. Furthermore, there are three kinds of dharmas: wholesome dharmas, unwholesome dharmas, and indeterminate dharmas. Furthermore, there are three kinds of defilement: the defilement of afflictions, the defilement of karma, and the defilement of birth. Furthermore, there are four noble truths: suffering, origin, cessation, and path. Furthermore, there are nine sequential absorptions: from the first dhyāna through to the cessation of perception and feeling. Furthermore, there are thirty-seven factors of awakening: foundations of mindfulness, right exertions, bases of spiritual power, faculties, powers, factors of awakening, and path factors. Furthermore, there are four fruits of the ascetic: the fruit of stream-entry, the fruit of once-returning, the fruit of non-returning, and the supreme fruit of arhatship. Furthermore, there are many supreme meritorious qualities: immeasurables, liberations, bases of mastery, totality bases, non-contention, wish-born knowledge, unimpeded knowledges, six supernatural powers, and so forth. Furthermore, based on the extensive vehicle, there are five categories: (1) characteristics, (2) names, (3) discrimination, (4) suchness, (5) correct knowledge. Furthermore, there are two kinds of emptiness: the emptiness of sentient beings and the emptiness of dharmas. Furthermore, there are two kinds of selflessness: the selflessness of sentient beings and the selflessness of dharmas. Furthermore, there is the practice of the middle, departing from two extremes: departing from the extreme of superimposition and from the extreme of denial. Furthermore, there are four kinds of reality: (1) reality established by the world, (2) reality established by principle, (3) the domain of knowledge purified of the afflictive obstruction, (4) the domain of knowledge purified of the cognitive obstruction. Furthermore, there are four kinds of investigation: investigation of names, investigation of things, investigation of the conventional designation of inherent nature, and investigation of the conventional designation of differentiation. Furthermore, there are four kinds of comprehensive knowledge in accord with reality: comprehensive knowledge in accord with reality induced by the investigation of names, induced by the investigation of things, induced by the investigation of the conventional designation of inherent nature, and induced by the investigation of the conventional designation of differentiation. Furthermore, there are three kinds of inherent nature: the imagined inherent nature (parikalpita-svabhāva), the dependent inherent nature (paratantra-svabhāva), and the consummate inherent nature (pariniṣpanna-svabhāva). Furthermore, there are three kinds of absence of inherent nature: the absence of inherent nature of characteristics (lakṣaṇa-niḥsvabhāvatā), the absence of inherent nature of arising (utpatti-niḥsvabhāvatā), and the absence of inherent nature of the ultimate (paramārtha-niḥsvabhāvatā). Furthermore, there are five aspects of great awakening: regarding its inherent nature, its function, its means, its turning, and its returning. Furthermore, there are five aspects of the great vehicle: (1) the seed, (2) the entering, (3) the sequence, (4) the correct turning, (5) the fruit of correct turning; namely: the initial generation of the aspiration, the arising of great compassion toward all sentient beings, the pāramitās, the means of attraction, and the maturation of one's own and others' continua. Furthermore, there are five immeasurable concepts: (1) the immeasurable concept of the sentient-being realm, (2) the immeasurable concept of world-systems, (3) the immeasurable concept of the dharma-realm, (4) the immeasurable concept of the realm of those to be disciplined, (5) the immeasurable concept of the realm of means of discipline. Furthermore, there is the reaching of the true meaning: reaching suchness and the knowledge therein across all immeasurable dharmas. Furthermore, there is inconceivable majestic faith. Furthermore, there is unobstructed knowledge. Furthermore, there are the thirty-two marks of a great being and the eighty minor marks. Furthermore, there are four kinds of all-aspect purity. The ten powers, four fearlessnesses, three mindful presences, three non-guardednesses, great compassion, the dharma of non-forgetfulness, the permanent severance of habitual tendencies, and the all-aspect sublime knowledge. As explained above, in summary there are only two kinds: (1) sentences interpreted within the śrāvaka vehicle, and (2) sentences interpreted within the great vehicle.

**Delusion** (viparyāsa): the four inversions: (1) the inversion of conceiving permanence in what is impermanent, (2) the inversion of conceiving pleasure in what is suffering, (3) the inversion of conceiving purity in what is impure, (4) the inversion of conceiving a self in what is without self. **Elaboration** (prapañca): the aggregates of afflictions and affliction-associated dharmas. **Abiding** (sthiti): the four bases of consciousness and the seven stations of consciousness. **Truth** (satya): suchness and the four noble truths. **Purity** (viśuddhi): three kinds of pure nature: (1) purity of inherent nature, (2) purity of domain, (3) purity of stage. **Sublimity** (praṇīta): it is called "sublime" because of the supreme establishment in the domain of the Three Jewels. **Quiescence** (śānti): from one's own wholesome desire for the Dharma through to all the factors of awakening and the realized fruits, all are called "quiescence." **Nature** (svabhāva): the characteristics of dharmas, whether specific characteristics, shared characteristics, conventionally designated characteristics, causal characteristics, or resultant characteristics, are collectively called "nature." **Principle** (nyāya): dependent arising and the four kinds of reasoning. **Provisional designation** (prajñapti): merely designating sentient beings in dependence on dharmas, and merely designating dharmas in dependence on characteristics. **Direct realization** (abhisamaya): six kinds of direct realization, as shall be explained in the chapter on the accomplishment of direct realization.

**Direction** (deśa): namely, the form aggregate. **Stage** (avasthā): namely, the feeling aggregate. **Discrimination** (saṃjñā): namely, the perception aggregate. **Action** (saṃskāra): namely, the formations aggregate. **Retention** (vijñāna): namely, the consciousness aggregate. **Increase** (upacaya): this has two kinds, one should understand: (1) increase of afflictions, (2) increase of karma. As with increase, **decrease** (apacaya) is also of two kinds: (1) decrease of afflictions, (2) decrease of karma. **Darkness** (tamas): ignorance and doubt. **Language** (vāc): the twelve divisions of the teaching's discourse, called "language." **What is realized** (adhigama): the various dharma-meanings that are taught, called "what is realized." **The supreme** (uttara): namely, the fruits of the ascetic. **Renunciation** (viveka): five kinds of renunciation: (1) renunciation from misconduct, (2) renunciation from sensuality, (3) renunciation from material possessions, (4) renunciation from assemblies, (5) renunciation from afflictions. **Transmigration** (saṃsāra): namely, the three realms and the five destinies. **Storage and protection** (ālaya): namely, attachment to the past, hope for the future, and clinging to the present.

**Investigation** (pravicaya): namely, a single practice, with propositions following the antecedent, propositions following the consequent, propositions with substance, propositions without substance, form dharmas and non-form dharmas, visible and invisible, resistant and non-resistant, with outflows and without outflows, conditioned and unconditioned, with contention and without contention, with relishing and without relishing, based on indulgence and based on renunciation, worldly and supramundane, belonging to and not belonging to, internal and external, coarse and subtle, superior and inferior, distant and near, with objects and without objects, associated and unassociated, with activity and without activity, with a basis and without a basis, cause and non-cause, fruit and non-fruit, ripened and non-ripened, with cause and without cause, with fruit and without fruit, with ripening and without ripening, with appropriation and without appropriation, created by the four great elements and not created by the four great elements, of the same class and of another class, with something superior and with nothing superior, past, future, and present, wholesome, unwholesome, and indeterminate, bound to the desire realm, bound to the form realm, and bound to the formless realm, of learning, beyond learning, and neither of learning nor beyond learning, severed by seeing, severed by cultivation, and not to be severed. Furthermore, there are four conditions: (1) causal condition, (2) immediately preceding condition, (3) object condition, (4) predominant condition. Furthermore, there are four reliances: (1) rely on the Dharma, not on persons, (2) rely on meaning, not on words, (3) rely on sūtras of definitive meaning, not on sūtras of provisional meaning, (4) rely on wisdom, not on consciousness. Furthermore, there are four immeasurables, four foundations of mindfulness, four right exertions, four bases of spiritual power, five faculties, five powers, seven factors of complete awakening, eight noble path factors, four kinds of practice, four traces, śamatha, vipaśyanā, the superior training in morality, the superior training in mind, the superior training in wisdom, liberations, bases of mastery, totality bases, and so forth.

**Manifestation** (samudācāra): namely, the active afflictions. **Sleep** (anuśaya): namely, the latent afflictions. **Connection** (samprayoga): namely, the six sense-bases; one should understand their connection as sharing the same basis. Furthermore, dharmas that can attract and gather other dharmas also mutually connect with each other: one should understand this. Furthermore, the faculties and their domains, the grasper and the grasped, also connect: one should understand this.

**Mutual inclusion of characteristics** (lakṣaṇa-saṃgraha): there are eleven kinds of mutual inclusion: (1) reciprocal inclusion, (2) inclusion by realm, (3) inclusion by characteristic, (4) inclusion by type, (5) inclusion by stage, (6) inclusion by non-separation, (7) inclusion by time, (8) inclusion by direction, (9) partial inclusion, (10) complete inclusion, (11) inclusion by the ultimate meaning.

**Correspondence** (samprayoga): namely, five kinds, one should understand: (1) correspondence with what is of a different nature, not with what is of the same nature, (2) among correspondence with what is of a different nature, correspondence with non-contradicting dharmas, not with contradicting dharmas, (3) among non-contradicting correspondence, similar correspondence of inferior, intermediate, and superior grades, not dissimilar, (4) among similar correspondence of inferior, intermediate, and superior grades, simultaneous correspondence, not non-simultaneous, (5) among simultaneous correspondence, correspondence on the same ground, not on different grounds.

**Teaching** (deśanā): namely, four kinds of verbal expression: (1) verbal expression of what is seen, (2) verbal expression of what is heard, (3) verbal expression of what is sensed, (4) verbal expression of what is known. **Upholding** (dhāraṇā): namely, four kinds of nutriment: (1) material nutriment, (2) contact nutriment, (3) mental intention nutriment, (4) consciousness nutriment. **Sequence** (krama): namely, five kinds of sequence: (1) the sequence of cyclic existence, (2) the sequence of accomplishing tasks, (3) the sequence of teaching, (4) the sequence of arising, (5) the sequence of direct realization.

**Task** (kṛtya): namely, eight kinds of task: (1) the cessation of the basis, (2) the transformation of the basis, (3) comprehensive knowledge of the object, (4) delight in the object, (5) attainment of the fruit, (6) dispassion, (7) transformation of the faculties, (8) generation of supernatural powers. **Object** (ālambana): namely, four kinds of object: (1) the pervading object, (2) the object that purifies conduct, (3) the object of skillful knowledge, (4) the object that purifies afflictions.

**Yoga**: this is either of four kinds or of nine kinds. The four kinds are: (1) faith, (2) aspiration, (3) right exertion, (4) skillful means. The nine kinds are: (1) the worldly path, (2) the supramundane path, (3) the preparatory path, (4) the uninterrupted path, (5) the path of liberation, (6) the path of progress, (7) the inferior path, (8) the intermediate path, (9) the superior path.

**Śamatha**: namely, the nine kinds of mental abiding. **Vipaśyanā**: namely, contemplation of three kinds of objects, or four kinds of contemplation, or contemplation of the six kinds of differentiated objects: one should understand accordingly. The three kinds of object-contemplation are: (1) contemplation with characteristics, (2) investigative contemplation, (3) analytical contemplation. The four kinds are: (1) contemplation of discriminating dharmas, (2) contemplation of thoroughly discriminating dharmas, (3) contemplation of comprehensive assessment, (4) contemplation of comprehensive analysis. The six kinds of differentiated object-contemplation are: (1) contemplation of meaning-objects, (2) contemplation of thing-objects, (3) contemplation of characteristic-objects, (4) contemplation of part-objects, (5) contemplation of time-objects, (6) contemplation of principle-objects.

**Mental attentions** (manaskāra): namely, seven kinds of mental attention: (1) the mental attention of discerning characteristics, (2) the mental attention of resolve, (3) the mental attention of separation, (4) the mental attention of gathering delight, (5) the mental attention of examination, (6) the mental attention of the ultimate skillful means, (7) the mental attention of the fruit of the ultimate skillful means.

**Instructions** (avavāda): namely, five kinds of instruction: (1) instruction through teaching, (2) instruction through realization, (3) instruction in due order, (4) non-inverted instruction, (5) instruction through supernatural transformation.

**Qualities** (guṇa): namely, the meritorious quality dharmas such as the immeasurables, liberations, and so forth as previously explained. **Awakening** (bodhi): namely, three kinds of awakening: (1) śrāvaka awakening, (2) pratyekabuddha awakening, (3) unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening. **Sacred teaching** (śāsana): namely, conferring the taking of refuge, establishing the training rules, establishing listeners, establishing the relationship of teacher and student, teaching on giving, teaching on morality, teaching on rebirth in the heavens, criticizing the taste of sensual desire and displaying the faults of sensual desire, revealing dharmas of defilement and purity, guiding toward liberation and praising meritorious qualities, and extensively teaching all dharmas belonging to the purified portion.

Furthermore, the verse states:

> If one wishes to correctly cultivate and practice
> The meritorious qualities of comprehensive knowledge and the rest,
> It is through the ten kinds of dharma-practice
> And the six kinds of approach.

**The treatise states:** If practitioners wish to correctly cultivate and practice the above nine topics, achieving the meritorious qualities of comprehensive knowledge, permanent severance, direct witness, and cultivation, it is through the ten kinds of dharma-practice and the six kinds of approach that they should train. The ten kinds of dharma-practice are as previously explained. The six kinds of approach are: (1) the approach of true meaning, (2) the approach of realization, (3) the approach of instruction, (4) the approach of departing from two extremes, (5) the approach of the inconceivable, (6) the approach of intention.

---

## Fascicle 5

### Chapter on Gathering the Meaning of Purity, Part One

Having thus explained the nine topics, we shall now explain the meaning of purity. The verse states:

> Among all treatises, the supreme treatise,
> Also well entering yoga,
> The meaning of purity should be known
> Through possessing four qualities of purity.

**The treatise states:** This *Compendium Elucidating the Sacred Teaching*, among all treatises, is the most supreme. What are these treatises? Treatises are briefly of four kinds: (1) treatises resembling the correct Dharma, which rely on sacred teaching to display dharma-characteristics in an inverted manner; (2) external medical treatises, namely external medical prescriptions; (3) disputational treatises, namely the false speculations of non-Buddhist teachers; (4) deceitful treatises, namely the evil spells and incantations of the brāhmaṇas. How is this treatise able to surpass all treatises? Because this treatise is able to display the non-inverted characteristics of all dharmas, is ultimately able to cure the inner disease of the mind, and counteracts disputation and evil spells. Furthermore, in this treatise four kinds of discourse can be found that cannot be found in other treatises; therefore it is the most supreme. What are the four? (1) The discourse that is not of two extremes, (2) the discourse that is not one-sided, (3) the discourse of comprehensive knowledge through severing all grasping, (4) the discourse establishing correct characteristics.

The **discourse that is not of two extremes** means: neither existent nor non-existent, neither different nor not-different, neither self nor non-self, neither permanent nor annihilated, and such discourses. The **discourse that is not one-sided** means: it is not the case that all pleasures should be pursued, namely those that bring about what is without benefit; it is not the case that all pleasures should not be pursued, namely those that bring about what is beneficial. As with pleasure, so too with suffering. And such discourses. The **discourse of comprehensive knowledge through severing all grasping** means: the discourse of comprehensive knowledge through severing the grasping of desire (kāmopādāna), the grasping of views (dṛṣṭyupādāna), the grasping of rules and rites (śīlavrataparāmarśa), and the grasping of a doctrine of self (ātmavādopādāna). The **discourse establishing correct characteristics** means: the non-inverted designation of the specific and shared characteristics of all dharmas, their causal and resultant characteristics, and such discourses.

**Also well entering yoga** means: there are four kinds of yoga: (1) faith, (2) aspiration, (3) right exertion, (4) skillful means. Because this treatise accords well with those, it is called "well entering yoga."

**The meaning of purity should be known** means: one should know that the second name is established as displaying the distinctive quality.

**Through possessing four qualities of purity** means: this intends to show that this name is established in accord with the meaning. How does this treatise possess four qualities of purity? The verse states:

> Because it gathers all meanings;
> Because it is externally indestructible;
> Because it is easy to enter; and once entered,
> Because its practice does not fail or deteriorate.

**The treatise states:** **Because it gathers all meanings** means: because this treatise gathers all meanings, namely the nine kinds of meaning, or the ten kinds, or the five kinds, or the four kinds, or the three kinds. The nine kinds of meaning have already been explained in the Compendium of Topics chapter.

The **ten kinds of meaning** are: (1) the meaning of everything knowable, (2) the meaning of things as they are known, (3) the meaning of the grasper, (4) the meaning of the grasped, (5) the meaning of the basis of abiding, (6) the meaning of what is enjoyed, (7) the meaning of inversion, (8) the meaning of non-inversion, (9) the meaning of defilement, (10) the meaning of purity. Herein, **the meaning of everything knowable** means: among defiled and pure dharmas, exhausting all kinds of differentiated limits. This is called the meaning of everything knowable; for example, the five enumerated aggregates, the six enumerated internal sense-bases, and so forth. **The meaning of things as they are known** means: the true, real nature within defiled and pure dharmas. This is called the meaning of things as they are known. This is again of seven kinds, namely from the suchness of cyclic existence through to the suchness of right conduct. **The meaning of the grasper** means: the five internal form-bases, mind, mentation, consciousness, and all mental factors. **The meaning of the grasped** means: the six external sense-bases. Furthermore, the meaning of the grasper is also the grasped. **The meaning of the basis of abiding** means: the external world-system; relying on this abode, the sentient-being realm can be found, namely: villages, hundreds of villages, thousands of villages, hundreds of thousands of villages, and so forth extensively explained through to the three-thousand great-thousand world-system, through to immeasurable, countless world-systems in the ten directions, as numerous as the atoms of countless hundreds of thousands of world-systems. **The meaning of what is enjoyed** means: the material possessions that are gathered and received. **The meaning of inversion** means: within the meanings of the grasper and others, the inversion of the perception, the mental inversion, and the view-inversion of conceiving permanence in what is impermanent; likewise through to the inversion of the perception, the mental inversion, and the view-inversion of conceiving a self in what is without self. **The meaning of non-inversion** means: the counteraction of the inversions described above: one should understand accordingly. **The meaning of defilement** means three kinds: within the three realms, the defilement of afflictions, the defilement of karma, and the defilement of birth. **The meaning of purity** means: for the purpose of realizing the liberation from the three kinds of defilement, all the factors of awakening that are cultivated. These ten kinds of meaning gather all meanings: one should understand this.

The **five kinds of meaning** are: (1) the object of comprehensive knowledge, (2) the meaning to be comprehensively known, (3) the comprehensive knowledge to be achieved, (4) attaining the fruit of comprehensive knowledge, (5) enjoying the fruit of comprehensive knowledge. **The object of comprehensive knowledge** means: all knowable objects, whether objects of the aggregates, objects of the internal sense-bases, objects of the external sense-bases, and so forth. **The meaning to be comprehensively known** means: exhausting all kinds as they should be known, whether the conventional truth or the ultimate truth, whether meritorious qualities or faults, whether the various conditions or the three times, whether the characteristics of arising, abiding, and destruction, whether as like a disease and so forth, whether suffering and its origin, whether suchness, the true limit, and the dharma-nature, whether extensive or brief, whether with a definitive answer, an analytical answer, a counter-question, or a setting-aside; whether concealed or revealed; such and other meanings to be comprehensively known: one should understand this. **Comprehensive knowledge** means: the factors of awakening that are able to take the preceding two domains as objects. **Attaining the fruit of comprehensive knowledge** means: the permanent cessation of desire, aversion, and delusion, the four fruits of the ascetic achieved through the severance of all remaining desire, aversion, and delusion without residue, together with the complete fulfillment and bearing witness of the shared and exclusive, worldly and supramundane meritorious qualities of śrāvakas, pratyekabuddhas, and tathāgatas. **Enjoying the fruit of comprehensive knowledge** means: the knowledge of liberation within the dharmas that have been realized, together with extensively opening, displaying, expounding, and discriminating them for others. These five kinds of meaning also gather all meanings: one should understand this.

The **four kinds of meaning** are: (1) the meaning of what the mind grasps, (2) the meaning of experience, (3) the meaning of cognition, (4) the meaning of defilement and purity. These four kinds of meaning also gather all meanings: one should understand this.

The **three kinds of meaning** are: (1) the meaning of words, (2) the meaning of meaning, (3) the meaning of realms. **The meaning of words** means: name-bodies and so forth. **The meaning of meaning** means ten kinds of meaning, one should understand: (1) the characteristic of the real, (2) the characteristic of comprehensive knowledge, (3) the characteristic of permanent severance, (4) the characteristic of direct witness, (5) the characteristic of cultivation, (6) various differentiated characteristics within the real and other characteristics, (7) the characteristic of the connection between basis and dependent, (8) the characteristic of dharmas that obstruct comprehensive knowledge and so forth, (9) the characteristic of dharmas that are conducive to comprehensive knowledge and so forth, (10) the characteristic of the faults and meritorious qualities of non-comprehensive-knowledge and comprehensive knowledge. **The meaning of realms** means five kinds of realm: (1) the physical world-system, (2) the sentient-being world-system, (3) the dharma-realm, (4) the realm of those to be disciplined, (5) the realm of means of discipline. These three kinds of meaning also gather all meanings: one should understand this.

**Because it is externally indestructible** means: all the above meanings cannot be destroyed by any non-Buddhist teacher, because they constitute correct and principled discourse that cannot be subdued. Non-Buddhist teachers are briefly of five kinds: (1) those who assert a self, (2) those who assert permanence, (3) those who assert annihilation, (4) those who assert nirvāṇa in the present life, (5) those who assert no cause.

**Because it is easy to enter** means: because in this treatise the words are complete and clear, its meaning is easy to enter. It is not like the evil spell-treatises of the brāhmaṇas, whose words are deficient and obscure, making their meaning difficult to enter.

**Because its practice does not fail or deteriorate once entered** means: because in this treatise the meaning is complete and correct, whoever has entered it and practices in accord with the teaching does not lose one's own benefit. It is not like the false treatises of the non-Buddhists: those who have entered them, even though they practice in accord with the teaching, are empty of personal benefit.
