Thus, because this treatise possesses the purity of gathering meanings, the purity of being indestructible, the purity of being easy to enter, and the purity of practice, it is called "the meaning of purity": one should understand this. What dharma taught by the Blessed One does this treatise now elucidate? The verse states:

> The buddhas teach the wondrous Dharma
> Correctly based on the two truths:
> The first is called conventional,
> The second is called ultimate.

**The treatise states:** The characteristics of the two truths, conventional and ultimate, have already been explained above. Furthermore, the verse states:

> First, self, dharmas, and function are taught;
> They are taught in accordance with the other.
> Seven kinds and four kinds:
> Suchness is called the ultimate.

**The treatise states:** The first, the **conventional truth** (saṃvṛti-satya), teaches self, teaches dharmas, and teaches function. Teaching self means: teaching sentient beings, life-possessors, those born, persons (pudgala), humans, gods, men, women, friends of the Buddha, friends of the Dharma, and so forth. Teaching dharmas means: teaching form, feeling, and so forth. Teaching function means: teaching the ability to see, the ability to hear, the ability to arise, the ability to cease, and so forth. This is what should be understood as the conventional truth. Although these are not ultimately real, they are spoken of as existent based on convention. Question: If the conventional truth does not exist by virtue of the ultimate, for what purpose is it taught? Answer: It is taught in accordance with the other. That is, the conventional truth is taught for the purpose of according with the ultimate truth. Question: What is the ultimate truth? Answer: Seven kinds and four kinds of suchness are called the ultimate, as explained in the Compendium of Topics chapter.

Having thus briefly explained the two truths, the Tathāgata further explains them extensively herein. The verse states:

> Inherent nature, meaning, establishment,
> Number, sequence, and skillful knowledge,
> And the differentiation of concepts:
> These reveal the conventional meaning of the aggregates.

**The treatise states:** **Inherent nature** means: transformation and deterioration, experience, cognition and designation, fabrication, and discernment. These are the inherent natures of the aggregates.

**Meaning** means: the meaning of "aggregate" is accumulation. This accumulation-meaning has four kinds, as shall be explained in the chapter on accomplishing skillful knowledge.

**Establishment** means: regarding the form aggregate, there are four kinds of establishment: (1) establishment by characteristics, (2) establishment by arising, (3) establishment by diminution, (4) establishment by differentiation.

**Establishment by characteristics**: the characteristics of the form aggregate are briefly of five kinds: (1) specific characteristics, (2) shared characteristics, (3) the characteristic of the connection between basis and dependent, (4) the characteristic of enjoyment, (5) the characteristic of function. **Specific characteristics** means: solidity and such are the characteristics of earth and the others; individual purity is the characteristic of the eye and the others. **Shared characteristics** means: the characteristic of all form being subject to transformation and deterioration. **The characteristic of the connection between basis and dependent** means: the great elements are the basis, and derived forms are the dependent. **The characteristic of enjoyment** means: through the predominant power of the internal sense-bases' enjoyment, the individually differentiated objects of external form are produced. Some form-aggregations arise with only solidity, some with only moisture, some with only heat, some with only movement, or they arise in combinations, because they accord with the enjoyment of the internal sense-bases. **The characteristic of function** means: as the great elements of earth and others have the characteristics of supporting, gathering, ripening, and developing; and so forth.

**Establishment by arising**: there are five kinds of arising: (1) arising through the basis, (2) arising through seeds, (3) arising through the impetus of force, (4) arising through favorable conditions, (5) arising through adverse conditions. **Arising through the basis** means: relying on the great elements, at the very locus of the great elements, other derived forms arise. For this reason, it is said that the four great elements "create." The meaning of "created" is that derived forms are included in the same location. **Arising through seeds** means: arising from one's own seeds. For example, a solid aggregation may, encountering conditions, transform and produce fluidity; or a fluid aggregation may transform and produce solidity; or what is not warm may transform and produce heat; or what is warm may transform and produce cold; or from stillness may arise movement; or from movement may arise stillness. Likewise, beautiful forms and ugly forms and other such mutually generated differentiations: one should understand this. If one considers the specific characteristics, they are mutually absent; if one considers the seeds, they are mutually present. Therefore, from such-and-such an aggregation, such-and-such differentiated form-dharmas arise. Such types are called arising through seeds: one should understand this. **Arising through the impetus of force** means: through the predominant power of the internal form-faculties, external objects arise in constant, continuous manifestation, such as the physical world-system. Furthermore, through the impetus of prior karma, the internal sense-bases arise; if there is a manifest desire, the material possessions of the gods and of the people of the Northern Continent mostly come into being through arising by the impetus of force: one should understand this regarding transmigration. As for the physical world-system of the human realm, only its constant continuity and such types are arising through the impetus of force: one should understand this. **Arising through favorable conditions** means: when one's own favorable conditions are obtained, such-and-such form-dharmas mutually nourish, mutually increase, and arise in a superior manner, just as water and other things moisten sprouts and so forth. Such types are called arising through favorable conditions. **Arising through adverse conditions** is the opposite of this: one should understand accordingly.

**Establishment by diminution** means: the establishment of atoms (paramāṇu). Furthermore, atoms are established through five kinds of establishment: one should understand this. (1) Through analysis, (2) through differentiation, (3) through singularity, (4) through companions, (5) through the nature of being without parts. **Through analysis** means: through the analysis of wisdom, one gradually reduces coarse form-dharmas to the most subtle limit and establishes atoms; this is not through their substance. For this reason, it is said that atoms have no arising and no cessation, and furthermore it is not said that atoms are assembled to form coarse form. **Through differentiation** means: there are briefly fifteen kinds of atoms: five kinds of atoms of the faculties such as the eye, five kinds of atoms of objects such as form, four kinds of atoms of the elements such as earth, and one kind of atom of real form included in the dharma-base. **Through singularity** means: establishing the specific characteristic of the actual atom. **Through companions** means: establishing the aggregate-atom, because at the locus of a single atom of earth and so forth, other form-dharmas are present in the same location and inseparable; therefore aggregate-atoms are established. **Through the nature of being without parts** means: a single atom does not have further subtle parts, because it is not an aggregate. If it were an aggregate-atom, it could have multiple parts. But the location occupied by a single atom cannot be further analyzed to establish additional parts. Therefore, atoms have no subtle parts.

**Establishment by differentiation** means: there are twenty-six kinds of form. (1) Form bound to the desire realm, which possesses all forms. (2) Form bound to the form realm, which excludes odor and taste. (3) Form bound to the formless realm, namely form arising from mastery in concentration, which is not karmic-retribution form. (4) Form of the pure realm, namely form produced through the predominance of supramundane dharmas, such as the forms of the dhyānas and liberations, and the forms of buddhas and bodhisattvas. (5) Internal form, namely the faculties and the form of the locations where the faculties reside. (6) External form, namely, excluding the faculties and the locations where they reside, the remaining form, sound, odor, taste, and tangibles. (7) Basis-form, namely the five faculties such as the eye. (8) Object-form, namely the five sense-objects and form included in the dharma-base. (9) Grasper-form, namely the basis-form itself. (10) Grasped-form, namely the object-form itself. (11) Appropriated form, namely that which serves as the basis for the arising of feeling, such as the form-faculties and the form of the locations where they reside, because it is the location where form, mind, and mental factors reside. Sharing the same benefit and harm is the meaning of appropriation. (12) Non-appropriated form, namely all remaining form besides this. (13) Same-class form, namely the form of the faculty that is exclusive to one's own consciousness, because it turns together with consciousness sharing the same object. (14) Other-class form, namely the form of the faculty shared with one's own consciousness, because it only turns in similarity within the continuity of one's own class. (15) Visible and resistant form, namely the form of the form-base. (16) Invisible but resistant form, namely the form of the remaining nine bases. (17) Invisible and non-resistant form, namely form included in the dharma-base. (18) Pure form, namely the five internal bases. (19) Form apprehended by what is pure, namely the five external bases. (20) Form apprehended by the mind, namely form included in the dharma-base. (21) Form serving as the basis of abiding, namely from the wind-circle through to the great earth. (22) Sheltering form, namely dwellings and so forth. (23) Material-possession form, namely ten kinds of material possessions: (i) food and drink, (ii) clothing and adornments, (iii) implements, (iv) amusements, (v) drums and dance, (vi) songs, (vii) music, (viii) incense, garlands, and cosmetics, (ix) various kinds of light, (x) service by men and women. (24) Form of the locations where the faculties reside, namely the locations where the five form-faculties reside. (25) Faculty-form, namely the five form-faculties. (26) Form that is the domain of concentration, as already explained in the Compendium of Topics chapter.

**The establishment of the feeling aggregate** has six kinds: (1) establishment by differentiation, (2) establishment by liberation, (3) establishment by observation, (4) establishment by arising, (5) establishment by characteristics, (6) establishment by teacher-sentences.

**Establishment by differentiation**: one may establish one feeling, as it is said: "All feelings whatsoever are suffering." Or one may establish two feelings: bodily feeling and mental feeling. Or three feelings: pleasant feeling, painful feeling, and neither-pleasant-nor-painful feeling. Or four feelings: feeling bound to the desire realm, bound to the form realm, bound to the formless realm, and unbound feeling. Or five feelings: the pleasure-faculty, the pain-faculty, the joy-faculty, the sorrow-faculty, and the equanimity-faculty. Or six feelings: feeling born of eye-contact through to feeling born of mental contact. Or eighteen feelings: the eighteen mental approaches: six approaches of joy, six approaches of sorrow, and six approaches of equanimity. Or thirty-six feelings: six joys based on indulgence and six joys based on renunciation; as with joy, so too with sorrow and equanimity. Or one hundred and eight feelings: the thirty-six feelings each based on the three times. Or one may open up immeasurable feelings, as whatever feelings are experienced give rise to countless feelings.

**Establishment by liberation**: the first dhyāna provides liberation from the sorrow-faculty; the second dhyāna provides liberation from the pain-faculty; the third dhyāna provides liberation from the joy-faculty; the fourth dhyāna provides liberation from the pleasure-faculty; the concentration of the signless mental dharmas provides liberation from the equanimity-faculty.

**Establishment by observation**: eight kinds of observation regarding feeling: What is feeling? What is the origin of feeling? What is the cessation of feeling? What is the practice leading to the origin of feeling? What is the practice leading to the cessation of feeling? What is the gratification of feeling? What is the danger of feeling? What is the escape from feeling?

**Establishment by arising**: feelings arise from sixteen kinds of contact. What are the sixteen? (1) Eye-contact, (2) ear-contact, (3) nose-contact, (4) tongue-contact, (5) body-contact, (6) mental contact, (7) resistant contact, (8) designative contact, (9) contact conducive to pleasant feeling, (10) contact conducive to painful feeling, (11) contact conducive to neither-pleasant-nor-painful feeling, (12) contact of desire, (13) contact of aversion, (14) contact of knowledge, (15) contact of ignorance, (16) contact of neither knowledge nor ignorance.

**Establishment by characteristics**: eight kinds of characteristics: (1) the characteristic of karmic retribution, (2) the characteristic of non-karmic-retribution, (3) the characteristic of having relish, (4) the characteristic of being without relish, (5) the characteristic of being based on indulgence, (6) the characteristic of being based on renunciation, (7) the characteristic of movement, (8) the characteristic of abiding. **The characteristic of karmic retribution** means: feeling associated with the ālaya-vijñāna. **The characteristic of non-karmic-retribution** means: feeling associated with the active consciousnesses. **The characteristic of having relish** means: feeling bound to the desire realm. **The characteristic of being without relish** means: feeling bound to the form and formless realms, and unbound feeling. **The characteristic of being based on indulgence** means: feeling associated with sensual desire. **The characteristic of being based on renunciation** means: feeling associated with the indefinite-ground wholesome dharmas induced by renunciation. **The characteristic of movement** means: feeling displayed in the sūtras through the simile of wind, because its continuity does not abide for long. **The characteristic of abiding** means: feeling displayed in the sūtras through the simile of a guesthouse, because its continuity abides only temporarily.

**Establishment by teacher-sentences**: the thirty-six teacher-sentences: six joys based on indulgence, six joys based on renunciation, six sorrows based on indulgence, six sorrows based on renunciation, six equanimities based on indulgence, and six equanimities based on renunciation.

What is **joy based on indulgence**? Regarding forms cognized by the eye that are pleasing, delightful, agreeable, and lovable, that are capable of arousing desire and deep attachment, whether through obtaining what is presently obtained or through recollecting what was previously obtained, joy arises. Joy of such characteristics is called joy based on indulgence.

What is **joy based on renunciation**? When, regarding those same forms, one has understood them to be impermanent, suffering, subject to change, subject to dispassion, cessation, quiescence, and disappearance, and when one has understood all forms, past and present, to be dharmas of impermanence, suffering, change, and so forth, and joy arises, joy of such characteristics is called joy based on renunciation.

Likewise regarding sounds cognized by the ear, odors cognized by the nose, tastes cognized by the tongue, tangibles cognized by the body, and dharmas cognized by the mind that are pleasing, delightful, agreeable, and lovable, that are capable of arousing desire and deep attachment, whether through obtaining what is presently obtained or through recollecting what was previously obtained, joy arises: joy of such characteristics is called joy based on indulgence. Likewise, when regarding sounds cognized by the ear through to dharmas cognized by the mind, one has understood them to be impermanent, suffering, subject to change, dispassion, cessation, quiescence, and disappearance, and when one has understood all dharmas, past and present, to be dharmas of impermanence, suffering, change, and so forth, and joy arises: joy of such characteristics is called joy based on renunciation.

What is **sorrow based on indulgence**? Regarding forms cognized by the eye that are pleasing, delightful, through to deeply attachable, whether through not obtaining what is presently obtained, or through recollecting what was previously obtained, if it has already passed, if it is exhausted, if it has ceased, if it has departed, if it has changed, and sorrow arises: sorrow of such characteristics is called sorrow based on indulgence.

What is **sorrow based on renunciation**? When, regarding those same forms, one has understood them to be impermanent through to to have disappeared, and when one has understood all forms, past and present, to be dharmas of impermanence, suffering, change, and so forth, and one generates the aspiration and vow to realize the supreme liberation, thinking: "When will I dwell in the complete fulfillment as the noble ones dwell in their complete fulfillment?" Such sorrow of apprehension and concern generated from the aspiration and vow to realize the supreme liberation is called sorrow based on renunciation. Likewise regarding sounds cognized by the ear through to dharmas cognized by the mind that are pleasing, delightful, through to deeply attachable, whether through not obtaining what is presently obtained, or through recollecting what was previously obtained, if it has already passed through to if it has changed, and sorrow arises: sorrow of such characteristics is called sorrow based on indulgence.

Likewise, when regarding sounds cognized by the ear through to dharmas cognized by the mind, one has understood them to be impermanent through to to have disappeared, and when one has understood all dharmas, past and present, to be dharmas of impermanence, suffering, change, and so forth, and one generates the aspiration and vow to realize the supreme liberation, thinking: "When will I dwell in the complete fulfillment as the noble ones dwell in their complete fulfillment?" Such sorrow of apprehension and concern generated from the aspiration and vow to realize the supreme liberation is called sorrow based on renunciation.

What is **equanimity based on indulgence**? A deluded, unknowing, unlearned ordinary being, regarding forms cognized by the eye, with longing attachment to equanimity, grasping and pursuing various actions, relying on form, depending on form, not relinquishing form, not transcending form: the equanimity herein is called equanimity based on indulgence.

What is **equanimity based on renunciation**? When, regarding those same forms, one has understood them to be impermanent through to to have disappeared, and when one has understood all forms, past and present, to be dharmas of impermanence, suffering, change, and so forth, one discriminatingly cultivates equanimity: this is called equanimity based on renunciation. Likewise, a deluded, unknowing, unlearned ordinary being, regarding sounds cognized by the ear through to dharmas cognized by the mind, with longing attachment to equanimity, grasping and pursuing various actions, relying on dharmas through to not transcending dharmas: the equanimity herein is called equanimity based on indulgence.

Likewise, when regarding sounds cognized by the ear through to dharmas cognized by the mind, one has understood them to be impermanent through to to have disappeared, and when one has understood all dharmas, past and present, to be dharmas of impermanence, suffering, change, and so forth, one discriminatingly cultivates equanimity: this is called equanimity based on renunciation.

Herein, the six joys based on indulgence, six joys based on renunciation, six sorrows based on indulgence, six sorrows based on renunciation, six equanimities based on indulgence, and six equanimities based on renunciation are collectively gathered as the thirty-six teacher-sentences.

Herein, by relying on and abiding in the six joys based on renunciation, one relinquishes, expels, and eliminates the six joys based on indulgence. Likewise, by relying on and abiding in the six sorrows based on renunciation, one relinquishes, expels, and eliminates the six sorrows based on indulgence. Likewise, by relying on and abiding in the six equanimities based on renunciation, one relinquishes, expels, and eliminates the six equanimities based on indulgence. Furthermore herein, by relying on and abiding in the six joys based on renunciation, one relinquishes, expels, and eliminates the six sorrows based on renunciation. By relying on and abiding in the six equanimities based on renunciation, one relinquishes, expels, and eliminates the six joys based on renunciation. Furthermore, there are two kinds of equanimity: (1) equanimity based on variety, (2) equanimity based on unity. **Equanimity based on variety** means: equanimity depending on form through to depending on dharmas. **Equanimity based on unity** means: equanimity depending on the station of boundless space through to depending on the station of neither-perception-nor-non-perception. Herein, by relying on and abiding in equanimity based on unity, one relinquishes, expels, and eliminates equanimity based on variety.

**The establishment of the perception aggregate** has three kinds: (1) differentiation by basis, (2) differentiation by mental attention, (3) differentiation by domain.

**Differentiation by basis** means: six perceptions, namely perception born of eye-contact through to perception born of mental contact.

**Differentiation by mental attention** is of two kinds: (1) perception with characteristics, (2) perception without characteristics. **Perception with characteristics** means: excluding the perception of those in the desire realm who have not yet learned to speak well, the perception of the pinnacle of existence, and supramundane perception, all remaining perception associated with mental attention that has characteristics. **Perception without characteristics** means: the perceptions excluded above, associated with mental attention without characteristics.

**Differentiation by domain** has four kinds: (1) small perception, (2) great perception, (3) immeasurable perception, (4) perception of nothingness. These correspond in order to: perception taking the desire realm as object, perception taking the form realm as object, perception taking the formless realm as object, and perception of the station of nothingness: one should understand accordingly.

**The establishment of the formation aggregate** has three kinds: (1) differentiation by what is predominant, (2) differentiation by basis, (3) differentiation by the designation of formations.

**Differentiation by what is predominant**: volition (cetanā) alone is the most predominant, included within the formation aggregate, because through the fabricating mind it causes defiled and pure dharmas to turn.

**Differentiation by basis** means: six volitions, namely volition born of eye-contact through to volition born of mental contact.

**Differentiation by the designation of formations** has three kinds: (1) the designation of defilement, (2) the designation of purity, (3) the designation of positions. **The designation of defilement** means: the afflictions and subsidiary afflictions. **The designation of purity** means: faith and others. **The designation of positions** means: birth and other formations dissociated from mind. These three aggregates, together with form included in the dharma-base and the unconditioned, are collectively called the dharma-realm, also called the dharma-base.

**The establishment of the consciousness aggregate** has three kinds: (1) differentiation by type, (2) differentiation by basis, (3) differentiation by defilement and purity.

**Differentiation by type** is of two kinds: (1) the ālaya-vijñāna (storehouse consciousness), (2) the active consciousnesses (pravṛtti-vijñāna). **Differentiation by basis** means: the six consciousness-bodies. Question: Among the six consciousnesses, in which is the ālaya-vijñāna included? Answer: It is included in all six, because it stores the seeds of all of them. Because this consciousness is secretly noted and gathered, the Blessed One does not teach it to everyone; those who are skillful can understand it on their own through this. **Differentiation by defilement and purity** means: as it is said in the sūtras, "A mind with desire one knows in accord with reality as a mind with desire; a mind free from desire one knows in accord with reality as a mind free from desire. Likewise, a mind with aversion, free from aversion, with delusion, free from delusion," and so forth extensively.

Herein there are three grades of mind: (1) the mind that has not yet set out toward concentration, (2) the mind that has set out but not yet attained concentration, (3) the mind that has attained concentration. This last is further of two kinds: (1) the impure mind, (2) the extremely pure mind. **The first grade of mind** means: sometimes a defiled mind arises, because it is bound by afflictions such as desire; sometimes a wholesome or indeterminate mind arises, because it is separated from afflictions such as desire. **The second grade of mind** means: sometimes one binds the mind inwardly in stillness; sometimes, losing mindfulness, one is scattered among the five desires; sometimes, restraining it to make it still, it is further obscured by torpor and sleepiness; sometimes, in order to sever that, one further stimulates the mind and places it upon a superior, pure object; sometimes, because of improperly placing it upon that object, the mind becomes agitated; or because of properly placing it, the mind is not agitated; sometimes, because one has not yet severed the hindrances of sinking and agitation, the mind is not quiescent in either aspect; or because one has severed them, the mind becomes quiescent. Because one can attain the mental attention of the fundamental dhyāna, it is called a concentrated mind; because one has not attained it, it is called an unconcentrated mind. Because the path is brought to completion, it is called the extremely cultivated mind; because severance is brought to completion, it is called the extremely liberated mind. The opposites of these two are called the uncultivated mind and the unliberated mind. From the concentrated mind through to here: this is the third grade of mind, one should understand.

**Number** means: because through five kinds of affair, all affairs of cyclic existence are comprehensively gathered. What are the five affairs? (1) The affair of what is enjoyed, (2) the affair of the enjoyer, (3) the affair of the grasping involved in enjoyment, (4) the affair of the defilement involved in enjoyment, (5) the affair of the pervading dharma that serves as the basis for those. **What is enjoyed** means: the form aggregate; through the gate of the five form-faculties being the basis and appropriation, it serves as what is enjoyed; through the gate of the form sense-objects being the objects, it serves as what is enjoyed. **The enjoyer** means: the feeling aggregate. **The grasping involved in enjoyment** means: the perception aggregate. **The defilement involved in enjoyment** means: the formation aggregate. **The pervading dharma that serves as the basis for those** means: the consciousness aggregate. Because the view of this consciousness as the enjoyer, through to as that which is defiled, is maintained, by exactly this meaning, the sequence is established. Furthermore, through five additional reasons, the sequence is established: (1) through arising, (2) through counteraction, (3) through cyclic existence, (4) through the stations of consciousness, (5) through manifestation.

**Through arising** means: as the sūtra says, "Conditioned by the eye and forms, eye-consciousness arises; through to: conditioned by the mind and dharmas, mental consciousness arises." Herein, form is explained first, then consciousness, because these two aggregates serve as the basis for all mental factors; they arise in dependence on these. The sūtra then says: "When three things come together, there is contact; conditioned by contact, feeling and other dharmas arise." Through this reason of arising, the sequence of the aggregates is explained.

**Through counteraction** means: in order to counteract the four inversions, the four foundations of mindfulness are taught. The four inversions are: (1) the inversion of conceiving purity in what is impure, (2) the inversion of conceiving pleasure in what is suffering, (3) the inversion of conceiving permanence in what is impermanent, (4) the inversion of conceiving a self in what is without self. Herein, the form aggregate is explained first, then the feeling aggregate, then the consciousness aggregate, and finally the perception and formation aggregates together. In this way, through counteraction, the sequence of the aggregates is explained.

**Through cyclic existence** means: the sense-faculties and sense-objects serve as the basis for the arising of two aggregates: experiencing the objects and painting the objects. Through this causal condition, one can give rise to the dharmas of disturbance that involve the enjoyment of present-life objects, and one can create wholesome and unwholesome karma. Through this cause, one can bring about the suffering and distress of birth and so forth in future lives. The consciousness aggregate alone is what is disturbed; therefore it is explained last.

**Through the stations of consciousness** means: the four stations of consciousness; through their sequence, the consciousness that can abide is produced.

**Through manifestation** means: when one has seen a person, one first notes their form; therefore the form aggregate is explained first. Next, through the feeling aggregate, one manifests whether they are noble or base, suffering or happy. Next, through the perception aggregate, one manifests their name, clan, and type. Next, through the formation aggregate, one manifests whether they are foolish or wise. Last, through the consciousness aggregate, one manifests the differentiation of their inner self: namely, among the preceding aggregates, the one who is noted, who has suffering and happiness, who is spoken of, who is foolish or wise, is named. Through manifestation, the sequence of the aggregates is explained.

Furthermore, through reliance on two kinds of affairs, the sequence is established: (1) affairs that assist the self, (2) the affair of the inner self. First, relying on the body and the various sense-objects, one then experiences suffering and happiness, then accordingly speaks of the names, clans, and types of self and others, then based on these two, all wholesome and unwholesome conduct is amassed: such and similar are called affairs that assist the self. The last aggregate is the affair of the inner self: one should understand this.

**Skillful knowledge** shall be extensively explained in the chapter on accomplishing skillful knowledge.

**Differentiation of concepts** means: having form and being formless, visible and invisible, resistant and non-resistant, with outflows and without outflows, conditioned and unconditioned, and so forth. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are of form? Answer: By the meaning of having the substance of form, one aggregate is of form. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are visible? Answer: By the meaning of being the domain of the eye, a small portion of one aggregate is visible. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are resistant? Answer: By the meaning of mutually touching and each occupying a location, and by the meaning of coarseness, a small portion of one aggregate is resistant. "By the meaning of coarseness" means: apart from three kinds of subtlety. What are the three? (1) Subtlety through diminution, (2) subtlety by type, (3) subtlety through the mastery of the mind. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, have outflows? Answer: By the meaning of being followed by heaviness, and by the meaning of mutually arising in dependence on afflictions, a small portion of all aggregates have outflows. Furthermore, there is an additional meaning of having outflows: wherever afflictions are able to give rise to four kinds of fault, that place is called "having outflows." What are the four? (1) The fault of non-quiescence, (2) the fault of internal and external alteration, (3) the fault of initiating misconduct, (4) the fault of gathering causes. Herein, the first fault should be understood as produced by the manifest activity of active afflictions. The second fault should be understood as produced by the basis and conditions of the afflictions, which accord with the afflictions. The third fault should be understood as produced by the afflictions. The fourth fault should be understood as the attraction of subsequent existence. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are conditioned? Answer: By the meaning of having already arisen, presently arising, and potentially arising from causes, all aggregates are conditioned. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are with contention? Answer: By the meaning of aversion predominantly exercising mastery, a small portion of all aggregates is with contention. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are with relish? Answer: By the meaning of desire and views predominantly exercising mastery, a small portion of all aggregates is with relish. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are based on indulgence? Answer: By the meaning of sensual desire predominantly exercising mastery, a small portion of all aggregates is based on indulgence. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are worldly? Answer: By the meaning of serving as the basis for verbal discourse, a small portion of all aggregates is worldly. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are included in the realms? Answer: By the meaning of the worldly within the three realms, a small portion of all aggregates is included in the realms. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are past? Answer: By the meaning of cause and fruit having already been used up and exhausted, a small portion of all aggregates is past. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are future? Answer: By the meaning of cause and fruit not yet having been used up and exhausted, a small portion of all aggregates is future. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are present? Answer: By the meaning of the cause having already been used up but the fruit not yet having been used up, a small portion of all aggregates is present. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are internal? Answer: By the meaning of the six internal bases and what is inseparable from them, four aggregates in full and a small portion of one are internal. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are external? Answer: By the opposite meaning of internal, a small portion of one aggregate is external. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are coarse? Answer: By the meaning of not being smooth, of being aggregated, and of being full in appearance, a small portion of all aggregates is coarse. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are subtle? Answer: By the opposite meaning of coarseness, a small portion of all aggregates is subtle. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are inferior? Answer: By the meaning of impermanence, suffering, impurity, and defilement, a small portion of all aggregates is inferior. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are superior? Answer: By the opposite meaning of inferiority, a small portion of all aggregates is superior. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are distant? Answer: By the meaning of being far away in location and far away in the time of the past and future, a small portion of all aggregates is distant. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are near? Answer: By the opposite meaning of distance, a small portion of all aggregates is near. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are bound to the desire realm? Answer: By the meaning that, having been born therein, one has not yet attained the counteraction, or having attained it has departed from it, manifesting in the three times, a small portion of all aggregates is bound to the desire realm. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are bound to the form realm? Answer: By the meaning that, having attained the counteraction of the form realm, whether abiding in that concentration or born therein, one has not yet attained the counteraction of the higher grounds, or having attained it has departed from it, manifesting in the three times, a small portion of all aggregates is bound to the form realm. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are bound to the formless realm? Answer: By the meaning that, having attained the counteraction of the formless realm, whether abiding in that concentration or born therein, one has not yet attained the counteraction of the higher grounds, or having attained it has departed from it, manifesting in the three times, a small portion of all aggregates is bound to the formless realm. Furthermore, there is an additional meaning: by the meaning of being included in the associated, co-existing dharmas and their resultant dharmas that are concurrent with a concentration and not separated from it, one is bound to the desire realm. By the meaning of being included in afflictions that depend on form and the opposite of the preceding, one is bound to the form realm. By the meaning of being included in the remaining afflictions having already separated from the form realm, and as included in the preceding, one is bound to the formless realm: one should understand accordingly. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are wholesome? Answer: By the meaning of bringing about pleasant resultant fruit in the future, by the meaning of severing afflictions and suffering, and by the meaning of counteracting them, a small portion of all aggregates is wholesome. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are unwholesome? Answer: By the meaning of bringing about painful resultant fruit in the future and by the meaning of initiating misconduct, a small portion of all aggregates is unwholesome. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are indeterminate? Answer: By the meaning of being contrary to both, a small portion of all aggregates is indeterminate. Furthermore, there is an additional meaning: by the meaning of being faultless, of counteracting faults, and of being conducive to meritorious qualities, it is wholesome. By the contrary meaning, it is unwholesome. By the meaning of being contrary to both, it is indeterminate. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are of learning? Answer: By the meaning of skillfully cultivating and training in the wholesome, a small portion of all aggregates is of learning. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are beyond learning? Answer: By the meaning of having completed the cultivation and training in the wholesome, a small portion of all aggregates is beyond learning. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are neither of learning nor beyond learning? Answer: By the meaning of being included in the wholesome, defiled, and indeterminate that remain after excluding the preceding two, a small portion of all aggregates is neither of learning nor beyond learning. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are severed by seeing? Answer: By the meaning of being severed through the direct realization of the truths by the wisdom of direct realization, a small portion of all aggregates is severed by seeing. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are severed by cultivation? Answer: By the meaning of being severed by the path of cultivation after the direct realization of the truths by the wisdom of direct realization, a small portion of all aggregates is severed by cultivation. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are not to be severed? Answer: By the meaning of ultimately counteracting all defilement and the meaning of all defilement being permanently severed, a small portion of all aggregates is not to be severed. Question: By what meaning, and how many aggregates, are formless, and so forth extensively? Answer: By the contrary meaning of form and so forth as previously explained, one should understand the meaning of formless and so forth.

---

## Fascicle 6

### Chapter on Gathering the Meaning of Purity, Part Two

**The treatise states:** Having thus extensively explained the conventional truth, what is the ultimate truth? The verse states:

> The five and three dharmas, and truth;
> That truth is further fourfold, one should know;
> Together with four kinds of investigation
> And four kinds of knowledge in accord with reality.

**The treatise states:** The **five dharmas** are: (1) characteristics (lakṣaṇa), (2) names (nāman), (3) discrimination (vikalpa), (4) suchness (tathatā), (5) correct knowledge (samyag-jñāna). **Characteristics** means, in brief: the basis on which all verbal expressions depend. **Names** means: the designative expressions applied to characteristics. **Discrimination** means: all mental factors included within the three realms. **Suchness** means: what is revealed through the selflessness of dharmas (dharma-nairātmya), the domain of noble knowledge, and not the basis on which any verbal expression depends. **Correct knowledge** is briefly of two kinds: (1) exclusively supramundane, and (2) both worldly and supramundane. **Exclusively supramundane correct knowledge** means: through correct knowledge, śrāvakas, pratyekabuddhas, and bodhisattvas penetrate suchness. Furthermore, bodhisattvas, through both worldly and supramundane knowledge, when diligently studying the five sciences, because the knowledge of all-pervading suchness frequently manifests in present awareness, they swiftly realize the purity of the cognitive obstruction. **Worldly and supramundane correct knowledge** means: after śrāvakas and pratyekabuddhas have initially penetrated suchness, through the power of the initial exclusively supramundane correct knowledge, they subsequently obtain worldly and supramundane correct knowledge. Through this, regarding the established truths, they give rise to the mind of disgust and fear toward the three realms, and to relishing the quiescent state of the three realms. Furthermore, because that correct knowledge frequently manifests in present awareness, they swiftly realize the purity of the afflictive obstruction.

The **three dharmas** means the three self-natures (tri-svabhāva): (1) the imagined self-nature (parikalpita-svabhāva), (2) the dependent self-nature (paratantra-svabhāva), (3) the consummate self-nature (pariniṣpanna-svabhāva). **The imagined self-nature** means: the self-nature conventionally designated in dependence on names and words, for the purpose of conforming to worldly verbal expressions. **The dependent self-nature** means: the self-nature of dharmas produced from conditions. **The consummate self-nature** means: the suchness of all dharmas, the domain of noble knowledge, the object of noble knowledge, that which noble knowledge takes as its object; for the purpose of realizing the most extreme purity, for the purpose of attaining liberation from the two bonds of characteristics and heaviness, and for the purpose of generating meritorious qualities.

**That truth is further fourfold, one should know** means: truth (satya) is further of four kinds: (1) worldly truth, (2) truth of principle, (3) truth in the domain of knowledge purified of the afflictive obstruction, (4) truth in the domain of knowledge purified of the cognitive obstruction.

**Worldly truth** means: all worldly beings, regarding various things, through the knowledge, insight, and understanding obtained by habituation, collectively establish conventional natures, as when regarding earth one says it is solely earth and not fire, and so forth. Likewise with water, fire, wind, form, sound, odor, taste, tangibles, food and drink, clothing, vehicles, various adornments, various utensils, incense, garlands, cosmetics, song, dance, music, companions, men and women, deportment, various practices, fields, dwellings, wealth, and suffering and happiness. Regarding suffering, one says it is suffering and not happiness; regarding happiness, one says it is happiness and not suffering. Furthermore, in brief: "This is this and not that"; likewise, "That is that and not something else." Whatever things the world of sentient beings comprehends with definite conviction, whatever names have been transmitted since ancient times in the world, whatever is definitely determined as self and other by verbal convention and collectively held to be true, not obtained through the observation of wrong thinking: this is called worldly truth.

**Truth of principle** means: those who possess correct knowledge have principled meaning. The intelligent, the clever, the inquiring, the analytical, those who abide on the ground of investigation, those who possess their own skill in debate, those who are in the ordinary-being stage, and those who practice in accord with observation, relying on the three valid means of knowledge, namely direct perception (pratyakṣa), inference (anumāna), and authoritative testimony (āptāgama), exercise their knowledge through extremely skillful determination regarding knowable objects. Because it is established through demonstrative reasoning, this is called truth of principle.

**Truth in the domain of knowledge purified of the afflictive obstruction** means: the domain of practice of the undefiled preparatory knowledge, the undefiled correct knowledge, and the subsequently attained worldly knowledge of all śrāvakas and pratyekabuddhas. This is called truth in the domain of knowledge purified of the afflictive obstruction. Because through taking this as object, knowledge becomes purified of the afflictive obstruction, and afterward one realizes and abides in the nature of unobstructedness. Therefore it is called truth in the domain of knowledge purified of the afflictive obstruction. Question: What herein is the truth? Answer: The four noble truths revealed by the names of suffering, origin, cessation, and path. Through discriminating these four noble truths, one enters the stage of direct realization; in the stage of direct realization, the knowledge of truth arises.

**Truth in the domain of knowledge purified of the cognitive obstruction** means: because among knowable things there is what obstructs knowledge, it is called the cognitive obstruction. If the true nature is the domain of practice of knowledge liberated from the cognitive obstruction, this is called truth in the domain of knowledge purified of the cognitive obstruction: one should understand this. What is this further? It is the domain of practice of the non-discriminating, equal knowledge based on the inexpressible self-nature and the conventionally designated self-nature of all dharmas, that which is apart from all verbal expression, for bodhisattvas and the blessed buddhas, whether those who enter the selflessness of dharmas or those who have entered and are extremely purified. This is the most supreme suchness, the unsurpassed ultimate nature of the knowable. This nature cannot be reversed or surpassed by any correct discrimination of dharmas. This is called truth in the domain of knowledge purified of the cognitive obstruction.

**Four kinds of investigation** (catvāri paryeṣaṇāni): (1) investigation of names, (2) investigation of things, (3) investigation of the conventional designation of self-nature, (4) investigation of the conventional designation of differentiation. **Investigation of names** means: bodhisattvas, regarding names, see only names. **Investigation of things** means: bodhisattvas, regarding things, see only things. **Investigation of the conventional designation of self-nature** means: bodhisattvas, regarding the conventionally designated self-nature, see only the conventionally designated self-nature. **Investigation of the conventional designation of differentiation** means: bodhisattvas, regarding the conventionally designated differentiation, see only the conventionally designated differentiation. This is called investigation of the conventional designation of differentiation. These bodhisattvas, regarding names and things, either observe them separately or observe them together. Based on the combined observation of names and things, they penetrate the conventional designation of self-nature and the conventional designation of differentiation.

**Four kinds of knowledge in accord with reality** (catvāri yathābhūta-parijñānāni): (1) knowledge in accord with reality induced by the investigation of names, (2) knowledge in accord with reality induced by the investigation of things, (3) knowledge in accord with reality induced by the investigation of the conventional designation of self-nature, (4) knowledge in accord with reality induced by the investigation of the conventional designation of differentiation.

**Knowledge in accord with reality induced by the investigation of names** means: because bodhisattvas, through investigating names, see only names, they know names in accord with reality: "This name was established in connection with this thing for this meaning, in order to cause the world to generate perception, views, and verbal expression. If in the perceived objects such as form, the names such as 'form' were not established, all the world would be unable to perceive those objects. If it is form and so forth, and if there were no perception, there would be no one able to generate superimposed grasping. If there is no grasping, there is no verbal expression." If one knows thus in accord with reality, this is called the first knowledge in accord with reality induced by the investigation of names.

**Knowledge in accord with reality induced by the investigation of things** means: because bodhisattvas, through investigating things, see only things, they know in accord with reality that the nature of perceived objects such as form is apart from all verbal expression and is inexpressible. This is called the second knowledge in accord with reality induced by the investigation of things.

**Knowledge in accord with reality induced by the investigation of the conventional designation of self-nature** means: because bodhisattvas, through investigating only conventional designations among the conventionally designated self-natures of perceived objects such as form, penetrate in accord with reality that the conventionally designated self-nature is not truly the self-nature of those things, yet appears as if it were the self-nature of those things. Furthermore, they are able to understand that the self-nature of those things is like a magical transformation, a reflected image, an echo, a shadow, a moon in water, a mirage, a dream, or an illusion, having the semblance of a real substance. This is called the third knowledge in accord with reality, whose domain is the profound meaning, induced by the investigation of the conventional designation of self-nature.

**Knowledge in accord with reality induced by the investigation of the conventional designation of differentiation** means: because bodhisattvas, through investigating the conventional designation of differentiation, see only the nature of conventional designation, they skillfully penetrate the meaning of non-duality within the conventionally designated differentiations of perceived objects such as form. That is, those things are neither of the nature of existence nor of the nature of non-existence. Because the expressible self-nature is not established, they are not of the nature of existence. Because the inexpressible self-nature is established, they are not of the nature of non-existence. Thus, there is no form insofar as the ultimate truth is concerned; yet form is not non-existent, because form is conventionally designated within the conventional truth. As with existence and non-existence, and form and non-form, so too with visible and invisible and other such dharma-gates of conventionally designated differentiation: all of them should be understood through this approach. If one is able to know in accord with reality the meaning of non-duality in such conventionally designated differentiation, this is called the fourth knowledge in accord with reality induced by the investigation of the conventional designation of differentiation.

Having thus revealed the object entered, the cause of entry, and what follows upon entry, furthermore the verse states:

> Establishment of the three self-natures,
> Differentiation, function, hidden meaning,
> Method, inclusion, and distinction:
> Each of these has many kinds.

**The treatise states:** The three self-natures have many kinds of establishment, differentiation, function, hidden meaning, method, inclusion, and distinction: one should understand each. **Establishment of many kinds**: as shall be extensively explained in the chapter on establishing non-nature.

**Differentiation**: Question: How many kinds does the imagined self-nature have? Answer: As many as the conventionally designated differentiations of self-nature found within the dependent self-nature, so too is the imagined self-nature. Therefore, the imagined self-nature is without limit: one should understand this. Furthermore, within the dependent self-nature there are two kinds of discrimination of the imagined self-nature: discrimination following predominant awareness, and the latent tendencies and latent afflictions following habitual seeds. Question: How many kinds does the dependent self-nature have? Answer: One should understand it as having as many kinds as there are differentiations of characteristics, namely: the characteristic of form, the characteristic of mind, the characteristic of mental factors, the characteristic of formations dissociated from mind, and so forth. Furthermore, in brief, there are two kinds of dependent self-nature: that which arises from the discrimination of the imagined self-nature, and that which does not arise from discrimination. Question: How many kinds does the consummate self-nature have? Answer: Because the consummate self-nature is of one flavor in all places, differentiation cannot be established within it.

**Function**: Question: How many functions does the imagined self-nature perform? Answer: Five kinds: (1) it produces the dependent self-nature, (2) within that very thing, it gives rise to verbal expressions, (3) it produces the grasping of sentient beings, (4) it produces the grasping of dharmas, (5) it gathers the heaviness of the habitual tendencies of the two kinds of grasping. Question: How many functions does the dependent self-nature perform? Answer: Five kinds: (1) it produces the substance of all defilements, (2) it serves as the basis for the imagined self-nature and the consummate self-nature, (3) it serves as the basis for the grasping of sentient beings, (4) it serves as the basis for the grasping of dharmas, (5) it serves as the basis for the heaviness of the habitual tendencies of the two kinds of grasping. Question: How many functions does the consummate self-nature perform? Answer: Five kinds, because it serves as the object for the arising of the counteraction of the two kinds of five functions.

**Hidden meaning**: one should interpret all sūtras of non-definitive meaning in accordance with the meaning of the three self-natures, because the hidden words of all tathāgatas and all bodhisattvas in the immeasurable sūtras can only be comprehended in accordance with the three self-natures. Question: The sūtras speak of three gates of liberation; how are they established? Answer: Through the three self-natures. Through the imagined self-nature, the gate of emptiness liberation is established. Through the dependent self-nature, the gate of wishlessness liberation is established. Through the consummate self-nature, the gate of signlessness liberation is established. Question: The sūtras speak of the patience of the non-arising of dharmas (anutpattika-dharma-kṣānti); how is it established? Answer: Through the three self-natures. Through the imagined self-nature, the patience of original non-arising is taught. Through the dependent self-nature, the patience of spontaneous non-arising is taught. Through the consummate self-nature, the patience of the non-arising of the defilements of affliction and suffering is taught. These three kinds of patience belong to the stage of non-retrogression: one should understand this. Through such and other differentiations of meaning, all other hidden expressions should be interpreted in accordance with the three self-natures: one should understand this.

**Method**: knowing these three self-natures serves as the method for the awakening of all śrāvakas, pratyekabuddhas, and the unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening.

**Inclusion**: the three self-natures and the five categories of characteristics, names, discrimination, and so forth are mutually inclusive. Question: How many categories are the first self-nature included in? Answer: None. Question: How many categories are the second self-nature included in? Answer: Four. Question: How many categories are the third self-nature included in? Answer: One.

**Distinction**: the imagined self-nature should solely be understood. The dependent self-nature should be understood and should be severed. The consummate self-nature should be understood and should be directly witnessed.

Having thus entered the conventional truth and the ultimate truth, furthermore the verse states:

> Hearing the twelve divisions of teaching,
> The three supreme refuges,
> The three trainings and the three awakenings:
> These are taught for the purification of sentient beings.

**The treatise states:** **Hearing the twelve divisions of teaching** means hearing the sacred teaching of: (1) sūtras (sūtra), (2) verses restating in poetry (geya), (3) prophecies (vyākaraṇa), (4) stanzas (gāthā), (5) inspired utterances (udāna), (6) narratives of conditions (nidāna), (7) parables (avadāna), (8) stories of former events (itivṛttaka), (9) stories of former lives (jātaka), (10) extensive discourses (vaipulya), (11) marvelous dharmas (adbhutadharma), (12) doctrinal expositions (upadeśa).

**Sūtras** means: the Blessed Buddha, at various times and places, in accordance with the various beings to be converted and the differentiations of their disciplinary practices, teaches dharmas included within the aggregates, dharmas included within the elements, dharmas included within the sense-bases, dharmas included within dependent arising, dharmas included within nutriments, dharmas included within the truths, dharmas included within śrāvakas, pratyekabuddhas, and tathāgatas, dharmas included within the foundations of mindfulness, right exertions, bases of spiritual power, faculties, powers, factors of awakening, and path factors, and dharmas included within impurity, mindfulness of breathing, training, confirmed faith, and so forth.

When the Tathāgata has spoken these words, the compilers joyfully and respectfully receive them. In order that the sacred teaching may long endure, using excellent name-bodies, phrase-bodies, and syllable-bodies, they compile in due order and arrange in due order. Because these are able to bind together and draw forth various benefits and draw forth various holy-life excellences, they are called sūtras.

**Verses restating in poetry** means: in the sūtras, whether in the middle or at the end, verses that repeat and reveal the meaning, together with non-definitive teachings in the sūtras: these are verses restating in poetry.

**Prophecies** means: in the sūtras, the records of the places of rebirth after death of the various disciples, together with the definitive teachings in the sūtras: these are prophecies.

**Stanzas** means: in the sūtras, what is not spoken in continuous prose but composed in verse form, whether of two lines, three lines, four lines, five lines, or six lines: these are stanzas.

**Inspired utterances** means: in the sūtras, without listing the name of the questioner, for the sake of the long endurance of the correct Dharma and the long endurance of the sacred teaching, what is spontaneously proclaimed: these are inspired utterances.

**Narratives of conditions** means: in the sūtras, after listing the name of the questioner, what is then proclaimed; together with all the teachings included within the Vinaya that have a narrative of conditions, such as the prātimokṣa precept-sūtras: these are narratives of conditions.

**Parables** means: in the sūtras, what is taught through parables, because through parables the original meaning becomes clear: these are parables.

**Stories of former events** means: the proclamation of related events from former lives: these are stories of former events.

**Stories of former lives** means: in the sūtras, the proclamation of how the Tathāgata in past lives, dwelling in various births and deaths, practiced the bodhisattva conduct: these are stories of former lives.

**Extensive discourses** means: in the sūtras, the proclamation of the paths of bodhisattvas that enable the realization of unsurpassed awakening, causing them to realize the ten powers, unobstructed knowledge, and so forth: these are extensive discourses.

**Marvelous dharmas** means: in the sūtras, the proclamation of the shared and exclusive meritorious qualities of the buddhas and their disciples, whether bhikṣus, bhikṣuṇīs, śikṣamāṇās, śrāmaṇeras, śrāmaṇerīs, upāsakas, or upāsikās, together with the most supreme, extraordinary, astonishing, and profound dharmas: these are marvelous dharmas.

**Doctrinal expositions** means: all the mātṛkā and abhidharma that investigate and explain the meaning of the sūtras: these are doctrinal expositions.

Among these twelve divisions of teaching, the three baskets (tripiṭaka) of sūtra, vinaya, and abhidharma are fully present. Herein, the sūtras, verses restating in poetry, prophecies, stanzas, inspired utterances, parables, stories of former events, stories of former lives, extensive discourses, and marvelous dharmas constitute the sūtra basket. Herein, the narratives of conditions constitute the vinaya basket. Herein, the doctrinal expositions constitute the abhidharma basket.

**The three supreme refuges** means: the three kinds of refuge in the Buddha, the Dharma, and the Saṅgha. **The three trainings** means: the superior training in morality (adhiśīla-śikṣā), the superior training in mind (adhicitta-śikṣā), and the superior training in wisdom (adhiprajñā-śikṣā). **The three awakenings** means: śrāvaka awakening, pratyekabuddha awakening, and unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening.

**Taught for the purification of sentient beings** means: for the purpose of enabling sentient beings to attain purification, these three kinds of dharma are taught in sequence: namely, the capacity, the means, and the fruit. **The capacity** means: hearing and taking refuge. **The means** means: the three trainings. **The fruit** means: the three awakenings.

Furthermore, how should hearing and the rest be discriminated? One should understand this. The verse states:

> Hearing, refuge, training, and awakening:
> Six, three, twelve, and five.
> Following the numerical order of each name,
> One should discriminate extensively as appropriate.

**The treatise states:** **Hearing discriminated into six** means: (1) basis, (2) inclusion, (3) purity, (4) practice, (5) approach, (6) meaning.

**Basis** means: based on the five sciences (pañca-vidyā): (1) the inner science (adhyātma-vidyā), (2) the science of logic (hetu-vidyā), (3) the science of grammar (śabda-vidyā), (4) the science of medicine (cikitsā-vidyā), (5) the science of arts and crafts (śilpakarmasthāna-vidyā).

**Inclusion** is of two kinds: included in the śrāvaka basket, and included in the bodhisattva basket.

**Purity** means: ten kinds of pure teaching. The purity of teaching has five: (1) because it is taught by one who teaches well, (2) because it is taught with clear words and phrases, (3) because it is taught according to the meaning of everything knowable and as things are known, (4) because it is taught as easy to practice with skillful means, (5) because it is taught as enabling liberation from all suffering. The purity of listening has five: (1) because one does not listen with the intention of finding faults, (2) because one listens with the intention of seeking nirvāṇa, (3) because one listens with the utmost care and sincerity, (4) because one listens by extremely skillfully discriminating the meanings of name-bodies, phrase-bodies, and syllable-bodies, (5) because one listens with the intention of correct practice.

**Practice** means: the ten kinds of dharma-practice.

**Approach** means: there are six kinds of approach: namely, the approach of true meaning through to the approach of intention. Herein, the first three approaches are interpreted by the latter three approaches: one should understand this. The approach of departing from two extremes interprets the approach of true meaning. The approach of the inconceivable interprets the approach of realization. The approach of intention interprets the approach of instruction. Herein, the true meaning itself is the approach; therefore it is called the approach of true meaning. Through to: the intention itself is the approach; therefore it is called the approach of intention. In each respective context, the nature of non-inversion is the meaning of "approach."

The **approach of true meaning** is briefly of six kinds, one should understand: namely, worldly truth through to truth in the domain of knowledge purified of the cognitive obstruction, together with established truth and non-established truth. Herein, the first four kinds of truth should be understood as previously discriminated. **Established truth** means the four noble truths. Suffering is truly suffering; therefore it is established as suffering. Through to: the path is truly the path; therefore it is established as the path. Question: For what reason is it called "established"? Answer: Because it is established through three kinds of convention: (1) through worldly convention, (2) through the convention of principle, (3) through the convention of realization. **Worldly convention** means: the establishment of fields, dwellings, pots, bowls, armies, forests, numbers, and so forth, and the establishment of self, sentient beings, and so forth. **The convention of principle** means: the establishment of aggregates, elements, sense-bases, and so forth. **The convention of realization** means: the establishment of the fruit of stream-entry and so forth, and the establishment of the dharmas on which they depend. Furthermore, there are four kinds of establishment: the preceding three kinds, and the fourth, the establishment through the convention of the ultimate, whereby the nature of the ultimate truth cannot be conventionally established, because it is internally self-realized; yet for the purpose of conforming to and inducing the knowledge thereof, it is conventionally established. **Non-established truth** means: the true, real nature of suchness of all dharmas.

The **approach of realization** is briefly of four kinds: (1) the realization of the karmic retribution of all sentient beings, (2) the realization of the śrāvaka vehicle, (3) the realization of the pratyekabuddha vehicle, (4) the realization of the great vehicle.

**The realization of the karmic retribution of all sentient beings** means: all sentient beings create pure and impure karma; relying on their own karma, within the cycle of the five destinies, they experience various kinds of ripening and receive various kinds of ripening.

**The realization of the śrāvaka vehicle** means: first receiving the three refuges, through to relying on the adornment of hearing, one attains five kinds of realization: (1) realization of grounds, (2) realization of knowledge, (3) realization of purity, (4) realization of fruits, (5) realization of meritorious qualities. **Realization of grounds** means attaining three grounds: (1) the ground of seeing (darśana-bhūmi), (2) the ground of cultivation (bhāvanā-bhūmi), (3) the ultimate ground (niṣṭhā-bhūmi). **Realization of knowledge** means attaining nine knowledges: (1) knowledge of dharmas (dharma-jñāna), (2) knowledge of types (anvaya-jñāna), (3) knowledge of suffering (duḥkha-jñāna), (4) knowledge of origination (samudaya-jñāna), (5) knowledge of cessation (nirodha-jñāna), (6) knowledge of the path (mārga-jñāna), (7) the worldly knowledge subsequently attained therefrom, (8) knowledge of exhaustion (kṣaya-jñāna), (9) knowledge of non-arising (anutpāda-jñāna). **Realization of purity** means the four confirmed faiths. **Realization of fruits** means the four fruits of the ascetic. **Realization of meritorious qualities** means the meritorious qualities of the immeasurables, liberations, bases of mastery, totality bases, non-contention, wish-born knowledge, unimpeded knowledges, six supernatural powers, and so forth. Furthermore, regarding the support of realization in the śrāvaka vehicle: first one cultivates the worldly path of separation from desire, next one cultivates the wholesome roots of the preparatory portion conducive to liberation (mokṣa-bhāgīya), and afterward one cultivates the wholesome roots of the preparatory portion conducive to penetration (nirvedha-bhāgīya).

**The realization of the pratyekabuddha vehicle** is briefly of three kinds: (1) through having previously obtained the wholesome roots of the preparatory portion conducive to penetration, (2) through having previously obtained the undefiled true realization, (3) through obtaining them in sequence. Herein, those who realize through the first two are called "non-solitary-victorious awakened ones"; those who realize through the latter are called "rhinoceros-horn-like awakened ones."

**The realization of the great vehicle** means: the realization of great compassion, the realization of generating the aspiration, the realization of the pāramitās, the realization of the means of attraction, the realization of the grounds, the realization of reaching suchness within the five immeasurables, the realization of inconceivable majestic power, and the realization of the exclusive dharmas of the Buddha. All of these should be understood as previously discriminated.

The **approach of instruction** is briefly included in three domains: (1) included in the baskets (piṭaka), (2) included in the mātṛkā, (3) included in both. **Included in the baskets** means: the śrāvaka-vehicle basket and the great-vehicle basket. **Included in the mātṛkā** means: the seventeen fundamental grounds and the four kinds of compilation. **Included in both** means briefly ten kinds, as should be understood from the ten kinds of meaning previously discriminated. This serves to compile all that is included in the baskets and all that is included in the mātṛkā; therefore it is called the comprehensive, summary mātṛkā.

Furthermore, there are twelve kinds of teaching. (1) **Teaching of objects**: the teaching that proclaims individual dharmas such as form, the eye, and all dharmas. (2) **Teaching of conceptual differentiation**: the teaching that proclaims the aggregates, elements, sense-bases, dependent arising, what is possible and impossible, the faculties, the truths, the foundations of mindfulness, having form and being formless, visible and invisible, resistant and non-resistant, and so forth; such extensively proclaimed immeasurable teachings of conceptual differentiation by the Blessed Buddha. (3) **Teaching of investigating one's own doctrine**: the teachings of sūtras, verses restating in poetry, prophecies, and so forth, as displayed in the compiled interpretations. (4) **Teaching of investigating other doctrines**: the teaching that, relying on the seven principles of logic, defeats others' treatises and establishes one's own treatise. The seven principles of logic, namely the body of the argument, the locus of the argument, and so forth, shall be discriminated later. (5) **Teaching of non-definitive meaning**: among sūtras, verses restating in poetry, prophecies, and so forth, the Blessed One has briefly indicated the meaning without extensively discriminating it, and it should be further revealed. (6) **Teaching of definitive meaning**: one should understand this as the opposite of the preceding. (7) **Teaching of the conventional truth**: all that is displayed through the path of verbal expression is collectively called the conventional truth. Furthermore, the characteristics, names, and discriminations that arise through the predominance of names, perceptions, and verbal expressions are also the conventional truth. (8) **Teaching of the ultimate truth**: the teaching of the four noble truths, together with the teaching of suchness, the true limit, and the dharma-nature. (9) **Hidden teaching**: for the most part, the teachings of the śrāvaka basket. (10) **Revealed teaching**: for the most part, the teachings of the great-vehicle basket. (11) **Teaching of recordable matters**: as in the four dharma-summary teachings: "All conditioned things are impermanent" through to "nirvāṇa is quiescence"; and such teachings. (12) **Teaching of non-recordable matters**: as when someone asks: "Is the world permanent or impermanent?" The Tathāgata at that time remains silent and does not record it, but merely tells them: "I declare that this matter cannot be recorded." Through to when someone asks: "After the Tathāgata's cessation, does he neither exist nor not exist?" The Tathāgata at that time remains silent and does not record it, but merely tells them: "I declare that this matter cannot be recorded."

Herein, non-recordable matters are proclaimed for four reasons, one should understand. (1) Because there is no real substance, they cannot be recorded. As when someone asks: "Is the self identical with or different from the aggregates? Is it permanent or impermanent?" And so forth. (2) Because it would lead to what is without benefit, it cannot be recorded. As the *Śiṃśapā-vana-sūtra* says: "There are immeasurable dharmas that I have fully realized but do not proclaim. Why? Because those dharmas would lead to what is without benefit." (3) Because it is extremely profound, it cannot be recorded. As when someone asks: "Does the self exist or not exist?" This cannot be recorded. Why? If the Tathāgata were to record that the self exists, that person might grasp that there is a self within the aggregates or a self apart from the aggregates. If he were to record that the self does not exist, that person might denigrate even the conventional verbal expression of self as non-existent. Through to when someone asks: "After the Tathāgata's cessation, does he exist, not exist, both exist and not exist, or neither exist nor not exist?" Because of its extreme profundity, none of these can be recorded. (4) Because the nature of those characteristics is simply thus, it cannot be recorded. The suchness of all dharmas, whether it is identical with or different from those dharmas, cannot be recorded, because the nature of that suchness simply is such that neither identity nor difference can be established.

Furthermore, for four additional reasons the Tathāgata proclaims non-recordable matters, one should understand: (1) because these matters are spoken of by non-Buddhist teachers, (2) because they are not in accord with principle, (3) because they do not lead to benefit, (4) because they only give rise to contentious afflictions. There are two reasons why they do not lead to benefit, one should understand: (1) because they are remote from the contemplation of cause and effect, (2) because they are remote from the contemplation of defilement and purity.

The **approach of departing from two extremes** is briefly of six kinds, one should understand: (1) departing from the extreme of superimposing what does not truly exist, (2) departing from the extreme of denying what truly exists, (3) departing from the extreme of grasping permanence, (4) departing from the extreme of grasping annihilation, (5) departing from the extreme of indulging in sensual pleasure, (6) departing from the extreme of indulging in self-mortification.

The **approach of the inconceivable** means briefly six kinds of inconceivable matters: (1) the self is inconceivable, (2) sentient beings are inconceivable, (3) the world is inconceivable, (4) the karmic retribution of all sentient beings is inconceivable, (5) those who have attained dhyāna and the domain of dhyāna are inconceivable, (6) the buddhas and the domain of the buddhas are inconceivable.

The **approach of intention** means briefly sixteen kinds of intention: (1) the intention of revelation, (2) the intention of dispassion, (3) the intention of exhortation, (4) the intention of encouragement, (5) the intention of praise and delight, (6) the intention of causing entry, (7) the intention of removing doubt, (8) the intention of maturation, (9) the intention of pacification, (10) the intention of liberation, (11) the intention based on a special meaning, (12) the intention of generating faultless joy in those who practice through realization, (13) the intention of causing those who practice through hearing to generate reverence for the Dharma teacher, (14) the intention of the dissemination of the Dharma-eye, (15) the intention of the increase of the wholesome, (16) the intention of crushing all characteristics.

**Meaning** means: non-definitive meaning and definitive meaning.

**Refuge discriminated into three** means: (1) accomplishment, (2) establishment, (3) differentiation.

**Accomplishment** means: only the Buddha, the Dharma, and the Saṅgha are the true refuge, not the gods and others. Why? For two reasons: (1) because they are incapable, (2) because they are not directly seen.

How are they **incapable**? The gods are unable to perform beneficial activities for sentient beings. These gods either lack the ability, or they await worship, or they cannot endure fatigue and suffering, or they lack compassion, or they have obstructions.
