For all these reasons, they are not true places of refuge: because they lack ability, because they are partisan, because they avoid their own fatigue and suffering and lack autonomy, because they lack sympathy, and because their virtues are meager.

How are they **not directly seen**? The gods are not seen through direct realization. The world has never seen a master who is not directly seen being able to serve as a support, except that, relying on faith, one can receive from others; but what is relied on through direct seeing is not seen. Question: Should not the gods be a refuge because they are seen in dreams? Answer: What is seen through the desire-imagination may or may not be real. Furthermore, why are they not directly seen while awake? Although in dreams one occasionally sees something real, this too is produced by the desire-imagination. Furthermore, when various conditions are present, the dreamer occasionally sees something, but this too is mostly false.

Furthermore, for five reasons the gods and others are not places of refuge. What are the five? (1) By their characteristics, (2) by their substance, (3) by their function, (4) by the nature of things, (5) by cause and effect.

**By their characteristics** means: the gods are not directly seen in the world; they have no discourse, and therefore one cannot take refuge. Their countenances are alarming, causing fear. They are habituated to heedlessness, having desire and attachment. They abandon the benefit of others, lacking compassion. They are unable to understand what should and should not be done, not reaching the true meaning. Therefore, one cannot take refuge in them. How, by their characteristics, can one take refuge in the Buddha? He is directly seen in the world, having discourse. His countenance is peaceful, without fear. He has departed from heedlessness, without desire or attachment. He does not abandon the benefit of others, possessing great compassion. He skillfully understands what should and should not be done, penetrating the true meaning. Furthermore, for five characteristics the Buddha is worthy of refuge. What are the five? Because he has realized the great awakening for the benefit of sentient beings. Because he presently appears in great assemblies and opens the eye of the correct Dharma. Because he equally benefits sentient beings whether foes or friends. Because he has relinquished the reception and attachment of all households, with faculties quiescent. Because he skillfully dispels the net of doubts of all sentient beings.

**By their substance** means: because the Tathāgata has permanently severed all outflows, having himself been disciplined, he also disciplines others; therefore one can take refuge. Because the gods possess all outflows, they are not yet able to discipline even themselves, let alone discipline others; therefore they are not places of refuge.

**By their function** means: because the Tathāgata abides in the vast, stainless functions of dhyāna and so forth, and furthermore is able to perform the function of benefiting sentient beings; therefore one can take refuge. Because the gods abide in the base, defiled function of enjoying sensual pleasures, and furthermore have the function of killing sentient beings; therefore they are not places of refuge.

**By the nature of things** means: all worldly and supramundane meritorious qualities and benefits are all obtained through reliance on one's own effort. If one is separated from one's own effort, even though one generates deep reverence and faith toward the gods, one cannot realize them. Even if one does not generate reverence and faith toward the gods, but only applies one's own effort, one can certainly realize them. Therefore, the gods are not places of refuge.

**By cause and effect** means: I now ask those who worship the gods: Is the substance of a god obtained through karmic action that leads to a heavenly rebirth, through worshipping the god, or without any cause? If it is obtained through karmic action, then one should take refuge in karma, not in the god. If it is obtained without any cause, then one should take refuge in the absence of cause, not in the god. If it is obtained through worshipping the god, is it obtained solely through the cause of worship, solely through the cause of the god, or through both? If solely through worship, then worshipping the god is futile; worship anywhere should be able to bring about a heavenly rebirth. If solely through the god, then setting up worship is futile; even without worship, merely through the god, one should obtain a heavenly rebirth. If through both, then merely by setting up worship and the god's reception, all that is prayed for should come to fruition. Yet regarding seven kinds of prayer, the result is not certain; therefore this is not so. The seven are: (1) regarding the reception through worship conditions, (2) regarding the reception through faith conditions, (3) regarding the faith of the faithful being able to bring about the most supreme god-substance, (4) regarding the ability to bring about the most supreme enjoyment of wealth and pleasure, (5) regarding the crushing of enemies such as the asuras, (6) regarding birth, (7) regarding death.

**Establishment**: Question: How many kinds of refuge are there? Answer: Three kinds of refuge: the Buddha, the Dharma, and the Saṅgha. Question: Why are there only three kinds of refuge? Answer: For four reasons, only the Tathāgata is worthy of being a refuge: because he has well disciplined himself, because he skillfully understands all kinds of means of discipline, because he is not pleased by material offerings, and because he is pleased by the offering of practice. Because he possesses these virtues, the Dharma he teaches and the assembly of his disciples are also worthy of being a refuge. Question: At what point is one called "one who has taken refuge"? Answer: Through possessing four causes, one is called "one who has taken refuge": (1) because one well knows the virtues, (2) because one well knows the differences, (3) because one personally vows to receive, (4) because one no longer takes refuge in anything else. Question: What is the correct practice of taking refuge? Answer: There are four kinds of correct practice of taking refuge, one should understand: (1) drawing near to good people, (2) listening to the correct Dharma, (3) correct mental attention, (4) practice in accord with the Dharma. Furthermore, there are four kinds of correct practice, one should understand: (1) well restraining the sense-faculties from agitation, (2) receiving the correct training rules, (3) compassion for sentient beings, (4) regularly making offerings to the Three Jewels in accord with the Dharma. Question: What are the benefits of taking refuge in the Three Jewels? Answer: There are four kinds of benefit: (1) one obtains vast meritorious qualities, (2) one obtains vast joy, (3) one obtains supreme concentration, (4) one obtains excellent purity. Furthermore, there are four kinds of benefit: (1) one is completely endowed with great protection; (2) all obstructions from wrong understanding gradually become attenuated, exhausted, and disappear; (3) one enters the assembly of intelligent and correct good people, namely the great teacher and the community of fellow practitioners of the holy life; (4) one is beloved by the gods who have faith in the sacred teaching. When those gods see anyone who has taken the three refuges, they generate great joy and tell each other: "In the past, all of us, through accomplishing the three refuges, passed from those lives and came to be born here. These good sons and others now also accomplish these three refuges and dwell much in them without abandoning them; before long they shall come to be our companions."

**Differentiation** means: through six kinds of cause, the differentiation of the Three Jewels should be understood: (1) by their characteristics, (2) by their function, (3) by their mode of faith, (4) by their practice, (5) by their recollection, (6) by their merit-generation. **Differentiation by their characteristics** means: the characteristic of self-realized awakening is the Buddha-jewel; the characteristic of the fruit of awakening is the Dharma-jewel; the characteristic of correct practice in accordance with others' teaching is the Saṅgha-jewel. **Differentiation by their function** means: the function of turning the wheel of correct teaching is the Buddha-jewel; the function of serving as the object of the severance of afflictions and suffering is the Dharma-jewel; the function of increasing diligence and vigor is the Saṅgha-jewel. **Differentiation by their mode of faith** means: regarding the Buddha-jewel, one should draw near and reverently attend; regarding the correct Dharma-jewel, one should have faith and reverence and bear witness through realization; regarding the Saṅgha-jewel, one should share the same Dharma, dwell together, and draw near with faith and reverence. **Differentiation by their practice** means: regarding the Buddha-jewel, one should practice inviting, welcoming, attending, and making offerings; regarding the correct Dharma-jewel, one should practice cultivating in accordance with principle through skillful means; regarding the Saṅgha-jewel, one should practice the mutual sharing of material goods and Dharma. **Differentiation by their recollection** means: regarding the Three Jewels, one should give rise to distinct practices of recollection for each. As the sūtra says: "This Blessed One, Tathāgata, Arhat, Perfectly and Fully Awakened One," and so forth extensively. **Differentiation by their merit-generation** means: regarding the Buddha-jewel, the most supreme merit is generated in dependence on a single sentient being; regarding the correct Dharma-jewel, the most supreme merit is generated in dependence on a superior dharma; regarding the Saṅgha-jewel, the most supreme merit is generated in dependence on many sentient beings.

---

## Fascicle 7

### Chapter on Gathering the Meaning of Purity, Part Three

**The treatise states:** **The twelve kinds of discrimination regarding the trainings** are: (1) discrimination by differentiation, (2) discrimination by arising, (3) discrimination by transformation, (4) discrimination by counteraction and what is counteracted, (5) discrimination by the ability to bring about excellent rebirth and definite excellence, (6) discrimination by concordant dharmas, (7) discrimination by persons, (8) discrimination by inferior, intermediate, and superior, (9) discrimination by yoga, (10) discrimination by mental attention, (11) discrimination by generation, (12) discrimination by question and answer.

**Discrimination by differentiation** means discriminating the differentiation of the three trainings.

**The differentiation of the superior training in morality** (adhiśīla-śikṣā): as the sūtra says, "If a bhikṣu dwells accomplished in morality, guarding the prātimokṣa restraint, with deportment and range of activity complete in every respect, seeing great danger in the slightest faults, taking up and training in the training rules, he is called one endowed with morality."

Herein, **dwelling accomplished in morality** means: regarding the training rules one has received, one's bodily actions are without transgression, one's verbal actions are without transgression, unbroken and unpierced. Thus one dwells accomplished in morality.

**Guarding the prātimokṣa restraint** means: the morality of the seven assemblies is called the prātimokṣa restraint. Because of the differentiation of these morality-assemblies, many kinds of restraint are established. The meaning herein is explained solely in terms of the bhikṣu restraint. This is called guarding the prātimokṣa restraint.

**Complete in deportment** means: whether in deportment, in activities, or in the skillful cultivation of wholesome practices, one is complete in deportment, conforming to the world and not violating the world, conforming to the Vinaya and not violating the Vinaya.

How is one complete in deportment, conforming to and not violating the world and Vinaya, **regarding deportment**? At whatever time and place one should walk, and in whatever manner one should walk, one correctly walks at that time and place in that manner, without being reproached or censured by the world, and without being reproached or censured by the intelligent, the correct, the good, those who share the same Dharma, those who uphold the Vinaya, and those who study the Vinaya. As with walking, so too with standing, sitting, and lying down: one should understand likewise.

How is one complete in deportment, conforming to and not violating the world and Vinaya, **regarding activities**? Activities include: putting on robes, relieving oneself, using water and tooth-sticks, entering a village to beg for food, returning and partaking, washing and storing the bowl, washing the feet, and arranging bedding. Furthermore, briefly attending to bowl-work, robe-work, and whatever other activities are in accord with the Dharma: these are called activities. As appropriate, at whatever time and place one should act, and in whatever manner one should act, one correctly acts at that time and place in that manner, without being reproached or censured by the world, and without being reproached or censured by the intelligent, the correct, the good, those who share the same Dharma, those who uphold the Vinaya, and those who study the Vinaya. This is called being complete in deportment, conforming to and not violating the world and Vinaya, regarding activities.

How is one complete in deportment, conforming to and not violating the world and Vinaya, **regarding the skillful cultivation of wholesome practices**? This includes: reading and reciting the scriptures, harmoniously respecting one's teachers, cultivating the work of attending and serving, visiting and caring for the sick, mutually giving rise to a compassionate mind and expressing one's wishes, skillfully cultivating practice, requesting, questioning, and listening to the Dharma with diligence and without falling away, personally attending upon the intelligent and those who share the holy life, encouraging and exhorting others to cultivate wholesome practices, proclaiming and teaching the profound and wonderful Dharma, and entering a quiet and secluded place to sit in cross-legged meditation. Such and other wholesome dharmas are called the skillful cultivation of wholesome practices. In the skillful cultivation of the wholesome practices taught as above, at whatever time and place one should cultivate, and in whatever manner one should cultivate, one correctly cultivates at that time and place in that manner. Through such cultivation, one is not reproached or censured by the world, and not reproached or censured by the intelligent, the correct, the good, those who share the same Dharma, those who uphold the Vinaya, and those who study the Vinaya. This is called being complete in deportment, conforming to and not violating the world and Vinaya, regarding the skillful cultivation of wholesome practices. If one's deportment is complete in such characteristics, this is called complete in deportment.

**Complete in range of activity** means: there are five places that are not the range of activity of a bhikṣu. What are the five? (1) A place of public announcement, (2) a prostitute's home, (3) a liquor seller's home, (4) a royal palace, (5) the home of a caṇḍāla or kācina; together with other places that the Tathāgata has decreed one should not frequent. All places other than these are one's range of activity. To frequent faultless places at the proper time is called complete in range of activity.

**Seeing great danger in the slightest faults** means: transgressions of the minor training rules that one can emerge from after committing are all called slight faults. Furthermore, those from which, after committing, one can emerge with little effort are called slight faults. If one sees great danger therein, thinking: "May I not, through committing this, become unfit to attain what has not yet been attained, to realize what has not yet been realized, to verify what has not yet been verified. May I not fall into evil destinies and engage in evil-destiny conduct. May I not later reproach myself. May I not be reproached by the great teacher, the gods, the intelligent, and those who share the holy life, through the Dharma. May my evil reputation not spread throughout the ten directions." Seeing such unpleasant consequences in both the present and future lives, one generates deep fear. For this reason, even regarding the minor training rules, even at the cost of one's life, one will never intentionally transgress them. If through a lapse of mindfulness one occasionally transgresses, one swiftly repents and emerges in accord with the Dharma. This is called seeing great danger in the slightest faults.

**Taking up and training in the training rules** means: when one first receives the prātimokṣa restraint, through the procedure of four announcements (jñapticaturtha-karman) for receiving full ordination, one has already heard in summary the substance of the training rules. Furthermore, regarding the more than one hundred and fifty training rules taught in the prātimokṣa sūtra, one personally vows: "I shall fully train in all the training rules." Furthermore, from one's upādhyāya, ācārya, and all those who engage in discussion, who question and challenge, who frequently associate with one, and who are in wholesome agreement, one repeatedly hears them. Furthermore, every half-month one hears the recitation of the prātimokṣa sūtra. Through thus receiving all the training rules, one is called one who has obtained the prātimokṣa restraint. From then onward, regarding all the training rules one has well trained in, there is no transgression; if there is transgression, one immediately emerges in accord with the Dharma. If regarding the training rules one previously vowed to receive, one does not understand or comprehend them well, one should, as when one first received them, repeatedly request, question, and listen from one's upādhyāya, ācārya, and others, so as to understand and comprehend them well. As the venerable one has taught, without addition or subtraction, one well cultivates and trains. Furthermore, one correctly upholds both the words and the meaning without error. This is called taking up and training in the training rules.

Having thus extensively explained the differentiation of morality and restraint, if one states the meaning in brief: herein, the Blessed One reveals the morality aggregate through three characteristics: (1) the characteristic of being without failure, (2) the characteristic of inherent nature, (3) the characteristic of the meritorious qualities of the inherent nature. Herein, "dwelling accomplished in morality" as stated above reveals the characteristic of the morality-restraint being without failure. Next, "guarding the prātimokṣa restraint" reveals the characteristic of inherent nature. Next, "with deportment and range of activity complete in every respect" reveals the characteristic of the meritorious quality and reputation of the received prātimokṣa restraint regarding observation by others. Why? Because others, seeing one's complete deportment and range of activity, those who have not yet generated faith generate faith, and those who have already generated faith increase it. Because those who have not yet believed come to believe through this, there is no contempt and no evil reputation. If this were not so, even though one were endowed with morality, through transgressing deportment and range of activity, there would be no observation by others regarding meritorious qualities and reputation. If the opposite were the case, there would be no fault. Lastly, "seeing great danger in the slightest faults and taking up and training in the training rules" reveals the characteristic of meritorious quality and reputation regarding observation of oneself. Why? Although through being complete in deportment and range of activity one obtains the meritorious quality and reputation of observation by others, if one then violates morality, through this causal condition one may be born in evil destinies, or one may become unfit to attain what has not yet been attained, to realize what has not yet been realized, to verify what has not yet been verified. If one is able to see danger even in the slightest faults, how much more in the greater ones. Furthermore, by taking up and training in the training rules, through this causal condition, when the body breaks apart and life ends, one is born in a good destiny, and furthermore one becomes fit to attain what has not yet been attained, to realize what has not yet been realized, to verify what has not yet been verified. For this reason, it is called the characteristic of the morality-restraint's meritorious quality and reputation regarding observation of oneself.

Furthermore, herein the Blessed One reveals three kinds of morality-nature: (1) the nature of received morality, (2) the nature of liberating morality, (3) the nature of cultivated morality. First, "dwelling accomplished in morality" reveals the nature of received morality. Next, "guarding the prātimokṣa restraint" reveals the nature of liberating morality. Why? Because the morality included in the prātimokṣa restraint is called the superior training in morality. Relying on the superior training in morality, one is able to cultivate the superior training in mind and the superior training in wisdom. By relying on these, one is able to bring about the liberation of the permanent exhaustion of all suffering. Such liberation is obtained only after first relying on the practice of morality; therefore the prātimokṣa restraint is called the nature of liberating morality. Lastly, "with deportment and range of activity complete in every respect, seeing great danger in the slightest faults, taking up and training in the training rules" reveals the nature of cultivated morality. If one cultivates the morality of the prātimokṣa restraint in accordance with such characteristics, this is called cultivation that is well cultivated. This is the discrimination by differentiation of the superior training in morality.

**The discrimination by differentiation of the superior training in mind** (adhicitta-śikṣā): a bhikṣu, separating from desires and unwholesome dharmas, with investigation and analysis, with joy and happiness born of separation, dwells in the complete fulfillment of the first dhyāna. Furthermore, with the stilling of investigation and analysis, with inner clarity, with the mind unified, without investigation and analysis, with joy and happiness born of concentration, one dwells in the complete fulfillment of the second dhyāna. Furthermore, with the separation from joy, one abides in equanimity and mindfulness, with clear knowing and bodily experience of happiness. As the noble ones declare: "One who has equanimity and mindfulness dwells in happiness." One dwells in the complete fulfillment of the third dhyāna. Furthermore, with the severance of pleasure and the prior severance of suffering, with the disappearance of joy and sorrow, with the purity of equanimity and mindfulness that is neither suffering nor pleasure, one dwells in the complete fulfillment of the fourth dhyāna. The meaning of these differentiations has been explained above. This is the discrimination by differentiation of the superior training in mind.

**The discrimination by differentiation of the superior training in wisdom** (adhiprajñā-śikṣā): a bhikṣu knows the noble truth of suffering in accord with reality as suffering, knows the noble truth of the origin of suffering in accord with reality as the origin, knows the noble truth of the cessation of suffering in accord with reality as cessation, and knows the noble truth of the path leading to the cessation of suffering in accord with reality as the path. This is the discrimination by differentiation of the superior training in wisdom.

**Discrimination by arising** means: through accomplishment in morality, there is no remorse. Through the absence of remorse, delight arises. Through delight, the mind is joyful. Through joy, the body is tranquil. Through tranquility, one experiences happiness. Through happiness, the mind becomes concentrated. Through concentration, one observes in accord with reality. Through observing in accord with reality, disenchantment arises. Through disenchantment, dispassion arises. Through dispassion, liberation arises. Through liberation, one personally declares: "I have verified liberation." Furthermore, this knowledge and vision arises: "Birth is exhausted, the holy life has been lived, what was to be done has been done, there will be no further existence."

**Discrimination by transformation** means: there may be the superior training in morality without the superior training in mind or wisdom. There may be the superior training in morality and the superior training in mind without the superior training in wisdom. If there is the superior training in wisdom, there is necessarily also the superior training in morality and the superior training in mind.

**Discrimination by counteraction and what is counteracted** means: the superior training in morality is the counteraction that brings about the cessation of active afflictions. The superior training in mind is the counteraction that suppresses active afflictions. The superior training in wisdom is the counteraction that permanently severs the latent tendencies of afflictions.

**Discrimination by the ability to bring about excellent rebirth and definite excellence** means: the superior training in morality and the superior training in mind are able to bring about a pure ground and the excellent rebirth of purity. The superior training in wisdom is able to bring about the virtue of definite excellence that is supramundane.

**Discrimination by concordant dharmas** means ten kinds of dharma that accord with the trainings: (1) prior cause, (2) conformity with teaching, (3) principled skillful means, (4) uninterrupted and earnest cultivation, (5) fierce aspiration, (6) the power of cultivation and maintenance, (7) pacification of bodily and mental heaviness, (8) repeated observation, (9) fearlessness, (10) absence of excessive pride. **Prior cause** means: the ripening and completion of faculties from previous lives. **Conformity with teaching** means: non-inverted, sequential teaching. **Principled skillful means** means: practicing in accord with the teaching; when one thus practices, one is able to generate right view. **Uninterrupted and earnest cultivation** means: during such skillful practice, not allowing any time to pass in vain, cultivating wholesome practices with sincerity and swiftly generating wholesome practices. **Fierce aspiration** means: regarding the supreme liberation, generating the aspiration and desire to realize it, thinking: "When shall I dwell in the complete fulfillment as the noble ones dwell?" **The power of cultivation and maintenance** means: the power of cultivation and maintenance is obtained through two causes: (1) because one's faculties are by nature keen, (2) because of long and thoroughly ripened cultivation. **Pacification of bodily and mental heaviness** means: if heaviness of body and mind arises through physical fatigue, one should gently change one's posture to achieve pacification. If heaviness of body and mind arises through excessive investigation and analysis, one should internally cultivate quiescence to achieve pacification. If heaviness of body and mind arises through straining the mind with excessive restraint, or through a sinking mind, or through torpor and sleepiness, one should cultivate wisdom-contemplation and the pure, superior mental attention to achieve pacification. If heaviness of body and mind follows and does not depart due to the uneliminated afflictions and affliction-concordant factors of one's own nature, one should correctly cultivate the noble path to achieve pacification.

**Repeated observation** means: relying on morality, one repeatedly observes, knowing in accord with reality what has been wrongly done and what has been rightly done. If a wrong action was not committed, one should not relinquish it; if a right action was not committed, one should relinquish it. If a wrong action was committed, one should relinquish it; if a right action was committed, one should not relinquish it. Through the predominant power of such observing mental attention, one repeatedly observes whether all afflictions have been severed or not. If one knows they have been severed, one should deeply rejoice. If one knows they have not been severed, one should repeatedly cultivate the counteracting path. **Fearlessness** means: regarding the dharmas that should be known and observed from time to time, because of not knowing, not observing, or not entering realization, fear arises, and the mind becomes wilted and exhausted. When this repeatedly arises, one does not cling to it but removes, severs, and relinquishes it. **Absence of excessive pride** means: regarding what has been attained, realized, and verified, one is free from excessive pride and does not grasp in an inverted manner. Regarding what has been attained, one generates the perception of having attained it; regarding what has been realized, one generates the perception of having realized it; regarding what has been verified, one generates the perception of having verified it. These ten dharmas, for those who delight in correctly cultivating the training rules, accord with the training rules in the beginning, middle, and end; therefore they are called dharmas that accord with the trainings. Among these ten dharmas, the first, prior cause, accords most supremely with the superior training in morality; the remaining nine accord most supremely with the superior training in mind and the superior training in wisdom.

**Discrimination by persons** means: these three trainings pervade both ordinary beings and those who have seen the truths.

**Discrimination by inferior, intermediate, and superior** means: by practice and by skillful means. **By practice** means: the practice of painful progress with slow penetration is called the inferior training. The practice of painful progress with swift penetration and the practice of pleasant progress with slow penetration are called the intermediate training. The practice of pleasant progress with swift penetration is called the superior training. **By skillful means** means: one who cultivates without earnest skillful means and without uninterrupted skillful means is called the inferior training. One who cultivates with one of the two skillful means is called the intermediate training. One who cultivates with both skillful means is called the superior training.

**Discrimination by yoga** means: relying on four kinds of yoga, one correctly trains in the training rules: (1) faith, (2) aspiration, (3) right exertion, (4) skillful means.

**Faith** has two aspects and two bases. The two aspects are: (1) the aspect of acceptance, (2) the aspect of purity. The two bases are: (1) the basis of observing the principles of the Dharma, (2) the basis of having faith in the majestic power of persons. **Aspiration** has four kinds: (1) aspiration for realization, (2) aspiration for questioning and discussion, (3) aspiration for the provisions of realization, (4) aspiration for the skillful cultivation of means. **Aspiration for realization** means: as when a practitioner generates the aspiration and desire to realize the supreme liberation, as extensively explained above. **Aspiration for questioning and discussion** means: as when a practitioner, having generated the aspiration for realization, goes to a monastery, approaches the knowledgeable, those who share the holy life, and those who possess correct practice and knowledge, for the purpose of hearing what has not been heard and for the clarification of what has been heard. **Aspiration for the provisions of realization** means: as when a practitioner generates the aspiration and desire to realize purity of morality and restraint, moderation in eating, the skillful means of wakefulness, clear knowledge in abiding, and advancement. **Aspiration for the skillful cultivation of means** means: generating the aspiration, desire, and wish for realization regarding uninterrupted skillful means, earnest skillful means, and the cultivation of the noble path.

**Right exertion** has four kinds: (1) for the sake of hearing the Dharma, (2) for the sake of reflection, (3) for the sake of cultivation, (4) for the sake of purifying obstructions. **For the sake of hearing the Dharma** means: for the purpose of hearing what has not been heard and for the clarification of what has been heard, one stimulates the mind and skillfully cultivates in areas not yet mastered. **For the sake of reflection** means: regarding the Dharma as heard, alone in a quiet place, one reflects, assesses, observes, and investigates its meaning. **For the sake of cultivation** means: dwelling in a quiet chamber, one repeatedly cultivates calming and contemplation. **For the sake of purifying obstructions** means: for the purpose of purifying the hindrances, day and night one is diligent in walking meditation and seated meditation, stimulating the mind and skillfully cultivating in areas not yet mastered.

**Skillful means** also has four kinds: through the predominant power of guarding morality and guarding the sense-faculties, one causes the restraint of the faculties to be purified and one well abides in mindfulness. Through well abiding in mindfulness, one attains non-heedlessness, guarding the mind and cultivating wholesome dharmas. Through non-heedlessness, one causes the inner mind to correspond with calming, and one attains the superior wisdom of contemplating dharmas. These four kinds of yoga are divided into sixteen practices. Herein, through faith, one trusts that one will attain the meaning. Through trusting that one will attain the meaning, one generates the aspiration to cultivate regarding all wholesome dharmas. Through the aspiration to cultivate, one is diligent day and night, abiding in stimulation, firmness, and courage. Through right exertion, one gathers and cultivates skillful means, for the purpose of attaining what has not yet been attained, realizing what has not yet been realized, and verifying what has not yet been verified. Therefore, these four dharmas are called yoga.

**Discrimination by mental attention** means the seven kinds of mental attention beginning with discerning characteristics. **The mental attention of discerning characteristics** (lakṣaṇa-pratyavekṣā-manaskāra): through this mental attention, one discerns the coarse characteristics of the desire realm and the quiescent characteristics of the first dhyāna. How does one discern the coarse characteristics of the desire realm? Through six kinds of investigation of the faults of sensual desires: (1) meaning, (2) objects, (3) characteristics, (4) categories, (5) time, (6) principle.

**Investigation of meaning** means: one investigates that sensual desires have many faults, many afflictions, many plagues, and many calamities. That these sensual desires have many faults through to many calamities is the coarse meaning.

**Investigation of objects** means: one either generates desire regarding internal desires or generates desire regarding external desires.

**Investigation of characteristics** means: one investigates both specific and shared characteristics. **Investigation of specific characteristics** means: "These are afflictive desires; these are objective desires." Such desires either accord with pleasure, or accord with suffering, or accord with neither pleasure nor suffering. Those that accord with pleasure are the basis of sensual desire and the basis of the inversion of perception and mind. Those that accord with suffering are the basis of aversion, anger, and resentment. Those that accord with neither pleasure nor suffering are the basis of concealment, vexation, deceit, flattery, shamelessness, and recklessness, and the basis of the inversion of views. Such desires are either accompanied by violent feeling or by non-violent feeling. This is called the investigation of the specific characteristics of desires. **Shared characteristics** means: one investigates that sensual desires are equally and uniformly pursued and bound by the suffering of birth through to the suffering of not obtaining what one seeks. Those who enjoy desires, even though desires are abundantly provided, should understand that these are dharmas of the suffering of birth and so forth, and that these abundantly provided desires will momentarily deteriorate. This is called the investigation of the shared characteristics of desires.

**Investigation of categories** means: one investigates that sensual desires fall into the category of darkness, like a chain of dry bones, like a piece of putrid flesh, like a grass torch, like a pit of burning coals, like a venomous viper, like what is seen in a dream, like borrowed ornamental adornments, like fruit at the top of a tree. Furthermore, one investigates that all sentient beings suffer the suffering caused by pursuit, the suffering caused by the separation of loved ones, the suffering caused by insatiability, the suffering caused by lack of autonomy, and the suffering caused by misconduct. Furthermore, the Blessed One says: "I declare that the pursuit of sensual desires has five dangers: (1) sensual desires have little savor, (2) those who pursue desires have much suffering and many faults, (3) those who pursue desires are insatiable, unsatisfied, without rest and without cessation, (4) those who pursue desires cause the fetters to grow, (5) those who pursue desires commit every kind of evil." Furthermore, the intelligent, correct, and good, through immeasurable approaches, censure sensual desires: "These desires increase defilement and are insatiable; they are shared by all and are inverted and contrary to the Dharma. They are the cause of misconduct and increase craving for desires. The wise abandon them far away, for they are swiftly scattered and destroyed. Relying on conditions, they are grounds for heedlessness. Their nature is impermanent, empty, and void. They are dharmas of deception and loss, like magical illusions and transformations, deceiving the foolish. Whether desires of the present life or desires of future lives, whether heavenly desires or human desires, all are the domain of Māra, the abode of Māra. Furthermore, relying on those desires, the mind gives rise to immeasurable, various kinds of unwholesome dharmas, namely desire, aversion, resentment, and other obstructive dharmas that obstruct the noble disciples when they train in the training rules." Through such and other differential faults, one mostly investigates that sensual desires fall into the category of darkness. This is called investigation of categories.

**Investigation of time** means: in the past, future, and present, constantly and continuously, one investigates that sensual desires have many afflictions, many calamities, and many faults. This is called investigation of time.

**Investigation of principle** means: these sensual desires require great resources, great pursuit, and great labor, and furthermore through various kinds of mixed occupations, only then are they fully obtained, established, and increased. Even though external material possessions are thus increased and fulfilled, by the nature of things they are swiftly scattered and destroyed. Furthermore, one's parents, wife and children, servants, workers, friends, officials, brothers, and relatives, though briefly gathered together, are before long scattered and destroyed. Furthermore, the coarse form of the inner body, produced by the four great elements, nourished by rice and gruel, constantly emitting filth, though temporarily tended through bathing, massage, and so forth, with the suffering that arises thereby, is ultimately a dharma of separation, scattering, and destruction. To treat the suffering of hunger and thirst, one receives food and drink. To treat the suffering of cold and heat, and to cover what is shameful, one receives and keeps clothing. To treat the suffering oppressed by torpor and sleep, and to counteract the fatigue of walking and standing, one receives bedding. To treat the suffering of various illnesses, one receives medicine. All these desires are merely for the treatment of suffering and should not be clung to, except as medicine taken by one who is gravely ill. One may investigate such coarse characteristics of desires in reliance on the sacred teaching, or through one's own internally generated knowledge and vision, or through investigating what accords with principle, or through investigating that the intrinsic nature of desires has been established since beginningless time as a matter of course, being an inconceivable dharma that should not be thought about and should not be discriminated. This is called investigation of principle.

Having thus discerned the coarse characteristics of desires through these six kinds of investigation, one further discerns the quiescent characteristics of the first dhyāna: within the first dhyāna, there are no extremely coarse and heavy characteristics like those of the desire realm. Because one has separated from such coarse characteristics of desire, this is called the quiescent characteristics of the first dhyāna. This is called discerning the quiescent characteristics of the first dhyāna. Through the mental attention of the meditative ground, one discerns the coarse characteristics of the desire realm and the quiescent characteristics of the first dhyāna. This is called the mental attention of discerning characteristics. Herein, there is still intermixture with hearing and reflection: one should understand this.

**The mental attention of resolve** (adhimokṣa-manaskāra): as appropriate, having investigated and discerned the coarse characteristics of the desire realm and the quiescent characteristics of the first dhyāna, without intermixture with hearing and reflection, one purely gives rise to the resolve of cultivation. While cultivating calming and contemplation with the coarse and quiescent characteristics as objects, one repeatedly gives rise to the resolve regarding the coarse and quiescent characteristics as investigated. Therefore it is called the mental attention of resolve.

**The mental attention of separation** (prāvivekyа-manaskāra): through abundantly cultivating this type, for the purpose of severing the initial portion of afflictions, one gives rise to the counteracting path. The mental attention that arises together with the counteracting path that can sever afflictions is called the mental attention of separation.

**The mental attention of gathering delight** (ratisaṃgrāhaka-manaskāra): having already severed the initial portion of afflictions of the desire realm and having already separated from the heaviness of that category, regarding the subsequent, superior severance and separation, one gives rise to delight. Furthermore, seeing the superior meritorious qualities at the place of severance, one verifies a portion of the joy and delight of separation. From time to time, through the pure, superior mental attention, one rejoices, for the purpose of severing the hindrances of torpor, sleepiness, and agitation. This is called the mental attention of gathering delight.

**The mental attention of examination** (mīmāṃsā-manaskāra): having thus correctly cultivated with the delight of severance and the delight of cultivation, supported by the skillful means of wholesome practices, the active afflictions bound to the desire realm no longer manifest whether one is in action or at rest. The practitioner then reflects: "In this body of mine, is there still desire or is there no desire? Do I not grasp at objects of desire?" For the purpose of self-examination, one directs one's attention to a particular object and contemplates its excellent and pure characteristics. Because the practitioner has not yet completely severed all latent tendencies, when contemplating such pure and excellent characteristics, one follows toward habitual defilement, tends toward habitual defilement, and approaches habitual defilement, not abiding in equanimity and not generating disgust, opposition, or rejection. The practitioner then knows: "I have not yet correctly separated from desires. My mind is not yet liberated. Therefore, the course of desire still binds and holds my mind. Just as water by its nature restrains and subdues, I now must doubly cultivate the counteracting path, so that the remaining latent tendencies may be severed without remainder." One then redoubles one's delight in superior severance and superior cultivation. This is called the mental attention of examination.

**The mental attention of the ultimate skillful means** (prayoga-niṣṭhā-manaskāra): through redoubled cultivation of the calming aspect, with delight in severance and delight in cultivation, one dually cultivates calming and contemplation, repeatedly examining. The practitioner thus cultivates the counteraction, examining from time to time what has been severed and what has not, causing the mind to be separated from the bondage of the afflictions of the desire realm. This is a temporary suppressive separation, not the ultimate permanent uprooting of seeds. The practitioner at this point attains the preparatory path of the first dhyāna, the ultimate counteracting mental attention for all afflictions. This is called the mental attention of the ultimate skillful means.

**The mental attention of the fruit of the ultimate skillful means** (prayoga-niṣṭhā-phala-manaskāra): immediately after this, through the preceding causal conditions, one enters and verifies the fundamental first dhyāna concentration. The mental attention that arises together with this fundamental first dhyāna is called the mental attention of the fruit of the ultimate skillful means. As the first dhyāna has seven kinds of mental attention, so too with the second, third, and fourth dhyānas through to the concentration of the station of neither-perception-nor-non-perception: one should understand each as appropriate.

Furthermore, **the coarse characteristics** means: regarding all lower grounds, from the desire realm through to the station of nothingness, there are briefly three kinds of lower-ground dharmas to be wearied of, one should understand: (1) the nature of an extremely suffering abiding, (2) the nature of an extremely non-quiescent abiding, (3) the nature of an extremely short-lived abiding.

**Discrimination by generation** means four kinds of generation: (1) generation of fruits, (2) generation of dispassion, (3) generation of transformation of faculties, (4) generation of superior meritorious qualities.

**Discrimination by question and answer** means: immeasurable question-and-answer discriminations; we now reveal a few. Question: How is the superior training in morality impure, and how is it pure? Answer: There are ten causes through which morality is impure. What are the ten? (1) Initially not receiving the morality-restraint in accord with the Dharma. (2) Extreme sinking. (3) Extreme scattering. (4) Being included in heedlessness and laziness. (5) Generating wrong aspirations. (6) Being included in transgressing deportment. (7) Being included in transgressing into wrong livelihood. (8) Falling into the two extremes. (9) Inability to achieve liberation. (10) Transgressing what was received.

**Initially not receiving the morality-restraint in accord with the Dharma** means: suppose there is a person who, to avoid the oppression of a king, the oppression of bandits, the oppression of creditors, through fear, or through the fear of not being able to sustain oneself, goes forth and receives the precepts, not for the sake of cultivating the noble path, not for the sake of the purity of the holy life, not for the sake of self-discipline, not for the sake of self-quiescence, not for the sake of self-nirvāṇa. Such and other cases are receiving the morality-restraint not in accord with the Dharma.

**Extreme sinking** means: suppose there is a person without shame or conscience, with feeble remorse, sluggish by nature, who cultivates the training rules with sluggishness. This is called extreme sinking.

**Extreme scattering** means: suppose there is a person who, through clinging to what was received, generates remorse where it is not warranted, and regarding others, in inappropriate situations, generates contempt and harmful intentions, repeatedly practicing without relinquishing. This is called extreme scattering.

**Being included in heedlessness and laziness** means: suppose there is a person who, regarding past transgressions, due to forgetfulness, does not apply the counteraction in accord with the Dharma; likewise regarding future and present transgressions, due to forgetfulness, does not apply the counteraction in accord with the Dharma. One has not previously generated the fierce aspiration: "I shall never transgress the precepts," thinking: "I shall act thus and thus, abide thus and thus, as I should act and as I should abide, so as to commit no transgression." Furthermore, when one acts thus and abides thus, where one might transgress, one does transgress. This person, in the initial, middle, and final periods, and in the time of conducting prior activities, consistently manifests heedlessness. Furthermore, one clings to sleep and rest as pleasurable, is sluggish and lazy, does not delight in cultivating, and does not bodily attend upon the wise in the holy life. This is called being included in heedlessness and laziness.

**Generating wrong aspirations** means: suppose there is a person who generates wrong aspirations in practicing the holy life, thinking: "Through the morality, precepts, and holy life I now cultivate, may I become the lord of the gods or some other god." Or one desires material gains and honor, seeking material gains and honor from others, or merely wishing for material gains and honor. This is called generating wrong aspirations.

**Being included in transgressing deportment** means: suppose there is a person who, regarding deportment, activities, or the skillful cultivation of wholesome practices as previously described, acts in violation of the world and in transgression of the Vinaya. This is called being included in transgressing deportment.

**Being included in transgressing into wrong livelihood** means: suppose there is a person who by nature has great desires and is not content, who is difficult to support and difficult to satisfy, and who seeks all robes, food, bedding, medicine, and other necessities through improper means rather than through the Dharma. For the sake of obtaining robes, food, and so forth, in order to display one's own meritorious qualities, one fraudulently manifests before others a deportment that is not one's own nature or habitual practice, and manifests the quiescence of the sense-faculties without agitation, intending that others will think one possesses virtue and will make offerings and provisions of robes, food, bedding, medicine, and other necessities and bodily service. Furthermore, this person's appearance is rough and offensive, their speech is coarse and intimidating, without restraint. They adorn themselves and extol their own name and lineage, or their learning, or their broad mastery of the Dharma, for the sake of gaining material support and respect, and they teach others the Dharma proclaimed by the buddhas and their disciples, or they themselves declare their own meritorious qualities, whether real or slightly exaggerated. Or they cause others to proclaim extraordinary signs, seeking superior robes, food, and various necessities of an ascetic. Even though their robes are not deficient, they deliberately wear tattered robes, intending that faithful householders and elders will know their deficiency and give them superior robes. As with robes, so too with other necessities of the ascetic life. Furthermore, when they do not obtain what they desire from faithful brāhmaṇas and elders, whether because the item does not exist or because it is in use and cannot be given, they coerce, scold, and demand. Or when they receive something inferior, they denigrate and return it, speaking thus before the donor: "Shame on you, good man! Other good men and good women, even those of lower lineage and poorer means than you, are able to give such-and-such wonderful and pleasing things. How much more should you, of higher lineage and greater wealth, rather than giving me such contemptible and hateful things!" And so forth. Whether through fraudulently manifesting deportment, through improper speech, through proclaiming extraordinary signs, through coercion and scolding, or through using material gain to attract more material gain, one improperly seeks robes, food, bedding, medicine, and other necessities rather than seeking them through the Dharma. This is called wrong livelihood. This is called being included in transgressing into wrong livelihood.

**Falling into the two extremes** means: suppose there is a person who delights in enjoying exquisite sensual pleasures, obtaining robes and other necessities from others, whether by proper or improper means, not seeing the danger and not knowing the escape, and enjoying them. This is one extreme. Furthermore, suppose there is a person who practices self-mortification, oppressing the body with immeasurable kinds of suffering, practicing various kinds of extreme ascetic precepts, whether dwelling among thorns, in ashes, on a pestle, on a plank, on a skull, or squatting, practicing squatting meditation, or tending a fire up to three times daily, or immersing in water up to three times daily, or standing on one foot and gazing at the sun as it turns, and such other practices of self-mortification. This is the second extreme. This is called falling into the two extremes.

**Inability to achieve liberation** means: suppose there is a person who grasps the view that through morality and other precepts alone, merely through the cultivation of morality and ascetic precepts, one will attain purity, liberation, and escape. Or one grasps the view that through well guarding and well purifying the precepts of non-Buddhist teachers, one will attain purity, liberation, and escape. Since both of these are not ultimately pure, one is unable to achieve liberation. This is called inability to achieve liberation.

**Transgressing what was received** means: suppose there is a person who is utterly without shame, who never respects the standards of an ascetic, who violates the precepts and practices unwholesome dharmas, who is internally rotten, who follows the lower stream, who is like a filthy snail-shell, who howls like a dog, who is not an ascetic but claims to be an ascetic, who has not lived the holy life but claims to have lived the holy life. This is called transgressing what was received. Through these ten causes, the superior training in morality is impure. If the opposite of these obtains, it is pure.

**The pure and impure meanings of the superior training in mind**: as explained in the section on pure dhyāna in the Compendium of Topics chapter. Furthermore, through the purity of this, the purity of the superior training in wisdom should also be understood. Such and other question-and-answer discriminations are immeasurable and limitless; one should understand them according to the meaning.

**The five discriminations of awakening** (bodhi) are: (1) lineage, (2) means, (3) time, (4) realization, (5) liberation.

**Lineage**: śrāvaka awakening depends on the lineage of dull faculties. Pratyekabuddha awakening depends on the lineage of intermediate faculties. Unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening depends on the lineage of keen faculties.

**Means**: śrāvaka awakening is through the skillful means of practicing the six bases of skillful knowledge. Pratyekabuddha awakening is mostly through the skillful means of practicing the profound dependent arising. Unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening is through the skillful means of the five sciences.

**Time**: śrāvaka awakening is obtained through a minimum of three lives of cultivation. Pratyekabuddha awakening is obtained through one hundred great kalpas of cultivation. Unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening is obtained through three great incalculable kalpas (asaṃkhyeya) of cultivation.

**Realization**: śrāvaka awakening is realized through a teacher. Pratyekabuddha awakening is realized without a teacher, solely aspiring for self-benefit. Unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening is realized without a teacher, for both self-benefit and the benefit of others.

**Liberation**: the transformation of the basis (āśraya-parāvṛtti) realized by śrāvaka awakening and pratyekabuddha awakening is liberation from the afflictive obstruction, included in the liberation-body. The transformation of the basis realized by unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening is liberation from all afflictive obstructions and cognitive obstructions, included in both the liberation-body and the dharma-body.

---

## Fascicle 8

### Chapter on Gathering the Meaning of Purity, Part Four

Having thus discriminated hearing, the refuges, and so forth, the next verse says:

> Noble practices, the unsurpassed vehicle, great awakening, and meritorious qualities,
> Divergent treatises, treatise-dharmas, and explanations: each of these, one should know, has many kinds.

**The treatise says**: The many kinds of noble practices (āryācāra) refer to four noble practices: (1) the practice of reaching the other shore (pāramitā), (2) the practice of the factors of awakening (bodhipakṣa), (3) the practice of supernatural powers (abhijñā), and (4) the practice of maturing sentient beings.

**The practice of reaching the other shore** refers to the ten pāramitās as previously explained. This is called the practice of reaching the other shore.

**The practice of the factors of awakening** refers to the four foundations of mindfulness (smṛtyupasthāna) and so forth, all thirty-seven factors of awakening (bodhipakṣa-dharma) as previously explained, together with the four investigations (paryeṣaṇā) and the four comprehensive knowledges in accord with reality (yathābhūta-parijñāna). This is called the practice of the factors of awakening.

**The practice of supernatural powers** refers to the six supernatural powers (abhijñā) as previously explained. This is called the practice of supernatural powers.

**The practice of maturing sentient beings** refers to the two kinds of immeasurables as previously explained: (1) the immeasurable of those to be disciplined and (2) the immeasurable of means of discipline. Furthermore, there are six aspects of maturation: (1) the substance of maturation itself, (2) those who are matured, (3) the differentiations of maturation, (4) the means of maturation, (5) those who are capable of maturing, and (6) the characteristics of those already matured. This is called the practice of maturing sentient beings.

**The many kinds of the unsurpassed vehicle** (anuttara-yāna) refer to five aspects of the Great Vehicle (mahāyāna): (1) the seed (bīja), (2) entering (avatāra), (3) the sequence (krama), (4) correct practice (samyak-pratipatti), and (5) the fruit of correct practice. These correspond, in their respective order, to the chapter on lineage (gotra), the chapter on generating the aspiration (cittotpāda), the chapter on stages (vihāra), and the remaining chapters together with the two chapters on establishing great awakening, all within the Bodhisattva Grounds (Bodhisattvabhūmi).

**The many kinds of great awakening** (mahābodhi) refer to five aspects of great awakening: (1) self-nature (svabhāva), (2) function (kāritra), (3) skillful means (upāya), (4) turning (pravṛtti), and (5) cessation (nirodha).

**Self-nature** refers to the transformation of the basis (āśraya-parāvṛtti) that surpasses anything attained by śrāvakas and pratyekabuddhas. This has four aspects: (1) the basis for arising, (2) the basis for non-arising, (3) the fruit of thoroughly observing the knowable, and (4) the pure characteristic of the dharma-realm (dharmadhātu).

*The basis for arising* means that the supramundane path within the continuum of a buddha arises in dependence on this transformation of the basis, and could not arise without it. If it could arise apart from this transformation of the basis, it should have already arisen before the transformation.

*The basis for non-arising* means that all afflictions (kleśa) and their habitual tendencies (vāsanā), in dependence on this transformation of the basis, no longer arise. If this were not so, then before the transformation of the basis, when all conditions converge, all afflictions and their habitual tendencies should already have been capable of permanent non-arising, which is not the case.

*The fruit of thoroughly observing the knowable* means that this transformation of the basis is the fruit of having thoroughly penetrated the true limit (bhūtakoṭi) of the knowable and the true suchness (tathatā) of the knowable. If this were not so, the self-substance of buddhas would require further knowing and further elimination.

*The pure characteristic of the dharma-realm* means that this transformation of the basis, being free from all characteristics, is revealed as the supremely pure dharma-realm. If this were not so, it would be an impermanent and conceivable dharma. Yet this transformation of the basis has the characteristic of permanence and is revealed as inconceivable and non-dual.

**The inconceivable nature** of this transformation of the basis furthermore has five aspects: (1) self-nature, (2) location, (3) abiding, (4) identity and difference, and (5) establishment of activity.

*Self-nature*: this transformation of the basis is identical with form yet apart from form: inconceivable. Likewise, it is identical with feeling, perception, formations, and consciousness yet apart from feeling, perception, formations, and consciousness: inconceivable. Whether identical with or apart from the earth element, water element, fire element, and wind element: inconceivable. Whether identical with or apart from the eye base, ear base, nose base, tongue base, body base, and mind base: inconceivable. Whether existent or non-existent and so forth: inconceivable.

*Location*: this transformation of the basis, whether in the desire realm or apart from the desire realm: inconceivable. Whether in the form or formless realms or apart from the form or formless realms: inconceivable. Whether among humans or among gods, whether present or apart: inconceivable. Whether in the world-systems of the ten directions or apart from them: inconceivable.

*Abiding*: this transformation of the basis abides in such-and-such an appearance and blissful abiding: an inconceivable abiding. It abides in such-and-such an appearance of śamatha: an inconceivable abiding. Whether abiding with mind or abiding without mind: an inconceivable abiding. Such-and-such an appearance of noble abiding: an inconceivable abiding. Such-and-such an appearance of divine abiding and brahmā abiding: inconceivable.

*Identity and difference*: all buddhas abide together in the single undefiled realm (anāsrava-dhātu). Whether they are identical or different: inconceivable.

*Establishment of activity*: all buddha-tathāgatas, equal in nature, equal in wisdom, power, and majestic virtue, abiding in the undefiled realm in dependence on the transformation of the basis, for the benefit of all sentient beings, establish such-and-such activities for the benefit of sentient beings: inconceivable. Furthermore, this is inconceivable for two reasons: (1) because its meaning transcends verbal expression, surpassing the path of language, it is inconceivable; (2) because its meaning is supramundane and without worldly comparison, it is inconceivable.

**Function** (kāritra): briefly stated, ten kinds of sovereign mastery (vaśitā) constitute the function. What are the ten? (1) Sovereign mastery over lifespan (āyur-vaśitā), (2) sovereign mastery over mind (citta-vaśitā), (3) sovereign mastery over requisites (pariṣkāra-vaśitā), (4) sovereign mastery over action (karma-vaśitā), (5) sovereign mastery over birth (upapatti-vaśitā), (6) sovereign mastery over aspiration (praṇidhāna-vaśitā), (7) sovereign mastery over resolve (adhimukti-vaśitā), (8) sovereign mastery over miraculous transformation (ṛddhi-vaśitā), (9) sovereign mastery over knowledge (jñāna-vaśitā), and (10) sovereign mastery over the Dharma (dharma-vaśitā).

**Skillful means** (upāya): briefly stated, four kinds of transformation (nirmāṇa) constitute the skillful means. (1) In order to mature those not yet matured, the buddha manifests the transformative appearances of the practices carried out by bodhisattvas. (2) In order to liberate those already matured, in the great trichiliocosm (trisāhasra-mahāsāhasra-lokadhātu), in one hundred koṭis of Jambudvīpas simultaneously, he manifests the transformative appearances of the Tathāgata. (3) As a skillful means of gathering and supporting, he manifests the transformative appearances of śrāvakas. (4) For those sentient beings who are to be disciplined by such means, he displays the transformative appearances of all pratyekabuddhas. The Blessed One, the Buddha, in these four kinds of transformative dharmas, performs unobstructed activity throughout the world-systems of the ten directions.

**Turning** (pravṛtti) has two kinds: (1) temporary turning and (2) ultimate turning.

*Temporary turning* means that as long as sentient beings are not yet matured and not yet liberated, the transformative turning of the buddha-tathāgatas does not cease.

*Ultimate turning* means that, like the inexhaustible and inconceivable majestic virtue and bright turning of all buddhas, it performs beneficial deeds for sentient beings, flowing on without cessation.

**Cessation** (nirodha) has two kinds: (1) temporary cessation and (2) ultimate cessation.

*Temporary cessation* means that for those sentient beings who are already matured and already liberated, the buddha-tathāgatas temporarily manifest the appearance of entering parinirvāṇa; this is not the ultimate cessation.

*Ultimate cessation* means the complete and permanent exhaustion of all afflictions, their habitual tendencies, and the suffering that serves as their basis.

**The many kinds of meritorious qualities** (guṇa): the summary verse says:

> The extraordinary and the non-extraordinary,
> Impartial mind and benefiting others,
> Repaying kindness and joyful praise,
> Unfailing skillful practice.

**The treatise says**: **The extraordinary** (viśeṣa) means that when bodhisattvas cultivate and study the unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening vehicle, there are five extraordinary dharmas. What are the five? (1) They give rise to affection for all sentient beings without cause or condition. (2) Solely for the benefit of sentient beings, they constantly dwell in saṃsāra and endure great suffering. (3) For sentient beings with many afflictions who are difficult to discipline, they skillfully comprehend the means of discipline. (4) They are able to awaken and enter the true meaning that is extremely difficult to understand. (5) They are endowed with inconceivable majestic virtue. Since these five dharmas are not shared by other sentient beings, they are called extraordinary.

**The non-extraordinary** (aviśeṣa) means that when bodhisattvas cultivate and study the unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening vehicle, there are five non-extraordinary dharmas. Because bodhisattva-mahāsattvas accomplish these five dharmas, the five extraordinary dharmas become manifest and accomplished. What are the five?

(1) Because bodhisattvas take the suffering that is the cause of benefiting others as their own happiness, they constantly and universally undertake the suffering that is the cause of benefiting others. This is the first non-extraordinary dharma.

(2) Although bodhisattvas thoroughly understand the faults of saṃsāra and the merits of nirvāṇa, they take as their own happiness the bringing of all sentient beings to ultimate purity. Therefore, through the dominant force of purifying sentient beings, bodhisattvas constantly vow to undertake and practice the dharmas of dwelling in saṃsāra. This is the second non-extraordinary dharma.

(3) Although bodhisattvas thoroughly understand the taste of the bliss of quiescence, they take as their own happiness the bringing of all sentient beings to ultimate purity. Therefore, through the dominant force of purifying sentient beings, bodhisattvas constantly and diligently employ skillful means to teach the Dharma. This is the third non-extraordinary dharma.

(4) Although bodhisattvas have already accumulated the roots of goodness of the six pāramitās, they take as their own happiness the bringing of all sentient beings to ultimate purity. Therefore, through the dominant force of purifying sentient beings, they give with pure intention and do not expect the maturation of the fruit of giving in return. This is the fourth non-extraordinary dharma.

(5) Because bodhisattvas take the benefiting of others as their own self-benefit, they constantly appear to undertake and carry out the deeds of benefiting all sentient beings. This is the fifth non-extraordinary dharma.

Because bodhisattva-mahāsattvas accomplish these five dharmas, the five extraordinary dharmas become manifest and accomplished.

**Impartial mind** (samacitta) means that bodhisattvas have five kinds of impartial mind toward all sentient beings.

(1) When bodhisattva-mahāsattvas first generate the aspiration, just as they give rise to the great vow for the sake of attaining great awakening, so too they give rise to an impartial mind for the benefit of all sentient beings.

(2) Bodhisattva-mahāsattvas give rise to an impartial mind accompanied by compassion toward all sentient beings.

(3) Bodhisattva-mahāsattvas give rise to an impartial mind accompanied by love for all sentient beings as for their only child.

(4) Bodhisattva-mahāsattvas, knowing that the conditioned formations arising from various conditions are imagined sentient-being affairs, comprehend that the dharma-nature (dharmatā) of one sentient being is precisely the dharma-nature of all sentient beings, and give rise to an impartial mind accompanied by the knowledge of equal dharma-nature toward all sentient beings.

(5) Bodhisattva-mahāsattvas, just as they perform beneficial deeds for one sentient being, so too they perform beneficial deeds for all sentient beings. Therefore they give rise to an impartial mind accompanied by the wish to perform beneficial deeds toward all sentient beings.

These are the five kinds of impartial mind that bodhisattva-mahāsattvas give rise to toward all sentient beings.

**Benefiting** (arthakṛtyā) means that the bodhisattva-mahāsattvas' performance of all beneficial deeds for sentient beings has five characteristics. What are the five?

(1) For those in harmful and damaging circumstances, they teach the dharma of right livelihood and thereby attract and guide them.

(2) For those who do not follow what is truly beneficial, they teach the dharma of accordance and thereby attract and guide them.

(3) For sentient beings who are without support, suffering, impoverished, and without a protector, they serve as their support and protector and thereby attract and guide them.

(4) They teach the path leading to fortunate destinies and thereby attract and guide them.

(5) They teach the path of the three vehicles leading to nirvāṇa and thereby attract and guide them.

**Repaying kindness** (kṛtajñatā) means that bodhisattva-mahāsattvas, toward sentient beings who have been kind to them, give rise to five kinds of reciprocal benefit. What are the five?

(1) They establish sentient beings and cause them to study their own virtuous qualities.

(2) Through skillful means they establish them and cause them to study the virtuous qualities of others.

(3) For those without support, without a protector, suffering, and impoverished, they serve as their support and protector.

(4) They encourage them to make offerings to the buddha-tathāgatas.

(5) They cause them to copy the Dharma proclaimed by the Buddha, and to receive, uphold, and make offerings to it.

**Joyful praise** (anumodanā) means there are five areas in which bodhisattva-mahāsattvas should constantly rejoice and praise. What are the five?

(1) Encountering the appearance of a buddha in the world and being able to attend upon him.

(2) Constantly hearing from the Tathāgata the Dharma of the bodhisattva treasury (bodhisattvavaipulyapiṭaka) that accords with the six pāramitās.

(3) Being capable of cultivating and practicing the deeds of maturing all sentient beings.

(4) Swiftly realizing unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening.

(5) After realizing awakening, having a great assembly of bodhisattvas and śrāvakas in harmony.

**Unfailing skillful practice** (amogha-upāya-caryā) means that bodhisattva-mahāsattvas have five kinds of unfailing, skillful, and superior practice for benefiting sentient beings. What are the five?

(1) Bodhisattva-mahāsattvas, toward all sentient beings, have the intention of benefiting and bringing happiness as their foremost concern.

(2) Bodhisattva-mahāsattvas accomplish non-inverted awareness and truly understand deeds of benefiting and bringing happiness as they really are.

(3) Through appropriate skillful means they teach various dharmas, causing sentient beings, according to their capacity, to all become disciplined. Only the Tathāgata can ultimately accomplish this fully.

(4) Bodhisattva-mahāsattvas at all times have minds without weariness or fatigue.

(5) Bodhisattva-mahāsattvas, with impartial great compassion (mahākaruṇā), toward sentient beings of lower, intermediate, and superior capacities, have minds without partiality.

The next summary verse says:

> Non-inverted skillful means, regression and advancement,
> Simulated and genuine meritorious qualities,
> And the skillful disciplining of sentient beings.

**The treatise says**: **Non-inverted skillful means** (aviparyasta-upāya) means that bodhisattva-mahāsattvas have five kinds of skillful means that encompass all correct skillful means. What are the five? (1) The skillful means of guarding and protecting (anurakṣaṇa), (2) the skillful means of being without fault (nirdoṣa), (3) the skillful means of the power of discernment (vicāra-bala), (4) the skillful means of pure superior intention (viśuddha-adhyāśaya), and (5) the skillful means of entering the determined (niyāma-avatāra).

*The skillful means of guarding and protecting* means that bodhisattva-mahāsattvas well guard their intelligence: through innate wisdom (sahaja-jñāna) they swiftly apprehend the Dharma. They also well guard their memory: through memory, the Dharma they have apprehended is retained without being lost. They also well guard their wisdom: through wisdom, they thoroughly observe the meaning of the Dharma they have apprehended and penetrate it with correct insight. This is because they keep far from the causes that lead to the regression of intelligence, memory, and wisdom, and because they closely cultivate and practice the causes that accord with the abiding and advancing stages. They also well guard their own minds, through well protecting and guarding the gates of the sense-faculties. They also well guard the minds of others, through correct skillful means of protecting others' minds.

*The skillful means of being without fault* means that bodhisattva-mahāsattvas have non-inverted vigor toward all wholesome dharmas, immeasurable and uninterrupted, dedicated to awakening.

*The skillful means of the power of discernment* pertains to the ground of resolve and practice (adhimukti-caryā-bhūmi).

*The skillful means of pure superior intention* pertains to the ground of pure superior intention (śuddha-adhyāśaya-bhūmi) and the ground of correct practice (samyak-pratipatti-bhūmi).

*The skillful means of entering the determined* pertains to the determined ground (niyata-bhūmi), the ground of determined practice (niyata-caryā-bhūmi), and the ground of reaching the ultimate (niṣṭhāgamana-bhūmi).

These five kinds of skillful means encompass all correct skillful means of bodhisattvas.

**Regression** (hāni) means that bodhisattvas have five dharmas leading to regression. (1) Not respecting the correct Dharma and its teachers. (2) Heedlessness and laziness. (3) Habituating oneself to afflictions. (4) Habituating oneself to unwholesome actions. (5) Comparing oneself with other bodhisattvas, giving rise to the conceit of superiority and inferiority, and giving rise to the conceit of superiority with respect to inverted dharmas.

**Advancement** (viśeṣa) means that bodhisattvas have five dharmas leading to advancement, which are, in their respective order, the reversal of the above five dark dharmas.

**Simulated meritorious qualities** (pratirūpaka-guṇa) means that bodhisattvas have five kinds of simulated meritorious qualities that are in reality faults. (1) Toward sentient beings who are violent, wicked, and who violate the precepts, they use this as a reason to cause them harm. (2) They fraudulently display the appearance of complete deportment. (3) Through worldly embellished compositions and the treatises of non-Buddhist teachings and their corresponding dharmas, they gain inclusion among the wise and intelligent. (4) They cultivate and practice giving and other wholesome deeds that involve transgression. (5) They proclaim and establish a simulated correct Dharma and cause it to spread widely.

**Genuine meritorious qualities** (bhūta-guṇa) means that bodhisattvas have five kinds of genuine meritorious qualities. (1) Toward sentient beings who are violent, wicked, and who violate the precepts, they use this as a reason to give rise to intensified compassion. (2) They are naturally endowed with complete deportment. (3) Through the pure and true teachings and realization proclaimed by the Tathāgata, they gain inclusion among the wise and intelligent. (4) They cultivate and practice giving and other wholesome deeds that are without transgression. (5) They reveal the correct Dharma and counter the simulated version.

**Skillful disciplining of sentient beings** (vinaya) means that bodhisattvas, in ten areas, without inversion, discipline the sentient beings to be transformed.

(1) In the area of separating from unwholesome actions.
(2) In the area of separating from sensual desire.
(3) In the area of non-transgression, and of emerging from transgressions already committed.
(4) In the area of guarding and protecting all the gates of the sense-faculties.
(5) In the area of abiding with clear knowing (samprajāna).
(6) In the area of separating from commotion and bustle.
(7) In the area of being far removed from all unwholesome discursive thoughts (vitarka).
(8) In the area of separating from obstructions.
(9) In the area of separating from the fetters of afflictions (kleśa-paryavasthāna).
(10) In the area of separating from the coarse heaviness of the affliction class.

The next summary verse says:

> The prediction of bodhisattvas,
