> Falling within the determined number,
> What must definitely be done and what should always be done,
> And the supreme dharmas: these should be known.

**The treatise says**: **The prediction of bodhisattvas** (vyākaraṇa) means that bodhisattvas, at six stages, receive from the tathāgatas the prediction of unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening.

(1) At the stage of abiding in the lineage, before generating the aspiration for awakening.
(2) After having generated the aspiration for awakening.
(3) While dwelling in manifest presence (pratyakṣa).
(4) While dwelling in non-manifest presence (aparokṣa).
(5) With a time limit: stating that within such-and-such a time one will realize unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening.
(6) Without a time limit: without specifying a definite time.

**Falling within the determined number** (niyāma-patita) means that bodhisattvas have three positions of falling within determination.

(1) *Determination by lineage* (gotra-niyāma): bodhisattvas who abide at the stage of the lineage already fall within the determined number of bodhisattvas. Why? Because bodhisattvas who possess the lineage, if they encounter favorable conditions, are necessarily capable of realizing anuttarā samyaksaṃbodhi.

(2) *Determination by generating the aspiration* (cittotpāda-niyāma): bodhisattvas who have generated the determined mind toward anuttarā samyaksaṃbodhi will, until they realize unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening, never regress.

(3) *Determination of unfailing practice* (amogha-caryā-niyāma): bodhisattvas who have attained sovereign mastery, whatever bodhisattva practices they wish to cultivate and carry out, none will be in vain. Based on this final determined position, the Tathāgata bestows upon bodhisattvas the prediction of falling within determination.

**What must definitely be done** (niyata-karaṇīya) means that bodhisattvas, in five areas, must definitely act; if they do not, they will certainly be incapable of realizing unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening. What are the five?

(1) Generating the aspiration for awakening (bodhicittotpāda).
(2) Giving rise to compassion toward all sentient beings.
(3) Courageous and vigorous exertion.
(4) Cultivating and practicing skillful means in the five sciences (pañca-vidyā).
(5) Having a mind without weariness or fatigue.

**What should always be done** (sadā-karaṇīya) means that bodhisattvas, in five areas, should always cultivate and act.

(1) One should always cultivate and practice the conduct of non-heedlessness (apramāda).
(2) For sentient beings without support, without a protector, and who are suffering, one should serve as their support and protector.
(3) One should always cultivate and practice the conduct of making offerings to buddhas.
(4) One should always fully understand error and non-error.
(5) In all actions, whether moving or dwelling, in all mental attentions (manaskāra), one should always cultivate and act with the mind of great awakening (bodhicitta) as the foremost guide.

**The supreme dharmas** (agrya-dharma) means that bodhisattvas, with regard to ten kinds of concordant supreme dharmas, should uphold and establish them as the most excellent. What are the ten?

(1) The bodhisattva lineage (gotra) is the most extraordinary among all lineages.
(2) The initial generation of the aspiration for awakening (bodhicittotpāda) is the most extraordinary among all correct vows.
(3) Diligent prajñā is the most extraordinary among all pāramitās.
(4) Loving speech (priyavāditā) is the most extraordinary among all means of gathering (saṃgraha-vastu).
(5) The Tathāgata is the most extraordinary among all sentient beings.
(6) Compassion (karuṇā) is the most extraordinary among all immeasurables (apramāṇa).
(7) The fourth dhyāna is the most extraordinary among all dhyānas.
(8) The emptiness samādhi (śūnyatā-samādhi) is the most extraordinary among the three samādhis.
(9) The attainment of cessation (nirodha-samāpatti) is the most extraordinary among the nine sequential attainments (anupūrva-samāpatti).
(10) Purified skillful means (pariśuddha-upāya-kauśalya) is the most extraordinary among all skillful means.

The next summary verse says:

> The various establishments and institutions,
> Investigation of all dharmas,
> Comprehensive knowledge in accord with reality,
> And the various immeasurables,
> Proclamation of the fruit and its benefits,
> The nature of the Great Vehicle and its encompassment,
> The ten things to know about bodhisattvas,
> And the establishment of their names and titles.

**The treatise says**: **The various establishments and institutions** (vyavasthāpana) refer to the four kinds of establishment and institution of bodhisattvas. Only tathāgatas and bodhisattvas are able to correctly establish and institute these; no other being, whether god, human, śramaṇa, or brāhmaṇa, is able to establish and institute them, unless they have stolen the Buddha's Dharma and placed it within their own treatises. What are the four?

(1) **The establishment of the Dharma** (dharma-vyavasthāpana): the sequential compilation, sequential arrangement, and sequential composition of the twelve divisions of teaching beginning with the sūtras. This is called the establishment of the Dharma.

(2) **The establishment of the truths** (satya-vyavasthāpana): one may establish one truth, since there is only one by the meaning of non-deception, without a second. Or one may establish two truths: (i) conventional truth (saṃvṛti-satya) and (ii) ultimate truth (paramārtha-satya). Or three truths: (i) the truth of characteristics (lakṣaṇa), (ii) the truth of designations (prajñapti), and (iii) the truth of function (kriyā). Or four truths: (i) the truth of suffering (duḥkha), (ii) the truth of the origin (samudaya), (iii) the truth of cessation (nirodha), and (iv) the truth of the path (mārga). Or five truths: (i) the truth of the cause, (ii) the truth of the fruit, (iii) the truth of what can know, (iv) the truth of what is known, and (v) the truth of non-duality. Or six truths: (i) the truth of the real, (ii) the truth of the false, (iii) the truth of what should be known, (iv) the truth of what should be abandoned, (v) the truth of what should be realized, and (vi) the truth of what should be cultivated. Or seven truths: (i) the truth of relishing, (ii) the truth of danger, (iii) the truth of escape, (iv) the truth of dharma-nature, (v) the truth of resolve, (vi) noble truth, and (vii) ignoble truth. Or eight truths: (i) the truth of the suffering of formations, (ii) the truth of the suffering of decay, (iii) the truth of the suffering of suffering, (iv) the truth of continued flowing, (v) the truth of the cessation of flowing, (vi) the truth of defilement, (vii) the truth of purification, and (viii) the truth of correct skillful means. Or nine truths: (i) the truth of impermanence, (ii) the truth of suffering, (iii) the truth of emptiness, (iv) the truth of non-self, (v) the truth of craving for existence, (vi) the truth of craving for non-existence, (vii) the truth of the means of eliminating those, (viii) the truth of nirvāṇa with remainder, and (ix) the truth of nirvāṇa without remainder. Or ten truths: (i) the truth of oppressive suffering, (ii) the truth of the suffering of inadequate enjoyment, (iii) the truth of the suffering of the contrariety of realm-natures, (iv) the truth of the suffering of the destruction of what is loved, (v) the truth of the suffering of coarse heaviness, (vi) the truth of action (karma), (vii) the truth of affliction, (viii) the truth of hearing the correct Dharma and attending with proper mental engagement, (ix) the truth of correct view, and (x) the truth of the fruit of correct view. This is called the bodhisattvas' establishment of the truths. If extensively discriminated, the kinds are immeasurable.

(3) **The establishment of reasonings** (yukti-vyavasthāpana): there are four kinds of reasoning: (i) the reasoning of dependence (apekṣā-yukti), (ii) the reasoning of function (kārya-karaṇa-yukti), (iii) the reasoning of proof (upapatti-sādhana-yukti), and (iv) the reasoning of the nature of dharmas (dharmatā-yukti). This is called the establishment of reasonings.

(4) **The establishment of vehicles** (yāna-vyavasthāpana): the śrāvaka, pratyekabuddha, and unsurpassed great vehicles each have seven kinds of establishment.

*The seven establishments of the śrāvaka vehicle*: (i) non-inverted wisdom regarding the four noble truths, (ii) the basis of this wisdom, (iii) the object of this wisdom, (iv) the companions of this wisdom, (v) the activity of this wisdom, (vi) the provisions that assist this wisdom, and (vii) the fruit realized by this wisdom. As with the śrāvaka vehicle, so too the seven establishments of the pratyekabuddha vehicle.

*The seven establishments of the unsurpassed great vehicle*: (i) the wisdom of non-discriminating (nirvikalpa-jñāna), which takes as its object the suchness of all dharmas that transcends verbal expression and is the impartial escape, (ii) the basis of this wisdom, (iii) the object of this wisdom, (iv) the companions of this wisdom, (v) the activity of this wisdom, (vi) the provisions that assist this wisdom, and (vii) the fruit realized by this wisdom. This is called the seven establishments of the three vehicles.

Thus the buddhas and bodhisattvas of the three times all employ these four correct establishments and institutions, without increase or decrease.

**Investigation of all dharmas** (sarva-dharma-paryeṣaṇā) means that bodhisattvas, in order to realize comprehensive knowledge in accord with reality concerning all dharmas, initiate the four investigations, as previously explained.

**Comprehensive knowledge in accord with reality** (yathābhūta-parijñāna) means that bodhisattvas initiate the four kinds of comprehensive knowledge in accord with reality concerning all dharmas, as previously explained.

**The various immeasurables** (apramāṇa) mean that bodhisattvas, in dependence on five immeasurables, are able to initiate all skillful activities. What are the five? (1) The immeasurable of the realm of sentient beings (sattva-dhātu), (2) the immeasurable of world-systems (loka-dhātu), (3) the immeasurable of the dharma-realm (dharma-dhātu), (4) the immeasurable of the realm of those to be disciplined (vineya-dhātu), and (5) the immeasurable of the realm of means of discipline (vinaya-upāya-dhātu).

**The immeasurable of the realm of sentient beings** refers to sixty-four kinds of sentient-being assemblies: (1) hell-beings, (2) animals, (3) hungry ghosts, (4) gods, (5) humans, (6) kṣatriyas, (7) brāhmaṇas, (8) vaiśyas, (9) śūdras, (10) women, (11) men, (12) those who are neither male nor female, (13) those of lower capacity, (14) those of intermediate capacity, (15) those of superior capacity, (16) householders, (17) renunciants, (18) practitioners of austerities, (19) those with moral restraint, (20) those without moral restraint, (21) those with neither restraint nor non-restraint, (22) those who have already separated from desire, (23) those who have not yet separated from desire, (24) those of the determined-for-wrong class (mithyātva-niyata-rāśi), (25) those of the determined-for-right class (samyaktva-niyata-rāśi), (26) those of the undetermined class (aniyata-rāśi), (27) bhikṣus, (28) bhikṣuṇīs, (29) śikṣamāṇās, (30) śrāmaṇeras, (31) śrāmaṇerīs, (32) upāsakas, (33) upāsikās, (34) those who practice samādhi, (35) those who recite the sūtras, (36) those who attend the sick, (37) elders, (38) middle-aged, (39) the young, (40) ācāryas, (41) upādhyāyas, (42) those who dwell together, (43) those who dwell nearby, (44) guests, (45) those who oversee saṅgha affairs, (46) those who delight in material gain, (47) those who delight in respect, (48) those who delight in solitude, (49) those of great learning, (50) those who possess wisdom, (51) those of great merit, (52) those who practice in accordance with the Dharma, (53) holders of the sūtra collection, (54) holders of the vinaya collection, (55) holders of the mātṛkā collection, (56) ordinary beings (pṛthagjana), (57) those who have seen the truth (darśana-mārga), (58) learners (śaikṣa), (59) non-learners (aśaikṣa), (60) śrāvakas, (61) pratyekabuddhas, (62) bodhisattvas, (63) wheel-turning sage-kings (cakravartin), and (64) tathāgatas. If distinguished by the difference of individual continua, they are immeasurable and limitless.

**The immeasurable of world-systems** refers to the immeasurable world-systems of the ten directions, with immeasurable differences in names. Just as this world-system is called Sahā, and the brahmā lord of this world is called the Lord of Sahā, so too there are immeasurable such differences.

**The immeasurable of the dharma-realm** refers to wholesome dharmas, unwholesome dharmas, and neutral dharmas, and such other gates of differentiation that are immeasurable.

**The immeasurable of the realm of those to be disciplined**: one may establish one kind of those to be disciplined, namely, among all sentient beings, those who can be disciplined form one class. Or two kinds: (i) those fully bound and (ii) those not fully bound. Or three kinds: (i) those of dull faculties, (ii) those of intermediate faculties, and (iii) those of keen faculties. Or four kinds: (i) kṣatriyas, (ii) brāhmaṇas, (iii) vaiśyas, and (iv) śūdras. Or five kinds: (i) those of the conduct of desire, (ii) those of the conduct of aversion, (iii) those of the conduct of delusion, (iv) those of the conduct of pride, and (v) those of the conduct of discursive thought. Or six kinds: (i) householders, (ii) renunciants, (iii) matured, (iv) not yet matured, (v) liberated, and (vi) not yet liberated. Or seven kinds: (i) those who are faithful and respectful; (ii) those who are contemptuous; (iii) those who are neutral; (iv) those who are disciplined by extensive teaching; (v) those who are opened to wisdom through brief instruction; (vi) those who must be constantly disciplined; (vii) those who are guided according to circumstance, meaning that when encountering such-and-such conditions they transform accordingly. Or eight kinds, namely the eight assemblies (aṣṭa-parṣad), from the kṣatriya assembly through the brahmā assembly. Or nine kinds: (i) those to be transformed by the Tathāgata, (ii) those to be transformed by śrāvakas, (iii) those to be transformed by pratyekabuddhas, (iv) those to be transformed by bodhisattvas, (v) those difficult to discipline, (vi) those easy to discipline, (vii) those disciplined through gentle speech, (viii) those disciplined through reproach and expulsion, and (ix) those disciplined from afar or from near. Or ten kinds: (i) hell-beings, (ii) animals, (iii) hungry ghosts, (iv) gods of the desire realm, (v) humans, (vi) beings in the intermediate existence (antarā-bhava), (vii) beings in the form realm, (viii) beings in the formless realm, (ix) beings with perception and without perception, and (x) beings with neither perception nor non-perception. Thus, in brief, there are fifty-five kinds; if distinguished by the difference of individual continua, they are immeasurable.

**Question**: What is the difference between the immeasurable of the realm of sentient beings and the immeasurable of the realm of those to be disciplined?

**Answer**: The realm of sentient beings refers, without distinction, to all sentient beings, whether they possess the lineage or lack the lineage. The realm of those to be disciplined refers only to those who possess the lineage, in their various stage-distinctions.

The immeasurable of the realm of means of discipline has been explained previously. This too, when discriminated in detail, has immeasurable kinds.

**Question**: Why are only these five immeasurables briefly stated?

**Answer**: Because bodhisattva-mahāsattvas are exclusively devoted to cultivating the practice of benefiting sentient beings, the immeasurable of the realm of sentient beings is established first. Because these sentient beings can receive transformation depending on their location, the immeasurable of world-systems is established second. Because these sentient beings in many world-systems undergo the differentiation of defilement and purification through various dharmas, the immeasurable of the dharma-realm is established third. In order to observe which sentient beings are ultimately capable of liberation from suffering, the immeasurable of the realm of those to be disciplined is established fourth. In order to establish whatever skillful means can cause sentient beings to realize liberation, the immeasurable of the realm of means of discipline is established fifth. Therefore, bodhisattva-mahāsattvas, in dependence on these five immeasurables, are able to initiate all skillful activities.

**The proclamation of the fruit and its benefits** (phala-anuśaṃsa-deśanā) means that when bodhisattva-mahāsattvas proclaim the correct Dharma to sentient beings, there are five kinds of vast fruit and benefit. What are the five?

(1) Some sentient beings, at the time of hearing the correct Dharma proclaimed, become free from dust and stain, and the Dharma-eye (dharma-cakṣus) arises in them with regard to all dharmas.

(2) Some sentient beings, at the very time the correct Dharma is being proclaimed, attain the exhaustion of all outflows (āsravakṣaya).

(3) Some sentient beings, because of the proclamation of this Dharma, generate the aspiration for unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening.

(4) Some sentient beings, upon hearing this Dharma proclaimed, immediately attain the supreme Dharma-forbearance (dharma-kṣānti) of a bodhisattva.

(5) Some sentient beings, after hearing the correct Dharma proclaimed by the Buddha and the bodhisattvas, receive, uphold, and practice it, and in turn proclaim it to others, causing the eye of the correct Dharma to long endure without perishing.

These are the five kinds of vast fruit and benefit of proclamation.

**The nature of the Great Vehicle** (mahāyānatva) means that the bodhisattva vehicle, because it accords with seven kinds of greatness (mahattva), is called the Great Vehicle. What are the seven?

(1) *Greatness of Dharma* (dharma-mahattva): among the twelve divisions of teaching, the vaipulya teaching encompassed in the bodhisattva collection.

(2) *Greatness of generating the aspiration* (cittotpāda-mahattva): having already generated the aspiration for unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening.

(3) *Greatness of resolve* (adhimukti-mahattva): giving rise to superior faith and understanding with regard to the greatness of Dharma described above.

(4) *Greatness of superior intention* (adhyāśaya-mahattva): having already surpassed the ground of resolve and practice and entered the ground of pure superior intention.

(5) *Greatness of provisions* (saṃbhāra-mahattva): having already accomplished the two great provisions of merit and wisdom, and thereby being able to realize unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening.

(6) *Greatness of time* (kāla-mahattva): being able to realize unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening over the time of three great incalculable kalpas (asaṃkhyeya).

(7) *Greatness of fulfillment* (saṃpatti-mahattva): namely, unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening itself. The self-substance of this fulfilled awakening, compared to any other fulfillment, has no equal; how much less could anything surpass it!

Among these, the first six, from greatness of Dharma through greatness of time, are the causes of the greatness of fulfillment. The single greatness of fulfillment is the fruit of the preceding six.

**Encompassment** (saṃgraha) means that eight kinds of dharma are able to completely encompass the entire Great Vehicle.

(1) The teaching of the bodhisattva collection (bodhisattva-piṭaka).
(2) Within the bodhisattva collection, the teaching that reveals the true meaning of all dharmas.
(3) Within the bodhisattva collection, the teaching that reveals the inconceivable, most excellent, vast, and profoundly majestic virtues of all buddhas and bodhisattvas.
(4) Proper hearing of the above (śravaṇa).
(5) First engaging in proper reflection (cintā), tending toward superior intention.
(6) Having attained superior intention, entering the initial stages of practice (bhāvanā).
(7) Through entering practice first, accomplishing the fruit of practice.
(8) Through the accomplishment of the fruit of practice, ultimate escape.

Through such cultivation and study, bodhisattva-mahāsattvas realize unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening.

**The ten things to know about bodhisattvas** (daśa-bodhisattva-jñeya) means that such bodhisattvas, who through cultivation and study are able to realize unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening, briefly have ten aspects.

(1) Abiding in the lineage (gotra-sthita).
(2) Having entered (avatīrṇa).
(3) Of impure superior intention (aśuddha-adhyāśaya).
(4) Of pure superior intention (śuddha-adhyāśaya).
(5) Not yet matured (aparipakva).
(6) Already matured (paripakva).
(7) Not yet determined (aniyata).
(8) Already determined (niyata).
(9) Bound by one more life (eka-jāti-pratibaddha).
(10) Dwelling in the final existence (carama-bhavika).

Among these, the bodhisattva who abides in the lineage, when through skillful means of cultivation they generate the aspiration for awakening, is called "having entered." Having entered, until one has not yet entered the ground of pure superior intention, one is called "of impure superior intention"; if one has entered it, one is called "of pure superior intention." The bodhisattva of pure superior intention, until one has not yet entered the ground of reaching the ultimate, is called "not yet matured"; if one has entered it, one is called "already matured." Among those not yet matured, until one has not yet entered the ground of determined practice, one is called "not yet determined"; if one has entered it, one is called "already determined." Among those already matured, there are furthermore two kinds: (i) "bound by one more life," meaning that after this life, without interval, one will realize anuttarā samyaksaṃbodhi; (ii) "dwelling in the final existence," meaning that in this very life one realizes unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening. Thus, from abiding in the lineage through unsurpassed, complete, perfect awakening, the ten kinds of bodhisattvas as described above completely encompass all bodhisattvas. The bodhisattva training grounds as described above completely encompass all training grounds of bodhisattvas.

**The establishment of names and titles** (nāma-saṃketa-vyavasthāpana) means that bodhisattvas, without distinction, have many names according to their qualities. They are called bodhisattva (bodhisattva), mahāsattva (mahāsattva), "accomplished in the wisdom of awakening," "supreme bright illumination," "offspring of the most excellent," "support of the most excellent," "emissary of the most excellent," "sprout of the most excellent." They are also called "the valiant," "supreme preceptor," "caravan leader" (sārthavāha), "one endowed with great renown," "one accomplished in compassion," "one of great merit," "one rich in sovereign mastery," and "great Dharma-teacher." Thus, throughout the limitless world-systems of the ten directions, in dependence on the immeasurable differences of inner qualities, innumerable names and titles are established.

Among these, if bodhisattvas proclaim themselves to be bodhisattvas yet do not diligently cultivate and practice all the training grounds of bodhisattvas, one should know that these are simulated bodhisattvas, not genuine bodhisattvas. If bodhisattvas proclaim themselves to be bodhisattvas and also diligently cultivate the training grounds of bodhisattvas, one should know that these are genuine bodhisattvas.

---

## Fascicle 9

### Chapter on Gathering the Meaning of Purity, Part Five

**The many kinds of heterodox treatises** (vāda) refer to sixteen kinds of heterodox treatise. What are the sixteen? The summary verse says:

> The thesis of the effect existing in the cause, manifestation, the real existence of past and future,
> The self, permanence, prior action as the cause, Īśvara and others as creators, killing as the correct Dharma,
> Finite and infinite, the immortal evasion, the view of no cause, annihilationism, emptiness,
> False attribution of supremacy, purity, and auspiciousness: these are the sixteen heterodox treatises.

**The treatise says**: The sixteen heterodox treatises are: (1) the thesis that the effect exists in the cause (satkārya-vāda), (2) the thesis of manifestation from conditions, (3) the thesis that past and future really exist, (4) the thesis of a self (ātma-vāda), (5) the thesis of permanence (śāśvata-vāda), (6) the thesis that prior action is the cause, (7) the thesis that Īśvara and others are the creators, (8) the thesis that killing is the correct Dharma, (9) the thesis of finitude and infinitude, (10) the thesis of immortal evasion (amarā-vikṣepa), (11) the view of no cause (ahetuka-dṛṣṭi), (12) the annihilationist view (uccheda-dṛṣṭi), (13) the emptiness view (śūnyatā-dṛṣṭi), (14) the thesis of false attribution of supremacy, (15) the thesis of false attribution of purity, and (16) the thesis of false attribution of auspiciousness.

#### 1. The thesis that the effect exists in the cause

This means that someone, whether a śramaṇa or a brāhmaṇa, gives rise to the following view and establishes the following thesis: the nature of the effect is always and permanently present within the cause. This is the position of the Sāṃkhya (rain-garment) non-Buddhists.

**Question**: For what reason do those non-Buddhists, with regard to causes, give rise to this view and establish the thesis that the effect is shown to exist within the cause?

**Answer**: Because of teaching and reasoning. *Teaching* means that the teaching-treasury composed by their former masters, transmitted by oral tradition from one to the next down to the present, declares that the effect is already present within the cause. *Reasoning* means that those śramaṇas and brāhmaṇas, being of a nature to investigate and observe, dwelling on the ground of investigation, the ground of self-determination, the ground of ordinary persons, and the ground of reflective observation, think as follows: when from a given substance another substance is produced, the entire world commonly acknowledges and establishes that the former is the cause of the latter and not anything else. Furthermore, when people of the world desire a given effect, they take only this particular cause and not any other. Furthermore, with regard to a given affair, they apply effort and labor only in this way and not to other affairs. Furthermore, if a given effect is produced precisely from that cause and not from any other, then the effect must already exist within the cause. If this were not so, then one should establish that everything is the cause of everything. Also, when seeking any given effect, one should take every cause. Also, one should apply effort and labor in every affair for the sake of a single effect. Also, from everything, every effect should arise. Thus, by reason of establishment, acquisition, engagement in affairs, and production, they see the nature of the effect as always present within the cause.

I now ask you; answer according to your own understanding. Are the two characteristics of cause and effect different or not different? If not different, then there are no determinate two entities of cause and effect, because these two characteristics are without distinction. To say that the effect exists in the cause is then unreasonable. If they are of different characteristics, what do you think? Does the effect-substance within the cause have the characteristic of not-yet-arisen or of already-arisen? If it has the characteristic of not-yet-arisen, then to say that the effect within the cause has not yet arisen yet is existent is unreasonable. If it has the characteristic of already-arisen, then the effect-substance has already arisen, and to say it arises again from the cause is unreasonable. Therefore the effect does not previously exist within the cause; rather, it is only because there is a cause that, awaiting conditions, it arises.

A dharma that has a characteristic can be understood to exist within a dharma that has a characteristic through five aspects: (1) understood by location, as water in a vase; (2) understood by basis of dependence, as visual consciousness within the eye; (3) understood by its own characteristic, as the cause's own substance is known not through inference; (4) understood by its own activity; (5) understood because when the cause changes the effect changes, or when the condition changes the effect changes. Therefore, to say that the effect always and permanently exists in the cause is unreasonable. By this meaning, the thesis they establish is not a reasoned statement. It is unreasonable because of non-difference of characteristics, because of difference of characteristics, because of the not-yet-arisen characteristic, and because of the already-arisen characteristic.

#### 2. The thesis of manifestation from conditions

This means that someone, whether a śramaṇa or a brāhmaṇa, gives rise to the following view and establishes the following thesis: the substance of all dharmas inherently exists from the very beginning; it is manifested through conditions but not produced by conditions. This is the position of both the thesis-holders of the effect existing in the cause and the thesis-holders of sound-characteristics (śabda-lakṣaṇa-vāda).

**Question**: For what reason do the thesis-holders of the effect existing in the cause see the nature of the effect as previously existing within causes and manifested from conditions?

**Answer**: Because of teaching and reasoning. Teaching is as previously stated. *Reasoning* means that someone, being of a nature to investigate and observe, and so forth as previously stated, thinks as follows: if the effect previously exists and yet arises again from the cause, this is unreasonable. Yet it is not the case that no effort is needed for the accomplishment of the effect. For what purpose then is effort applied? Is it not solely for the purpose of manifesting the effect? Having engaged in such false reflection, they propound the thesis of manifestation.

I now ask you; answer according to your own understanding. Is there obstruction because there is no obstructing condition, or is there obstruction because there is an obstructing condition? If there is none, then to have obstruction without an obstructing condition is unreasonable. If there is one, why does the cause to which the effect belongs not also obstruct? They are equally existent. Just as darkness obstructs the water in a vase and also obstructs the vase, if you say it also obstructs the cause, then both should be obstructed and both should be manifested. To manifest only the nature of the effect as previously existing within the cause while not manifesting the cause is unreasonable.

You should further state: is the obstructing condition the existence-nature or the effect-nature? If the existence-nature is the obstructing condition, then the existence-nature would be permanently unmanifest, which is unreasonable. Furthermore, the cause is also existent; why is it not the obstructing condition? If the effect-nature is the obstructing condition, then a single dharma would be both cause and effect, as a sprout is the effect of the seed and the cause of the stem, and thus a single dharma would be both manifest and unmanifest, which is unreasonable.

Furthermore I ask you; answer according to your own understanding. Is the manifestation identical with or different from the original dharma? If identical, then that originally existent dharma is always manifest, and to manifest what is already manifest is unreasonable. If different, does this manifestation have a cause or not? If it has no cause, then to become manifest without a cause is unreasonable. If it has a cause, the effect-nature can be manifested but not the cause-nature; for a cause that is unmanifest to manifest an effect is unreasonable. Thus, because of no obstructing condition, because of an obstructing condition, because of the existence-characteristic, because of the effect-characteristic, because of manifestation being identical, and because of manifestation being different, it is unreasonable. Therefore your claim that if a dharma's nature is non-existent then it is characteristicless, and if its nature is existent then it has characteristics, and if its nature is non-existent it cannot be manifested, and if its nature is existent it can be manifested, is unreasonable.

I now briefly state: although something truly exists, its characteristic may not be apprehensible. Some things are not apprehensible because they are distant. Some are not apprehensible because they are obstructed by four kinds of obstructing causes. Some are not apprehensible because they are extremely subtle. Some are not apprehensible because the mind is distracted. Some are not apprehensible because the sense-faculties are impaired. Some are not apprehensible because one has not yet attained the corresponding wisdom. Thus, the thesis that the effect exists in the cause and is manifested from conditions is unreasonable. The thesis of the sound-characteristics school should likewise be understood. The difference here is that the external sound-thesis teachers give rise to the following view and establish the following thesis: the characteristic of sound inherently exists, without arising and without perishing; however, through repeated utterance it becomes manifest, and the substance of sound is permanent. Therefore the thesis of manifestation from conditions is not a reasoned statement.

#### 3. The thesis that past and future really exist

This means that someone, whether a śramaṇa or a brāhmaṇa, either abiding in this dharma, through incorrect mental engagement (ayoniśo-manaskāra), gives rise to the following view and establishes the following thesis: the past and the future, endowed with their own characteristics, are real and not provisional, just like the present.

**Question**: For what reason do they give rise to this view and establish this thesis?

**Answer**: Because of teaching and reasoning. Teaching is as previously stated.

Furthermore, regarding this dharma, there are scriptural teachings that derive from incorrect mental engagement. As the sūtra says: "All that exists refers to the twelve bases (āyatana); these twelve bases exist by virtue of their own characteristics." Also, the Blessed One said: "There is past action (karma)." Also, the sūtra says: "There is past form, there is future form," and so forth through consciousness.

*Reasoning* means that someone, being of a nature to investigate and observe, and so forth as previously stated, thinks as follows: if a dharma's own characteristic abides in this dharma, then it is truly existent. If this dharma were non-existent in the future, then at that time it should not yet have received its own characteristic. If this dharma were non-existent in the past, then at that time it should have lost its own characteristic. If so, the own characteristics of dharmas would not be established, and therefore the natures of dharmas would not be true and real. If so, this is unreasonable. Through such thinking, they give rise to this view and establish the thesis that the nature and characteristics of the past and future really exist.

I now ask you; answer according to your own understanding. Are the two characteristics of past and future not different from the characteristic of the present, or are they different? If not different, then to establish the three characteristics of the three times is unreasonable. If different, then to say their nature and characteristics really exist is unreasonable.

Furthermore, you should state your own preference: do dharmas that fall within the three times have the characteristic of permanence or impermanence? If they have the characteristic of permanence, then to fall within the three times is unreasonable. If they have the characteristic of impermanence, then to be permanently real and existent throughout the three times is unreasonable.

Furthermore, you should state your own preference: do you hold that a future dharma comes to the present world? Or that a future dharma perishes and the present world arises? Or that a dharma abides in the future and serves as a condition for the arising of the present world? Or that what originally had no functional activity now has functional activity? Or that what originally had incomplete characteristics now has complete characteristics? Or that the original characteristic was different and the present characteristic is different? Or that in the future there is the characteristic of a present portion?

If you say that a future dharma itself comes to the present, then it should have spatial location; furthermore, future and present would be without difference; furthermore, it should be permanent. This is unreasonable. If you say that the future perishes and the present arises, then the future dharma does not arise in the present, and a dharma that originally did not exist now arises. Furthermore, for the future to not yet have arisen yet to be said to perish is unreasonable.

If you say that a dharma abides in the future and serves as a condition for the arising of the present world, then it should be permanent; furthermore, it should be something that originally did not exist and now arises, rather than a future dharma that arises. This is unreasonable. If you say that what originally had no functional activity now has functional activity, then what originally existed and now exists would have the faults previously described, and it is unreasonable. Furthermore, regarding this functional activity, what do you think? Is the characteristic of this functional activity different from or not different from the original dharma? If different, then since this functional-activity characteristic does not exist in the future, it is unreasonable. If not different, then to have originally had no functional activity and now to have functional activity is unreasonable.

As with no functional activity having this fault, so too with incomplete characteristics, different characteristics, and the future-portion characteristic. The difference here is that there is additionally the fault of confusion of self-substance. This is unreasonable. As the future moves toward the present, so too the present goes to the past; the faults apply as appropriate, following the causes as previously maintained and the reasoning as previously refuted.

Thus, because of own characteristics, shared characteristics, coming, perishing, arising, function, complete characteristics, different characteristics, and the future portion, the thesis that the past and future really exist is unreasonable.

Having thus refuted, there is a further objection: if the past and future are non-existent, how can cognition operate with regard to the non-existent as its object? If you say cognition operates with regard to the non-existent, how do you avoid the fault of contradicting the teaching? As it says: "All that exists refers to the twelve bases."

I now ask you; answer according to your own understanding. Does worldly cognition that apprehends the non-existent arise or not? If it does not arise, then the cognition that apprehends the non-self, the cognition of rabbit horns, the son of a barren woman, and so forth should all be non-existent. This is unreasonable. Furthermore, the Blessed One said: "My non-deceitful śrāvakas, when correctly practicing as I have taught, know what exists as existent and know what is non-existent as non-existent." If this were so, it would be unreasonable. If you say it does arise, what is your preference regarding this cognition that apprehends the non-existent? Does it operate as an existent mental act or as a non-existent mental act? If it operates as an existent mental act, then for a cognition that apprehends the non-existent to operate as an existent mental act is unreasonable. If it operates as a non-existent mental act, what is your preference regarding this cognition of a non-existent mental act? Does it operate with an existent object or with a non-existent object? If it operates with an existent object, then for a cognition of a non-existent mental act to operate with an existent object is unreasonable. If it operates with a non-existent object, then there is no cognition that takes the non-existent as its object. This is unreasonable.

Although it says "all that exists refers to the twelve bases," this is said with a hidden intention (abhiprāya): regarding existent dharmas, it speaks with the hidden intention of their having the characteristic of existence; regarding non-existent dharmas, it speaks with the hidden intention of their having the characteristic of non-existence. Why? Because dharmas with the existent characteristic sustain the existent characteristic, and dharmas with the non-existent characteristic sustain the non-existent characteristic. Therefore both are called "dharmas" and both are called "existent." If this were not so, then practitioners who know only the existent and do not know the non-existent would not have uninterrupted observation of the knowable dharmas, which is unreasonable.

Furthermore, although it says "there is past action," and because of this action sentient beings receive harmful and non-harmful feelings, this too is said with the hidden intention of referring to habitual tendencies (vāsanā), provisionally designating those dharmas as existent. That is, within the formations (saṃskāra), pure and impure actions have arisen and ceased; because of this causal condition, those formations flow on in a continuum of superior and different characteristics. This is called habitual tendencies. Because of the habitual tendencies contained within this continuum, agreeable and disagreeable fruits arise. Therefore there is no fault on our part, while your position is unreasonable.

Furthermore, although it says "there is past form, there is future form, there is present form," and so forth through consciousness, this too is said with the hidden intention of three kinds of aspects: the causal aspect (hetu-nimitta), the own-nature aspect (sva-lakṣaṇa), and the fruition aspect (phala-nimitta). With the hidden intention of the causal aspect, it says "there is the future." With the hidden intention of the own-nature aspect, it says "there is the present." With the hidden intention of the fruition aspect, it says "there is the past." Therefore there is no fault.

Furthermore, one should not say that the past and future have real, existent characteristics. Why? Because one should know that the future has twelve kinds of characteristics: (1) the characteristic of being revealed by a cause, (2) the characteristic of its substance being not yet arisen, (3) the characteristic of awaiting conditions, (4) the characteristic of being of the class of what has already arisen, (5) the characteristic of being a dharma that should arise, (6) the characteristic of being a dharma that will not arise, (7) the characteristic of not-yet-arisen defilement, (8) the characteristic of not-yet-arisen purification, (9) the characteristic of being something that should be sought, (10) the characteristic of being something that should not be sought, (11) the characteristic of being something that should be examined, and (12) the characteristic of being something that should not be examined.

One should know that the present likewise has twelve kinds of characteristics: (1) the characteristic of being revealed by a fruit, (2) the characteristic of its substance having already arisen, (3) the characteristic of the convergence of conditions, (4) the characteristic of being of the class of what has already arisen, (5) the characteristic of being momentary (kṣaṇa), (6) the characteristic of being a dharma that will not arise again, (7) the characteristic of present defilement, (8) the characteristic of present purification, (9) the characteristic of being something pleasant, (10) the characteristic of being something unpleasant, (11) the characteristic of being something that should be examined, and (12) the characteristic of being something that should not be examined.

One should know that the past likewise has twelve kinds of characteristics: (1) the characteristic of having passed beyond the cause, (2) the characteristic of having passed beyond the conditions, (3) the characteristic of having passed beyond the fruit, (4) the characteristic of its substance having been destroyed, (5) the characteristic of being of the class of what has already perished, (6) the characteristic of being a dharma that will not arise, (7) the characteristic of the quiescence of defilement, (8) the characteristic of the quiescence of purification, (9) the characteristic of being something one might fondly recall, (10) the characteristic of being something one should not fondly recall, (11) the characteristic of being something that should be examined, and (12) the characteristic of being something that should not be examined.

#### 4. The thesis of a self

This means that someone, whether a śramaṇa or a brāhmaṇa, gives rise to the following view and establishes the following thesis: there is a self (ātman), there is a being (sattva), there is a life-force (jīva), there is one who is born, there is one who nourishes, there is a person (pudgala), and so forth; these are real and permanent. The various non-Buddhists hold this position.

**Question**: Why do those non-Buddhists give rise to this view and establish this thesis?

**Answer**: Because of teaching and reasoning. Teaching is as previously stated. *Reasoning* means that someone, being of a nature to investigate and observe, and so forth as previously stated. There are two kinds of causes: (1) prior to deliberate thought, spontaneously, one obtains the cognition of a being; and (2) through prior deliberate thought, one sees that there is an agent.

They think as follows: if there were no self, then upon seeing the five things, the five kinds of cognition of a self should not arise.

(1) Having seen a physical form, one should give rise only to the cognition of form and should not give rise to the cognition of a being.

(2) Having seen actions that accord with suffering and pleasure, one should give rise only to the cognition of feeling and should not give rise to the cognition of a superior or inferior being.

(3) Having seen that names have been established and that the actions associated with name-characteristics occur, one should give rise only to the cognition of perception and should not give rise to the cognition of beings as kṣatriyas, brāhmaṇas, vaiśyas, śūdras, Buddhapālita, Guṇamitra, and so forth.

(4) Having seen actions associated with pure and impure conduct, one should give rise only to the cognition of formations and should not give rise to the cognition of foolish or wise beings.

(5) Having seen consciousness follow and turn in accordance with sense-objects, one should give rise only to the cognition of mind and should not give rise to the cognition of a being such as "I am the one who sees."

Since prior to deliberate thought, with regard to these five things, one gives rise only to these five kinds of cognition of a being and not to cognitions of the formations, it follows that prior to deliberate thought, having seen these, one spontaneously gives rise to the cognition of a being. Thus one knows with certainty that there is a real self.

Furthermore they think as follows: if there were no self, then within the formations, through prior deliberate thought, there should not be any agent. That is, "I shall see forms with the eye," "I am presently seeing forms with the eye," "I have seen forms with the eye," or again one thinks: "I shall not see," and so forth. All such functions are preceded by the cognition of "I." As with the eye seeing, so too with the ear, nose, tongue, body, and mind.

Furthermore, with regard to the performance and cessation of wholesome actions, and the performance and cessation of unwholesome actions, and similar affairs, deliberate thought comes first before there can be any function. Such functions should not pertain merely to the formations; this is unreasonable. Through such thinking, they assert the existence of a self.

I now ask you; answer according to your own understanding. Does the cognition of a being arise with regard to the very thing that is seen, or with regard to something other than what is seen? If the cognition of a being arises with regard to the very thing that is seen, then you should not say that to conceive of a being with regard to form and so forth, and to conceive of a self, is an inverted cognition. If the cognition of a being arises with regard to something other than what is seen, then to say that the self has shape and size is unreasonable. To say the self is superior or inferior, or is a kṣatriya and so forth, or is foolish or wise, or is able to apprehend objects of form and so forth, is unreasonable.

Furthermore, what is your preference? Does this cognition arise only with regard to this very dharma's own substance, or does it also arise with regard to other substances? If it arises only with regard to this very dharma's own substance, then the cognition of a self arising with regard to the very thing that is seen should not be called an inverted cognition. If it also arises with regard to other substances, then every object would be the cause of cognition of every other object, which is unreasonable.

Furthermore, what is your preference? Does the cognition of a sentient being arise with regard to insentient things? Does the cognition of an insentient thing arise with regard to sentient beings? Does the cognition of one sentient being arise with regard to another sentient being? Does it arise or not? If it arises, then the insentient would be sentient, the sentient would be insentient, and one sentient being would be another sentient being. This is unreasonable. If it does not arise, then one cannot deny direct perception. This is unreasonable.

Furthermore, what is your preference? Does this cognition of a self apprehend the meaning of direct perception (pratyakṣa), or the meaning of inference (anumāna)? If it apprehends the meaning of direct perception, then since only form and other aggregates are the objects of direct perception, and the self is not an object of direct perception, this is unreasonable. If it apprehends the meaning of inference, then just as the foolish and young who are not yet able to reason should not spontaneously give rise to the cognition of a self.

Furthermore, I now ask you; answer according to your own understanding. Is what the world does caused by cognition or caused by the self? If caused by cognition, then to maintain that the self is the agent is unreasonable. If caused by the self, then that one sees agency through prior deliberate thought is unreasonable.

Furthermore, what is your preference? Is the cause of action impermanent or permanent? If impermanent, then the substance of this causal agent undergoes change, and to maintain that the self is the agent is unreasonable. If permanent, then there is no change, and for a dharma without change to be maintained as having agency is unreasonable.

Furthermore, what is your preference? Does a self that has movement have agency, or does a self without movement have agency? If a self with movement has agency, then it always acts, and there should be nothing further to be done. If a self without movement has agency, then to have agency without the nature of movement is unreasonable.

Furthermore, what is your preference? Does the self have agency because of a cause, or without a cause? If because of a cause, then this self would require some other cause to impel it before it has agency, which is unreasonable. If without a cause, then it should act in all things at all times, which is unreasonable.

Furthermore, what is your preference? Does this self have agency in dependence on itself, or in dependence on something else? If in dependence on itself, then this self would inflict upon itself old age, sickness, death, suffering, defilement, and so forth, which is unreasonable. If in dependence on something else, then to maintain that the self is the agent is unreasonable.

Furthermore, I now ask you; answer according to your own understanding. Is the self designated (prajñapti) with respect to the aggregates, or within the aggregates, or somewhere outside the aggregates, or as not of the nature of the aggregates?

If the self is designated with respect to the aggregates, then the self and the aggregates are without difference, and to maintain that there is a self that is real and permanent is unreasonable.

If within the aggregates, is this self permanent or impermanent? If permanent, then for a permanently abiding self to be damaged and benefited by suffering and pleasure is unreasonable. If it is neither damaged nor benefited, then for dharma and non-dharma to arise is unreasonable. If dharma and non-dharma do not arise, then the body of aggregates should ultimately never arise, and furthermore, the self should be always liberated without any effort. If impermanent, then apart from the aggregate substance, a dharma that arises, perishes, and flows on in continuity is not obtainable, and so this is unreasonable. Furthermore, after its destruction here, to not have acted yet to have obtained results elsewhere has a great fault, and so this is unreasonable.

If somewhere outside the aggregates, then the self you maintain should be unconditioned (asaṃskṛta).

If not of the nature of the aggregates, then the self should at all times be without defilement. Furthermore, the self and the body should not be related, which is unreasonable.

Furthermore, what is your preference? Is the self you maintain identical with the characteristics of a seer and so forth, or separate from the characteristics of a seer and so forth?

If identical with the characteristics of a seer and so forth, is the seer and so forth provisionally established upon seeing and so forth, or separately established apart from seeing and so forth?

If provisionally established upon seeing and so forth, then seeing and so forth would themselves be the seer and so forth, and for you to establish the self as the seer and so forth is unreasonable, because the seer and so forth would be without difference from seeing and so forth.

If separately established apart from seeing and so forth, then are the dharmas of seeing and so forth the actions accomplished by the self, or the instruments wielded by the self?

If they are the actions accomplished by the self: if this self is like a seed, it should be impermanent. If you say it is like provisionally established persons such as potters, then this self should be impermanent and provisionally established, and your claim that it is permanent and real is unreasonable. If you say it is like provisionally established persons who possess supernatural powers, then this self should likewise be impermanent and provisionally established, freely acting as it wishes in all activities. This too is unreasonable as before. If you say it is like earth, it should be impermanent. Furthermore, the self you maintain does not have clearly apparent functional activity like the earth element, and so this is unreasonable. Why? Because the functional activity of the earth element in the world is clearly obtainable, namely, supporting the myriad things and preventing them from falling. No such clearly obtainable functional activity exists for the self. If you say it is like space, then it should be a non-existent dharma, which is unreasonable. Why? Because space is merely provisionally established where form is absent. Although space is provisionally existent, its functional activity is clearly obtainable, unlike the self you maintain. This is unreasonable. The clearly obtainable functional activity of space in the world is that through space, the activities of coming and going, bending and stretching, and so forth are possible. Therefore, that seeing and so forth are actions accomplished by the self is unreasonable.

If they are instruments wielded by the self: if you say they are like a sickle, then just as apart from the sickle other things also have the functional activity of cutting, so too the functional activity of seeing and so forth should be obtainable on things other than seeing and so forth; but it is not, and so this is unreasonable. If you say they are like fire, then to posit a self is in vain, which is unreasonable. Why? Because in the world, fire, apart from any burner, is able to burn by itself.

If you say that apart from the characteristics of a seer and so forth there is separately a self, then the characteristics of the self you maintain contradict all valid means of knowledge (pramāṇa). This is unreasonable.

Furthermore, I now ask you; answer according to your own understanding. Is the self you maintain associated with the characteristics of defilement and purification and thereby defiled and purified, or is it not associated with the characteristics of defilement and purification yet defiled and purified? If it is associated with the characteristics of defilement and purification and thereby defiled and purified, then within the formations, there are pestilence, calamity, and their cessation and benefit to be found. Those very formations, although without a self, can be said to have the characteristics of defilement and purification associated with them. Just as with external things, so too with the internal body: although there is no self, the meaning of defilement and purification is accomplished. Therefore your thesis of a self is unreasonable. If it is not associated with the characteristics of defilement and purification yet defiled and purified, then for the self to be defiled and purified apart from the characteristics of defilement and purification is unreasonable.

Furthermore, I now ask you; answer according to your own understanding. Is the self you maintain associated with the characteristic of transmigration (saṃsāra) and thereby transmigrating and ceasing, or is it not associated with the characteristic of transmigration yet transmigrating and ceasing? If it is associated with the characteristic of transmigration and thereby transmigrating and ceasing, then within the formations, five kinds of flowing characteristics are obtainable: (1) having a cause, (2) being capable of arising, (3) being capable of ceasing, (4) arising in continuous succession, and (5) undergoing change. If the characteristic of transmigration is obtainable within the formations, then just as in the transmigrating functions of bodies, teeth, rivers, lamps, vehicles, and so forth, although there is no self, those very formations can have transmigration and cessation, what need is there to posit a self? If it is not associated with that characteristic yet has transmigration and cessation, then for the self you maintain to have transmigration and cessation without the characteristic of transmigration is unreasonable.

Furthermore, I now ask you; answer according to your own understanding. Is the self you maintain said to be the experiencer, the agent, and the one who is liberated because it is changed by the suffering and pleasure produced by sense-objects, by the actions of deliberate thought, and by afflictions and secondary afflictions? Or is it said to be the experiencer and so forth without being changed by these? If it is changed by these, then the formations themselves are the experiencer, the agent, and the one who is liberated; what need is there to posit a self? Even if there were a self, the self would be impermanent, which is unreasonable. If it is not changed by these, then for the self to be the experiencer, the agent, and the one who is liberated without undergoing change is unreasonable.

Furthermore, you should now state your own preference: is only the self said to be the agent, or are other dharmas also said to be agents? If only the self, then the world should not say that fire is the burner and light is the illuminator. If other dharmas are also agents, then seeing and other sense-faculties can be said to be agents, and to discriminate and posit a self is in vain. This is unreasonable.

Furthermore, you should state your own preference: is "I" (ātman) established only with regard to the self, or also with regard to other dharmas? If only with regard to the self, then the world should not, with regard to provisionally designated persons, call them Guṇamitra, Buddhapālita, and so forth. If also with regard to other dharmas, then "self" is merely provisionally designated with regard to the formations; what need is there to further maintain that there is a separate self? Why? Because people of the world, solely with regard to the provisionally designated person, give rise to the idea of a being, establish the name of a being, and speak of the differences between self and other.

Furthermore, what is your preference? Is the view of a self wholesome or unwholesome? If it is wholesome, then why do extremely deluded persons deeply give rise to the view of a self? Furthermore, it arises spontaneously without effort. Furthermore, it causes sentient beings to fear liberation. Furthermore, it increases all faults and transgressions. This is unreasonable. If it is unwholesome, it should not be called correct and non-inverted. If it is inverted, then for the self that is maintained to be a truly existent substance is unreasonable.

Furthermore, what is your preference? Is the view of non-self wholesome or unwholesome? If it is wholesome, then to have a correct and non-inverted view of non-self with regard to a permanent, truly existent self is unreasonable. If it is unwholesome, then since it is proclaimed by the omniscient one, produced through diligent effort and skillful means, does not cause sentient beings to fear liberation, enables the swift realization of the fruit of purity, and is the genuine antidote to all faults and transgressions, this is unreasonable.

Furthermore, what do you think? Does the self itself, by its own nature, conceive of a self, or is it through the view of a self? If the self itself, by its own nature, conceives of a self, then at all times there should be no cognition of non-self. If it is through the view of a self, then although there is no real self, through the power of the view of a self, within the formations one falsely conceives of a self. Therefore, your assertion that there definitely and really is a self is unreasonable.

Thus, because of cognition arising prior to deliberate thought, because of seeing agency through deliberate thought, because of establishment within the aggregates, because of being established as existent by those characteristics, because of establishing defilement and purification, because of establishing transmigration and cessation, because of establishing the experiencer, the agent, and the one who is liberated, because of designating an agent, because of designating speech, and because of designating a view, the assertion that there really is a self is in every case unreasonable.

I shall now state the characteristic of the self in the ultimate sense. What is called "self" is merely provisionally established (prajñapti) with regard to dharmas; there is no real self. Yet this provisional self cannot be said to be either different from or not different from those dharmas, lest one suppose that this self is a truly existent substance, or that those dharmas themselves have the nature and characteristics of a self. Furthermore, this provisional self has the characteristic of impermanence, the characteristic of non-endurance, the characteristic of non-security, the characteristic of decay and destruction, the characteristic of a dharma subject to arising, the characteristic of a dharma subject to old age, sickness, and death, the characteristic of being merely dharmas, and the characteristic of being merely suffering. Therefore the Blessed One said: "Bhikṣus, you should know that with regard to dharmas, a self is provisionally established. This self is impermanent, non-enduring, insecure, and subject to decay and destruction," and so forth extensively.

One should know that for four reasons a self is provisionally designated within the formations: (1) so that worldly speech and communication may be facilitated, (2) in order to conform to the conventions of the world, (3) in order to eliminate the terror of those who think there is definitely no self, and (4) in order to cause definite faith and understanding to arise by proclaiming the accomplishment of virtuous qualities and the accomplishment of faults in oneself and others. Therefore the thesis of a self is not a reasoned statement.

#### 5. The thesis of permanence

This means that someone, whether a śramaṇa or a brāhmaṇa, gives rise to the following view and establishes the following thesis: the self and the world are both permanent, neither made nor the product of what is made, neither transformed nor the product of what is transformed, not subject to harm, abiding in an accumulated mass, like the iśīkā reed. This includes those who, asserting the past, declare that everything is permanent or that a portion is permanent, and those who, asserting the future, declare the existence of perception, non-perception, or neither-perception-nor-non-perception. There are also those who maintain that atoms (paramāṇu) are permanent.

**Question**: Why do those non-Buddhists give rise to this view and establish the thesis that the self and the world are permanent?

**Answer**: The causes of their thesis are as extensively explained in the sūtras; one should understand them as appropriate. Among these, those who assert the past, depending on lower, intermediate, or superior dhyāna, give rise to the recollection of past abodes (pūrva-nivāsānusmṛti). Because they do not skillfully understand dependent arising (pratītyasamutpāda), they merely recall the past formations without knowing them as they truly are, take the past world as the anterior limit, and generate the view of permanence. Or depending on the divine eye, they take the present world as the anterior limit. Because they do not truly understand the momentary arising, ceasing, and flowing of formations, and furthermore, because they see consciousness flowing and continuing without interruption from this world to another world, they generate the view of permanence. Or they see Brahmā freely establishing things. Or they see the transformation of the four great elements. Or they see the transformation of the various consciousnesses.

Those who assert the future: although they see differences in perception and feeling, they do not see any difference in the own-characteristic of the self. Therefore they generate the view of permanence, asserting that the self and the world are both permanent.

Furthermore, those who maintain that atoms are permanent, relying on worldly dhyāna, give rise to this view. Because they do not truly understand dependent arising, they maintain that prior existence is the basis for the arising and accumulation of effects, and that prior dispersal is the basis for the destruction of effects. For this reason, they maintain that from the nature of many atoms the coarse material effect arises, and that when coarse matter is progressively analyzed it abides as atoms. Therefore coarse matter is impermanent, but atoms are permanent.

Among these, the theses of permanence asserting the past and future are subsumed under the thesis of a self as differentiated aspects. Since the thesis of a self has been refuted, one should know that the thesis of its differentiated aspects has likewise been refuted.

Furthermore, I now ask you; answer according to your own understanding. Does the recollection of past abodes apprehend the aggregates or apprehend the self? If it apprehends the aggregates, then to maintain that the self and the world are permanent is unreasonable. If it apprehends the self, then to recall the various sentient beings of the past with such-and-such a name, thinking "I was of such-and-such a name, such-and-such a clan," and so forth, is unreasonable.

Furthermore, what do you think? When visual consciousness arises depending on the presently convergent form-object, do the remaining consciousnesses with regard to other objects that are not present and not convergent cease or continue? If they cease, then to maintain that a consciousness that has ceased and been destroyed is permanent is unreasonable. If they continue, then for all consciousnesses to arise at all times with regard to a single object is unreasonable.

Furthermore, what is your preference? Does the self you maintain undergo change through what perception produces and what feeling produces, or does it not undergo change? If it does undergo change, then to maintain that the world and the self are permanent is unreasonable. If it does not undergo change, then to have one kind of perception and then give rise to various kinds of perception, and further to have small perceptions and immeasurable perceptions, is unreasonable. Furthermore, to first have exclusively pleasure and then exclusively suffering, and then to have suffering, pleasure, and neither-suffering-nor-pleasure, is unreasonable.

Furthermore, if one maintains that the life-force is identical with the body, one sees the self as form. If one maintains that the life-force is different from the body, one sees the self as non-form. If one maintains that the self pervades both without duality or deficiency, one sees the self as both form and non-form. If, in order to counter this, one grasps at the same meaning through different phrases and different words, one sees the self as neither form nor non-form. Furthermore, if one sees a small amount of form and a small amount of non-form, one sees the self as finite. If one sees these as immeasurable, one sees the self as infinite. If one pervades both, seeing the form portion as small and the non-form as immeasurable, or the form as immeasurable and the non-form portion as small, one sees the self as both finite and infinite. If, in order to counter this, one grasps merely through different words but not different meanings, one sees the self as neither finite nor infinite, or one sees the liberated self as free from both.

---

## Fascicle 10

### Chapter on Gathering the Meaning of Purity, Part Six

*[Continuing the refutation of the sixteen heterodox treatises]*

#### 5. The thesis of permanence (continued): the permanence of atoms

Furthermore, regarding those who maintain that atoms (paramāṇu) are permanent, I now ask you; answer according to your own understanding. Did you assert that atoms are permanent after having investigated, or without having investigated? If without having investigated, then to assert permanence without the investigation of wisdom is unreasonable. If you say you have investigated, then because it contradicts the valid means of knowledge (pramāṇa), it is unreasonable.

Furthermore, what is your preference? Do you maintain that atoms are permanent because they are subtle, or because their characteristics are different from coarse resultant things? If because they are subtle, then being dispersed, diminished, and weak in nature, yet to call them permanent is unreasonable. If because their characteristics are different, then atoms would transcend the characteristics of earth, water, fire, and wind, and since they would not be of the same class of characteristics, to say they can produce those effects is unreasonable. Furthermore, no different characteristic of atoms can be found, so this is also unreasonable.

Furthermore, what is your preference? Are the coarse things that arise from atoms of the same or different characteristics? If of the same characteristics, then since they are without difference from their cause, they too should be permanent, and there would then be no determination of cause and effect. If of different characteristics, what do you think? Do coarse things arise from atoms that are dispersed, or from atoms that are aggregated? If from dispersed atoms, then at all times all effects should arise, and there would be no determination of cause and effect. If from aggregated atoms, what do you think? When coarse resultant things arise from atoms, do they not exceed the size and quality of the atoms, or do they exceed it? If they do not exceed the size and quality, then for a thing possessing form and quality to arise from things that are portions of form and quality is unreasonable. If they exceed it, then since the substance of atoms has no subtle parts and cannot be analyzed further, the coarse things produced from them should also be permanent. This is unreasonable. If furthermore one says that atoms, which originally did not exist, now arise, then to maintain that atoms are permanent is unreasonable.

Furthermore, what is your preference? Do atoms fashion coarse things like seeds and so forth, or like potters and so forth? If like seeds, then just as seeds are impermanent in substance, atoms should likewise be impermanent. If like potters, then atoms should have deliberate thought, like potters. If you say they are not like seeds or potters, then since no similar analogy can be found, this is unreasonable.

Furthermore, what do you think? Does the arising of external things occur through sentient beings or not? If through sentient beings, then for external coarse things to arise through sentient beings while the subtle things that serve as their basis do not arise through sentient beings is unreasonable; who could control their capacity? If not through sentient beings, then for external things to arise without any purpose is unreasonable.

Thus, because of recollecting the aggregates and sentient beings, because of the unbroken flow of all consciousnesses with regard to a single object, and because of the change and non-change of perception and feeling, the theses of permanence asserting the past and asserting the future are unreasonable.

Furthermore, because of investigating and not investigating, because of shared characteristics, because of own characteristics, because of fashioning, and because of the fundamental utility, the thesis of the permanence of atoms is unreasonable. Therefore the thesis of permanence is not a reasoned statement.

Furthermore, I shall now state the characteristic of permanence. If at all times there is no characteristic of change, if in all respects there is no characteristic of change, if naturally there is no characteristic of change, if through another there is also no characteristic of change, and furthermore if there is no characteristic of arising, one should know this is the characteristic of permanence.

#### 6. The thesis that all is caused by prior action

This means that someone, whether a śramaṇa or a brāhmaṇa, gives rise to the following view and establishes the following thesis, as extensively explained in the sūtras: "Whatever a person of the world experiences, namely the suffering presently experienced, is all caused by prior action," meaning caused by past evil deeds. "Through diligent effort one expels old karma," meaning through extremely tormenting oneself in the present life. "Because present new karma is destroyed by the cause of non-action," meaning unwholesome karma. "Thus afterward there are no further outflows (āsrava)," meaning that one becomes exclusively of a wholesome nature, and thus afterward is without outflows. "Through the exhaustion of outflows, karma is exhausted," meaning evil karma. "Through the exhaustion of karma, suffering is exhausted," meaning the suffering caused by past conditions and the suffering invited by present practices. "Through the exhaustion of suffering, one realizes the limit of suffering," meaning one realizes the exhaustion of the suffering of continued existence in future lives. This is the position of the Nirgrantha (Jain) non-Buddhists.

**Question**: For what reason do those non-Buddhists give rise to this view and establish this thesis?

**Answer**: Because of teaching and reasoning. Teaching is as previously stated. *Reasoning* means that someone, being of a nature to investigate and observe, and so forth as previously stated, seeing that the functional efficacy of actions in the present life is indeterminate, reasons as follows: they see in the world that although one employs correct means, one incurs suffering, and although one employs wrong means, one obtains happiness. They think: if the functional efficacy of actions in the present life were the cause, the results should be the opposite. Since what they observe is not inverted, everything must have prior action as its cause. Through this reasoning, they give rise to this view and establish this thesis.

One should now ask them: what is your preference? Is the suffering incurred through the practices of the present life caused by prior action, or caused by present practices? If caused by prior action, then your previous statement that "through diligent effort one expels old karma" and "because present new karma is destroyed by the cause of non-action, thus afterward there are no further outflows," and so forth, is unreasonable. If caused by present practices, then your previous statement that "whatever a person of the world experiences is all caused by prior action" is unreasonable.

Thus, because present-life suffering being caused by prior action is unreasonable, and because present functional efficacy being the cause is also unreasonable, this thesis is not a reasoned statement.

Furthermore, I shall now state the characteristic of the true cause. Some sufferings are caused solely by prior action. For example, someone, through the dominant force of their own karma, is born in an evil destiny, or born in a poor and lowly family, or suffering arises from mixed causes. For example, someone serves a wicked king and, instead of obtaining a pleasant result, incurs suffering. As with serving a king, so too through various activities such as speech, commerce, agriculture, robbery, or harming other sentient beings: those who have merit obtain wealth and happiness, while those without merit, though they exert effort, achieve no result. Or again, there are dharmas produced purely through present functional effort as their cause, such as newly created actions that lead to further existence, or hearing the correct Dharma and awakening to the Dharma, or initiating the path of deportment, or studying and practicing technical arts. Such things are caused solely by the present skillful functional effort.

#### 7. The thesis that Īśvara and others are the creators

This means that someone, whether a śramaṇa or a brāhmaṇa, gives rise to the following view and establishes the following thesis: whatever a person of the world experiences, all of it is caused either by the transformation of Īśvara (the Great Lord), or by the transformation of some other supreme being, and so forth. This is the position of those who propound the thesis of unequal causes such as Īśvara.

**Question**: For what reason do those non-Buddhists give rise to this view and establish this thesis?
